Maturidi and Ash'ari: Agreement and Difference on Ru'ya
Maturidi and Ash'ari agree on ru'ya being possible without modality, differ on reason's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the points of agreement and disagreement between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools regarding the vision of Allah (ru'ya), particularly the role of reason.

Core Idea
Both schools affirm ru'ya without modality, but differ on whether reason can prove its possibility.

In this passage, we explore a subtle but important theological difference between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools. While both agree that believers will see Allah in the afterlife without knowing how, they part ways on whether human reason can demonstrate that such vision is even possible. Let's examine their common ground and their point of divergence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the agreement
Find what both schools accept: ru'ya without modality (bila kayfiyya), and the obligation of ru'ya by transmitted evidence (sam'an).
Note that the passage says there is no disagreement on the transmitted evidence.
3
Identify the disagreement
Find the exact point of difference: whether reason (al-'aql) can prove the permissibility of ru'ya. Maturidi says no, Ash'ari says yes.
This is the central point of contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماتريدي والأشعري أن الرؤية بلا كيفية، ولكنهم يختلفون بعد ذلك فيما بينهم على أن ذلك هل يعلم بدليل من العقل أو أن ذلك مرده إلى النقل وحده. أما النقل فلا خلاف بينهم. وأهل السنة جميعاً من ماتريدية وأشعرية لا يختلفون فيما بينهم على تفسير هذه الآيات للتدليل على وجوب الرؤية سمعاً. لكنهم مختلفون حول جوازها عقلاً. أما الماتريدي فقد ذهب إلى أن رؤية الله واجبة سمعاً من غير تفسير أي أننا نؤمن بأن الرؤية واجبة بالكتاب والسنة ولكن العقل عاجز عن إقامة الدليل على إمكان رؤية الله. أما الأشعري فقد ذهب إلى أنه يمكن التدليل عقلاً على جواز رؤية الله.
Al-Maturidi and Al-Ash'ari [agree] that vision (ru'ya) is without modality, but they differ after that among themselves as to whether that is known by a rational proof or whether that is referred to transmitted evidence alone. As for transmitted evidence, there is no disagreement among them. And all Ahl al-Sunna, Maturidis and Ash'aris, do not differ among themselves regarding the interpretation of these verses to prove the obligation of vision (ru'ya) by transmission (sam'ān). However, they differ regarding its intellectual permissibility. As for Al-Maturidi, he held that vision of Allah is obligatory by transmission without interpretation (tafsīr), meaning that we believe that vision is obligatory by the Book and Sunna, but reason is incapable of establishing a proof for the possibility of vision of Allah. As for Al-Ash'ari, he held that it is possible to demonstrate rationally the permissibility of vision of Allah.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage that delineates the points of agreement and disagreement between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools on the issue of ru'ya (seeing Allah). It begins by stating their common ground: both affirm that ru'ya occurs 'without modality' (bila kayfiyya), meaning they accept its reality without claiming to know how. Then it identifies their central point of contention: whether this truth is known by rational proof (dalil al-'aql) or solely by transmitted evidence (al-naql). The author notes that regarding the transmitted evidence (i.e., the Quran and Sunna), there is no dispute—both schools accept the obligation of ru'ya based on scripture. The passage then clarifies that all Ahl al-Sunna, whether Maturidi or Ash'ari, agree on the interpretation of the relevant Quranic verses to prove the obligation of ru'ya by sam' (transmitted testimony). Their difference lies in the question of intellectual permissibility (jawaz 'aqlan). The author then presents the specific positions: Al-Maturidi holds that ru'ya is wajib sam'an without tafsir (interpretation), meaning we affirm it by revelation but reason cannot demonstrate its possibility. Al-Ash'ari holds that reason can indeed demonstrate its permissibility. This sets the stage for the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
رؤية
بلا كيفية
جواز عقلاً
وجوب سمعاً
تفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidis and Ash'aris disagree on whether ru'ya will actually occur in the afterlife.
Correction: Both schools agree that ru'ya is obligatory (wajib) by transmitted evidence; their disagreement is only about whether reason can prove its permissibility, not about its occurrence.

Checkpoint
What is the point of disagreement between Maturidis and Ash'aris regarding ru'ya?
A) Whether ru'ya is possible at all
B) Whether ru'ya is wajib (obligatory) by transmitted evidence
C) Whether reason can prove the permissibility of ru'ya
D) Whether ru'ya is without modality

Key Takeaway
Maturidis and Ash'aris both affirm ru'ya without modality and accept its obligation by revelation, but Ash'aris hold that reason can prove its permissibility while Maturidis maintain reason cannot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If reason cannot fully grasp the possibility of seeing Allah, does that diminish your longing to see Him in the Hereafter? Are you content with the promise of revelation, or do you secretly wish for a rational guarantee?
?
In your daily life, do you sometimes demand logical explanations for matters of the unseen—like how Allah will be seen—before you can truly believe? How does the Maturidi position challenge that tendency, and what does that say about your trust in Allah's words?

Maturidi and Ash'ari on the Vision of Allah: Agreement and Disagreement
Introduces Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on ru'ya and their point of difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while all Ahl al-Sunnah agree the vision of Allah is obligatory by revelation, the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools differ on whether reason can prove its permissibility.

Core Idea
Revelation obliges the vision; reason is either silent or speaks.

The vision of Allah (ru'ya) is a key attribute discussed by Sunni theologians. Both Maturidis and Ash'aris affirm it based on revelation, but they part ways on whether the human intellect can independently establish its possibility. This lesson introduces their respective positions and sets the stage for the Ash'ari rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on agreement
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the statement that all Ahl al-Sunnah agree on the vision being wajib sam'an. Note the common ground.
Both schools are united on the revealed obligation.
2
Identify the point of difference
Spot where the text says 'different about its permissibility by reason.' Recognize that the disagreement is on rational proof (ʿaql), not on the revealed ruling.
Maturidi denies rational proof; Ash'ari affirms it.
3
Understand Maturidi's position
Al-Maturidi holds that reason cannot prove the possibility or impossibility; we simply accept it by revelation. This is a principled stance on the limits of reason.
This does not mean denying the vision, only that reason cannot confirm it.
4
Preview Ash'ari's proof
The passage introduces Ash'ari's famous proof from existence (dalīl al-wujūd). In the next lesson, we will explore it in detail.
This proof attempts to show rational permissibility using a method called sabr wa taqsīm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نبي مقدمة. الله ، وهل يليق بمقام النبوة أن يجهل موسى من أمر ربه ما عرفته المعتزلة . هذه من الحجج البالغة والأدلة الدامغة التي استخلصها أهل السنة من القرآن لإثبات الرؤية . في مثل هذه التفسيرات الرائعة للقرآن الكريم تكمن قوة الفهم والعقل عند أهل السنة . وأهل السنة جميعاً من ماتريدية وأشعرية لا يختلفون فيما بينهم على تفسير هذه الآيات للتدليل على وجوب الرؤية سمعاً . لكنهم مختلفون حول جوازها عقلاً. أما الماتريدي فقد ذهب إلى أن رؤية الله واجبة سمعاً من غير تفسير " أي أننا نؤمن بأن الرؤية واجبة بالكتاب والسنة ولكن العقل عاجز عن إقامة الدليل على إمكان رؤية الله . أما الأشعري فقد ذهب إلى أنه يمكن التدليل عقلاً على جواز رؤية الله ، وله على ذلك دليل مشهور يسميه المتكلمون بدليل الوجود يمكن أن نلخصه على الوجه الآتي : إن إمكان الرؤية في الشاهد إنما نشأ من الوجود لا غير ، والله تعالى يرى . أما أن المرئيات في الشاهد جائزة الرؤية لوجودها ليس موجود ، فيجوز أن يرى غير ؛ فالدليل عليه أننا نرى أشياء مختلفة الحقائق من جواهر وأعراض ، ولا يجوز أن يكون المصحح للرؤية ما يختلف فيه ؛ لأن ذلك يؤدي إلى أن يكون للحكم الواحد علتان مختلفتان ، وذلك مدفوع في بداية العقول . فلا بد إذن من إيجاد وصف مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة التي نراها نرجع إليه جواز هذه الرؤية حتى تطرد العلة وتنعكس . ولقد دل السبر والتقسيم على أنه لا مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة من الجواهر والأعراض إلا الوجود والحدوث، لكن الحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون علة لصحة الرؤية ؛ لأن الحدوث عبارة عن وجود مسبوق بعدم ، والعدم لا تأثير له في الحكم. وإذا لم يصح الحدوث لم يبق إلا الوجود ، والوجود مشترك فيه بين الشاهد والغائب ، فإذن وجود الله علة صالحة لصحة رؤيته ، وإذا حصلت العلة حصل الحكم لا محالة ، فوجب القول بصحة رؤيته . (۱) الصابوني : البداية ص ٧٤ ، دار المعارف بمصر ١٩٦٩ ، وانظر أيضاً الرازي : الأربعين ۲۰۱-۲۰۰ ، الماتريدي : التوحيد ص ٧٧-٨٥ .
Prophet introduction. Allah, and is it fitting for the station of prophethood that Moses should be ignorant of his Lord's command what the Mu'tazila knew? These are among the conclusive arguments and irrefutable proofs that Ahl al-Sunnah extracted from the Qur'an to prove the vision (ru'ya). In such magnificent interpretations of the Noble Qur'an lies the strength of understanding and intellect of Ahl al-Sunnah. And all Ahl al-Sunnah, both Maturidis and Ash'aris, do not differ among themselves regarding the interpretation of these verses to prove the obligation of the vision by revelation (sam'an). But they differ regarding its permissibility by reason (ʿaqlan). As for al-Maturidi, he held that seeing Allah is obligatory by revelation without rational interpretation, meaning we believe that the vision is obligatory by the Book and the Sunnah, but reason is incapable of providing proof for the possibility of seeing Allah. As for al-Ash'ari, he held that it is possible to give rational proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah, and he has a famous proof for that which the theologians call the proof from existence (dalīl al-wujūd), which we can summarize as follows: The possibility of vision in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) arises only from existence, and Allah is seen. But that visible things in the witnessed world are possible to be seen because of their existence, not because they are existent, so it is permissible that others be seen. The proof for it is that we see things with different realities, such as substances and accidents, and it is not permissible that what corrects vision be something that differs among them, because that would lead to there being two different causes for one judgment, and that is rejected by basic intellects. So it is necessary to find a common description among these different realities that we see, to which the permissibility of this vision is referred, so that the cause is consistent and reversible. And the method of exploration and division (al-sabr wa al-taqsīm) has shown that there is nothing common among these different realities of substances and accidents except existence and temporal origination (ḥudūth), but temporal origination cannot be the cause for the validity of vision, because temporal origination is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect on the judgment. And if temporal origination is invalidated, nothing remains but existence, and existence is common between the witnessed and the absent (al-ghāʾib). So the existence of Allah is a valid cause for the validity of seeing Him, and when the cause obtains, the judgment obtains inevitably, so it is necessary to affirm the validity of seeing Him. (1) al-Ṣābūnī: al-Bidāya p. 74, Dār al-Maʿārif bi-Miṣr 1969, and also see al-Rāzī: al-Arbaʿīn 201-200, al-Māturīdī: al-Tawḥīd pp. 77-85.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage presents the positions of the two main Sunni schools—Maturidi and Ash'ari—regarding the vision of Allah (ru'ya) and then details the Ash'ari rational proof. It begins by stating that both schools agree that the vision of Allah is obligatory based on revelation (samʿan), i.e., proven by Quran and hadith. Their disagreement is whether reason (ʿaql) can also prove its permissibility (jawāz). Al-Maturidi holds that reason is incapable: we accept it by revelation alone, but rational proof is beyond us. Al-Ash'ari, however, argues that rational proof is possible, and he provides his famous 'proof from existence' (dalīl al-wujūd). The proof starts from the observation that in the witnessed world (al-shāhid), the permissibility of seeing something arises solely from its existence, not from any other attribute. Since we see things of different kinds (substances and accidents), the cause must be something common to all of them. Through the method of exploration and division (al-sabr wa al-taqsīm), we find that the only common qualities are existence and temporal origination (ḥudūth). Temporal origination is invalid because it involves non-existence, which cannot ground a positive judgment. Therefore, existence remains as the sole cause. Since Allah exists, His existence is a valid cause for the permissibility of seeing Him. The conclusion is that it is rationally permissible to see Allah. The footnote cites sources for this argument.

Key Terms
رؤية
وجوب سمعاً
جواز عقلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidis deny the vision of Allah altogether.
Correction: Maturidis affirm the vision as wajib sam'an; they only deny that reason can prove its possibility, not the vision itself.

Checkpoint
On what point do Maturidis and Ash'aris agree regarding the vision of Allah?
A) That it is rationally possible
B) That it is obligatory by revelation
C) That reason cannot prove it
D) That it is impossible

Key Takeaway
All Sunni schools affirm the vision of Allah by revelation; the Maturidi-Ash'ari disagreement is only about whether reason can prove its possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief that your mind finds difficult to accept, do you submit to revelation even without rational proof, or do you demand a logical explanation? How does the Maturidi position challenge your intellectual habits?
?
Is your certainty about Allah's attributes based more on what you have studied from scripture or on your own reasoning? Are you willing to affirm something simply because Allah and His Messenger said it, even if your mind cannot grasp how?
?
The Ash'ari sought to use reason to support revelation. In your own life, do you use your intellect to strengthen your faith, or do you rely on reason as a substitute for revelation? How can you ensure your reasoning serves your faith rather than undermining it?

The Ash'ari Proof for the Rational Possibility of Seeing Allah
Presents the full Ash'ari rational proof (dalīl al-wujūd) for the permissibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the Ash'ari proof from existence (dalīl al-wujūd) and how it uses the method of exploration and division (sabr wa taqsīm) to establish that existence is the cause of visibility.

Core Idea
Existence alone makes something seeable; since Allah exists, He can be seen.

This lesson unpacks the famous Ash'ari proof for the rational permissibility of seeing Allah. The argument moves from observable realities in the witnessed world (shāhid) to the unseen (ghā'ib) using a rigorous method of identifying a common cause. By the end, you will see how the proof concludes that Allah's existence suffices to make His vision possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof in Arabic
Read the entire proof passage from 'إن إمكان الرؤية في الشاهد' to 'فوجب القول بصحة رؤيته'. Try to follow the logical flow without stopping.
The proof is compact; take your time.
2
Understand the starting observation
The proof begins by noting that in the witnessed world, visibility depends solely on existence. We see many different kinds of things (substances and accidents), so the cause cannot be something specific to one kind.
The variety of visible things shows the cause must be common.
3
Follow the method of sabr wa taqsīm
By exploring and dividing possible causes, the proof eliminates all but two: existence and temporal origination (ḥudūth). Then it argues that ḥudūth involves non-existence and cannot ground a positive judgment like visibility.
This leads to the conclusion that existence is the sole cause.
4
Apply to Allah
Since existence is common between the witnessed (shāhid) and the unseen (ghā'ib), and Allah exists, His existence is a valid cause for the possibility of seeing Him. Therefore, it is rationally permissible.
The proof does not prove that we will see Allah, only that it is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نبي مقدمة. الله ، وهل يليق بمقام النبوة أن يجهل موسى من أمر ربه ما عرفته المعتزلة . هذه من الحجج البالغة والأدلة الدامغة التي استخلصها أهل السنة من القرآن لإثبات الرؤية . في مثل هذه التفسيرات الرائعة للقرآن الكريم تكمن قوة الفهم والعقل عند أهل السنة . وأهل السنة جميعاً من ماتريدية وأشعرية لا يختلفون فيما بينهم على تفسير هذه الآيات للتدليل على وجوب الرؤية سمعاً . لكنهم مختلفون حول جوازها عقلاً. أما الماتريدي فقد ذهب إلى أن رؤية الله واجبة سمعاً من غير تفسير " أي أننا نؤمن بأن الرؤية واجبة بالكتاب والسنة ولكن العقل عاجز عن إقامة الدليل على إمكان رؤية الله . أما الأشعري فقد ذهب إلى أنه يمكن التدليل عقلاً على جواز رؤية الله ، وله على ذلك دليل مشهور يسميه المتكلمون بدليل الوجود يمكن أن نلخصه على الوجه الآتي : إن إمكان الرؤية في الشاهد إنما نشأ من الوجود لا غير ، والله تعالى يرى . أما أن المرئيات في الشاهد جائزة الرؤية لوجودها ليس موجود ، فيجوز أن يرى غير ؛ فالدليل عليه أننا نرى أشياء مختلفة الحقائق من جواهر وأعراض ، ولا يجوز أن يكون المصحح للرؤية ما يختلف فيه ؛ لأن ذلك يؤدي إلى أن يكون للحكم الواحد علتان مختلفتان ، وذلك مدفوع في بداية العقول . فلا بد إذن من إيجاد وصف مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة التي نراها نرجع إليه جواز هذه الرؤية حتى تطرد العلة وتنعكس . ولقد دل السبر والتقسيم على أنه لا مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة من الجواهر والأعراض إلا الوجود والحدوث، لكن الحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون علة لصحة الرؤية ؛ لأن الحدوث عبارة عن وجود مسبوق بعدم ، والعدم لا تأثير له في الحكم. وإذا لم يصح الحدوث لم يبق إلا الوجود ، والوجود مشترك فيه بين الشاهد والغائب ، فإذن وجود الله علة صالحة لصحة رؤيته ، وإذا حصلت العلة حصل الحكم لا محالة ، فوجب القول بصحة رؤيته . (۱) الصابوني : البداية ص ٧٤ ، دار المعارف بمصر ١٩٦٩ ، وانظر أيضاً الرازي : الأربعين ۲۰۱-۲۰۰ ، الماتريدي : التوحيد ص ٧٧-٨٥ .
Prophet introduction. Allah, and is it fitting for the station of prophethood that Moses should be ignorant of his Lord's command what the Mu'tazila knew? These are among the conclusive arguments and irrefutable proofs that Ahl al-Sunnah extracted from the Qur'an to prove the vision (ru'ya). In such magnificent interpretations of the Noble Qur'an lies the strength of understanding and intellect of Ahl al-Sunnah. And all Ahl al-Sunnah, both Maturidis and Ash'aris, do not differ among themselves regarding the interpretation of these verses to prove the obligation of the vision by revelation (sam'an). But they differ regarding its permissibility by reason (ʿaqlan). As for al-Maturidi, he held that seeing Allah is obligatory by revelation without rational interpretation, meaning we believe that the vision is obligatory by the Book and the Sunnah, but reason is incapable of providing proof for the possibility of seeing Allah. As for al-Ash'ari, he held that it is possible to give rational proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah, and he has a famous proof for that which the theologians call the proof from existence (dalīl al-wujūd), which we can summarize as follows: The possibility of vision in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) arises only from existence, and Allah is seen. But that visible things in the witnessed world are possible to be seen because of their existence, not because they are existent, so it is permissible that others be seen. The proof for it is that we see things with different realities, such as substances and accidents, and it is not permissible that what corrects vision be something that differs among them, because that would lead to there being two different causes for one judgment, and that is rejected by basic intellects. So it is necessary to find a common description among these different realities that we see, to which the permissibility of this vision is referred, so that the cause is consistent and reversible. And the method of exploration and division (al-sabr wa al-taqsīm) has shown that there is nothing common among these different realities of substances and accidents except existence and temporal origination (ḥudūth), but temporal origination cannot be the cause for the validity of vision, because temporal origination is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect on the judgment. And if temporal origination is invalidated, nothing remains but existence, and existence is common between the witnessed and the absent (al-ghāʾib). So the existence of Allah is a valid cause for the validity of seeing Him, and when the cause obtains, the judgment obtains inevitably, so it is necessary to affirm the validity of seeing Him. (1) al-Ṣābūnī: al-Bidāya p. 74, Dār al-Maʿārif bi-Miṣr 1969, and also see al-Rāzī: al-Arbaʿīn 201-200, al-Māturīdī: al-Tawḥīd pp. 77-85.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage presents the positions of the two main Sunni schools—Maturidi and Ash'ari—regarding the vision of Allah (ru'ya) and then details the Ash'ari rational proof. It begins by stating that both schools agree that the vision of Allah is obligatory based on revelation (samʿan), i.e., proven by Quran and hadith. Their disagreement is whether reason (ʿaql) can also prove its permissibility (jawāz). Al-Maturidi holds that reason is incapable: we accept it by revelation alone, but rational proof is beyond us. Al-Ash'ari, however, argues that rational proof is possible, and he provides his famous 'proof from existence' (dalīl al-wujūd). The proof starts from the observation that in the witnessed world (al-shāhid), the permissibility of seeing something arises solely from its existence, not from any other attribute. Since we see things of different kinds (substances and accidents), the cause must be something common to all of them. Through the method of exploration and division (al-sabr wa al-taqsīm), we find that the only common qualities are existence and temporal origination (ḥudūth). Temporal origination is invalid because it involves non-existence, which cannot ground a positive judgment. Therefore, existence remains as the sole cause. Since Allah exists, His existence is a valid cause for the permissibility of seeing Him. The conclusion is that it is rationally permissible to see Allah. The footnote cites sources for this argument.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari proof, what is the common cause that makes vision possible in the witnessed world?
A) Existence and temporal origination
B) Existence alone
C) Temporal origination alone
D) Specific essence

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof demonstrates that existence is the sole rational cause for visibility, and since Allah exists, His vision is rationally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see Allah, or are you content with the temporary distractions of this world? What does your daily preoccupation reveal about your answer?
?
If the greatest vision of Allah is rationally possible, what are you doing to prepare your heart for that moment? Are you seeking knowledge that brings you closer to that reality, or are you satisfied with empty arguments?
?
When you look at the most beautiful things in creation, do you recognize that their visibility is due only to existence? How does that knowledge redirect your longing from the creation to the Creator?

Maturidi's Proof for God's Unity from the World's Finitude
Maturidi links God's oneness to the finitude of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that the finitude of the world necessitates the oneness of God.

Core Idea
If the world were infinite, multiple gods could exist; but it is finite, so there is only one God.

This lesson unpacks a concise but powerful argument from Imam Maturidi. He links the observable fact that the world is finite to the rational necessity of God's unity. The argument works by showing that multiple gods would require an infinite world, contradicting the world's finitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Maturidi structures his argument as a conditional: 'If... then...'.
The passage is brief but dense. Read it twice.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument has three parts: (1) assumption of multiple gods, (2) consequence of infinite creation, (3) conclusion that world must be infinite, but it is finite, so assumption false.
This is a classical reductio ad absurdum.
3
Consider the premise of world's finitude
Maturidi takes the finitude of the world as a given, based on the cosmology of his time. Notice that the argument's strength depends on this premise.
Later Islamic philosophers debated this premise, but Maturidi accepted it.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that God must be one. Multiple gods would lead to an infinite regress of creators and creations, which is impossible given the finite world.
This proof is sometimes called 'the argument from the impossibility of an infinite series of causes'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو لم يكن الله واحداً لم يكن العالم متناهياً، إذ لوجاز أن يكون هناك أكثر من إله واحد لأمكن حينئذ أن نفترض ما لا نهاية له من الآلهة التي لو كان من كل منهم شيء واحد لخرجت جملة العالم عن التناهي بخروج المحدثين له.
If God were not one, the world would not be finite, for if it were possible that there be more than one god, then it would be possible to assume infinite gods, each of whom if they produced a single thing, the entirety of the world would exceed finitude due to the multiplicity of the originated things.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's argument for the unity of God (tawḥīd) based on the finitude of the world. It is a conditional proof: if there were multiple gods, the world would not be finite. The reasoning is that if multiple gods were possible, we could suppose an infinite number of them, each creating at least one thing. This would lead to an infinite number of created things, making the world infinite. Since the world is observed to be finite (a premise Maturidi accepts from Aristotelian physics), the supposition of multiple gods is false, so there is only one God. The argument ties divine unity to a feature of the cosmos.

Key Terms
وحدانية
تناهي العالم
آلهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi assumes the world's finitude without any justification.
Correction: In this passage, Maturidi does not argue for the world's finitude; he takes it as a premise from the scientific consensus of his time. Later theologians debated this premise, but the proof itself is logically valid given that premise.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi link the finitude of the world to God's unity?
A) A finite world cannot have many creators
B) Multiple gods would each create an infinite number of things
C) If there were many gods, the world would be infinite, but it is finite
D) The world's finitude proves there is only one God because a second god would make it infinite

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's conditional proof demonstrates that God's unity is rationally necessary because the world is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's finitude is a sign of God's oneness, what does your own finite life—its beginning and end—tell you about who alone deserves your ultimate loyalty?
?
In what areas of your daily decisions do you act as if there are multiple 'gods'—competing masters like status, comfort, or approval—and what step will you take this week to dethrone them?
?
This proof shows that multiple gods would lead to endless creation. Where in your heart have you allowed an endless chase after created things, hoping they will satisfy what only the One God can fill?

Divine Attributes

5 min
Essential and Active Divine Attributes
Maturidi lists divine attributes and defines essential attributes as eternal meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of divine attributes into essential and active, and the doctrine that essential attributes are eternal meanings neither identical to nor other than God's essence.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are real and eternal, neither separate from God nor identical to His essence.

Maturidi introduces a foundational teaching on divine attributes. He affirms that God is described with both essential and active attributes in pre-eternity, and then clarifies the nature of essential attributes: they are eternal meanings subsisting through God's essence. Understanding this formula is key to avoiding extremes in describing God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the list of attributes and the key phrase at the end.
The passage uses the phrase 'la hiya dhatuhu wa la ghayruha' which is crucial.
2
Identify the two groups of attributes
Find the essential attributes (knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech, power, will) and note that active attributes are also mentioned. Observe Maturidi's claim that there is no distinction between them in description.
3
Analyze the formula 'neither identical nor other'
Reflect on why Maturidi uses this paradoxical-sounding formula. It avoids equating attributes with God (which would imply multiplicity) and separating them as independent entities (which would imply polytheism).
This formula is a hallmark of Ash'ari-Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله تعالى موصوف في الأزل بكل ما وصف به ذاته من العلم والحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام والقدرة والإرادة والتكوين والرحمة والرزق وغيرها من الذاتية والفعلية، لا فرق في ذلك بين صفة ذاتية وصفة فعلية. أما الطائفة الأولى من الصفات ونعني بها الصفات الذاتية وهي العلم والحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام والقدرة والإرادة فلا خلاف عليها بين أهل السنة جميعاً من حيث أنها معان قديمة قائمة بذات الله، لا هي ذاته ولا غير ذاته.
And God Most High is described in pre-eternity with all that He described His Essence with: knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech, power, will, origination, mercy, provision, and other essential and active attributes. There is no distinction in that between an essential attribute and an active attribute. As for the first group of attributes, by which we mean the essential attributes—they are knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech, power, and will—there is no disagreement upon them among all the Ahl al-Sunna insofar as they are eternal meanings subsisting through God’s essence, neither identical to His essence nor other than it.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement by Maturidi on the divine attributes. He affirms that God is described in pre-eternity (al-azal) with all attributes mentioned in revelation, including both essential attributes (dhātiyya) and active attributes (fiʿliyya). He lists essential attributes as knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech, power, and will; active attributes include origination, mercy, provision, and others. He states there is no distinction in ruling between essential and active attributes; both are real. Then he specifically discusses the essential attributes: there is consensus among Ahl al-Sunna that they are eternal meanings (maʿānī qadīma) subsisting through God’s essence. Crucially, they are neither identical to the essence (lā hiya dhātuhu) nor other than it (wa lā ghayruhā). This formula avoids both identifying the attributes with God (which would imply multiplicity in the essence) and separating them as independent entities (which would imply polytheism). This is a classic Ashʿari-Māturīdi position on the attributes.

Key Terms
صفات ذاتية
صفات فعلية
معان قديمة
لا هي ذاته ولا غير ذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'neither identical nor other' is a logical contradiction or that Maturidi denies the reality of attributes.
Correction: Maturidi maintains that attributes are real and eternal, but in conceptual analysis they are distinct from the essence while in external existence they are not separate. The phrase is a technical formula to avoid both identity and separation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, the essential attributes are:
A) Created and temporal.
B) Eternal meanings subsisting through God's essence.
C) Identical to God's essence in every respect.
D) Other than God's essence as separate entities.

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are real, eternal, and subsist in God's essence without being identical to it or separate from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that God's attributes are neither identical to His essence nor separate from it. How does this truth affect your understanding of who God is? Are you tempted to think of God's attributes as mere names without reality, or as separate entities? Repent of any anthropomorphism or stripping of attributes.
?
If God's attributes like knowledge and power are eternal and real, how should this shape your reliance on His knowledge in your daily decisions? Do you truly act as if God's knowledge is infinite and always with you? What decision this week requires you to trust that?
?
The essential attributes are eternal and subsisting in God. Does your heart feel awe that God's hearing and sight are ever-present? How does that change how you live when no one else sees you? Commit to a specific action to live in the awareness of His sight.

Divine Attributes

6 min
Attributes of Essence: Neither Identical nor Other
Maturidi defines essential attributes and resolves the 'neither identical nor other' paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of essential attributes and the resolution of the apparent paradox that they are neither identical to God's essence nor other than it.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are conceptually distinct but existentially identical to the divine essence.

Maturidi begins by listing the essential attributes and affirming that they are eternal meanings subsisting in God. He then introduces a crucial formulation that has puzzled some: attributes are neither identical to the essence nor other than it. This lesson unpacks that formulation and shows how a distinction between mental and external existence resolves the seeming contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of attributes and the key phrase.
Focus on the wording 'لا هي ذاته ولا هي غير ذاته'.
2
Identify the crucial formulation
The phrase 'neither identical nor other' is the standard Ash'ari-Maturidi position to avoid two extremes. Consider why both extremes are problematic.
This formulation safeguards tawhid while affirming real attributes.
3
Understand the resolution
Sheikh Ali al-Qari uses the distinction between mental existence (الوجود الذهني) and external existence (الوجود الخارجي). In the mind, attributes are distinct; in reality, they are one with the essence.
This removes the apparent contradiction without denying reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله تعالى موصوف في الأزل بكل ما وصف به ذاته من العلم والحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام والقدرة والإرادة والتكوين والرحمة والرزق وغيرها من الذاتية والفعلية، لا فرق في ذلك بين صفة ذاتية وصفة فعلية أما الطائفة الأولى من الصفات ونعني بها الصفات الذاتية وهي العلم والحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام والقدرة والإرادة فلا خلاف عليها بين أهل السنة جميعاً من حيث أنها معان قديمة قائمة بذات الله، لا هي ذاته ولا هي غير ذاته. وقد ذهب الدكتور محمود قاسم إلى أن عبارة لا هي ذاته ولا هي غير ذاته، هو بحسب المفهوم تحتوي على تناقض لا سبيل إلى رفعه. لكن الشيخ علي القاري يرفع هذا التناقض إذ يميز بين الوجود في الذهن والوجود في الخارج فيقول: إنها لا الذهني، ولا غيره بحسب الوجود الخارجي، فإن مفهوم الصفات غير مفهوم الذات، إلا أنها لا تتغاير باعتبار ظهورها في الكائنات.
And God Most High is described in pre-eternity with all that He described His essence with: knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech, power, will, bringing into existence, mercy, provision, and others, both essential and active; there is no distinction in that between an essential attribute and an active attribute. As for the first category of attributes, by which we mean the essential attributes—they are knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech, power, and will—there is no disagreement upon them among all Ahl al-Sunnah, in that they are eternal meanings subsisting through God's essence, neither identical to His essence nor other than it. Dr. Mahmoud Qasim thought that the expression 'neither identical to His essence nor other than it' contains a contradiction that cannot be resolved. But Sheikh Ali al-Qari resolves this contradiction by distinguishing between mental existence and external existence, saying: They are not identical in the mind, nor are they other in terms of external existence; for the concept of the attributes is other than the concept of the essence, yet they do not differ with respect to their manifestation in creatures.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage defines the attributes of essence (الصفات الذاتية) as knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech, power, and will. They are described as eternal meanings that subsist in God's essence. The crucial theological formulation is that they are neither identical to the essence nor other than it (لا هي ذاته ولا هي غير ذاته). This is a standard Ash'ari-Maturidi position to avoid both anthropomorphism and denial of attributes. A modern scholar, Dr. Mahmoud Qasim, considered this phrase contradictory, but Sheikh Ali al-Qari resolves it by applying the distinction between conceptual existence (الوجود الذهني) and external existence (الوجود الخارجي). In the mind, the concept of an attribute is distinct from the concept of the essence; but in external reality, the attribute is not a separate entity from the essence—they are one in existence. Thus the apparent contradiction is resolved.

Key Terms
صفات ذاتية
معان قديمة
لا هي ذاته ولا هي غير ذاته
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'neither identical to the essence nor other than it' is logically contradictory.
Correction: The apparent contradiction is resolved by distinguishing mental existence from external existence: conceptually distinct but existentially identical.

Checkpoint
How does Sheikh Ali al-Qari resolve the seeming contradiction in 'not identical nor other'?
A) By denying the attributes
B) By distinguishing between mental and external existence
C) By claiming the attributes are created
D) By saying the phrase is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are eternal meanings that are conceptually distinct from God's essence but existentially one with it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined God's attributes as separate 'parts' of Him? How does this lesson challenge that mental image, and what will you do to correct your understanding?
?
The phrase 'neither identical nor other' seems to contradict our logic. Are you willing to accept that some truths about Allah transcend human reasoning? Recall one time you demanded a logical explanation for a divine matter and commit to surrendering your intellect to revelation.
?
Consider a belief you hold about Allah that you think you fully understand. Could it be that your mental model is mistaken? Write down one specific aspect of your 'Aqeedah that you will re-examine in light of this distinction between mental concepts and external reality.

Attributes of Action: Narrowing the Gap Between Maturidi and Ash'ari
Maturidi discusses attributes of action and shows alignment with Baqillani and Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi's view on eternal descriptive attributes of action aligns with later Ash'ari scholars like Baqillani and al-Ghazali, narrowing the supposed gap.

Core Idea
The gap between Maturidis and Ash'aris on action attributes is narrower than imagined.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid addresses a common misconception: that Maturidis and Ash'aris are far apart on whether God's attributes of action are eternal. By comparing Maturidi's own words with those of Baqillani and al-Ghazali, the author shows that both schools affirm the eternal nature of God's descriptive attributes while recognizing temporal application.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow.
Notice that the passage begins by stating a misconception.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage claims that the gap between Maturidis and Ash'aris is not wide. Find the sentence that expresses this.
It is near the beginning: 'But this imagination dissipates...'
3
Compare Maturidi's principle with Baqillani
Read Maturidi's quote and then Baqillani's. Notice how both affirm that the description of God with action attributes is eternal, even if the act itself occurs in time.
Baqillani explicitly echoes Maturidi's meaning.
4
Examine Ghazali's contribution
Ghazali uses Aristotelian terms but arrives at the same conclusion. See how his quote about the Creator's name being true eternally aligns with Maturidi.
The sword metaphor illustrates potential vs. actual.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this narrowing of the gap matters for Ahl al-Sunnah unity. The passage shows that later Ash'aris are closer to Maturidi than earlier polemics suggest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الطائفة الثانية في الصفات ونعني بها صفات الفعل كالتكوين والرحمة والرزق وغيرها فقد توهم بعض المحققين من علماء الكلام من الأشعرية والماتريدية أن شقة الخلاف بين مدرستي أهل السنة في هذه المسألة واسعة لأن الماتريدية تعتبر الصفات الفعلية صفات حقيقية قديمة قائمة بذات الله بينما ترى الأشعرية أنها مجرد صفات نسبية حادثة ولكن هذا الوهم يتبدد، كما تضيق شقة الخلاف بين مدرستي أهل السنة في هذه المسألة حين نقارن بين آراء الماتريدي وآراء الأشاعرة الذين جاءوا بعده مثل الباقلاني والغزالي. يقول الماتريدي في كتاب التوحيد: والأصل أن الله تعالى إذا أطلق الوصف له، وُصف بما يوصف من الفعل والعلم ونحوه، يلوم الوصف به في الأزل. وإذا معه الذي هو تحت وصفه به من المعلوم والمقدور عليه والمراد والمكون يتذكر فيه أوقات تلك الأشياء لئلا يتوهم قدم تلك الأشياء. وهذا المعنى هو الذي قصد إليه الباقلاني حيث يقول: أما صفات الفعل فهي كل صفة كان قبل فعله لها، وإن كان وصفه لنفسه بذلك قديم. أما الغزالي فإنه وإن كان يستخدم معنيي القوة والفعل الأرسطيين لحل هذا الإشكال إلا أنه لا يخرج مع ذلك عما قصد إليه الماتريدي من معنى. يقول الغزالي: وأما ما يشتق له من الأفعال كالرازق والخالق فقد اختلف في أنه يصدق في الأزل أم لا ... فقال قوم: هو صادق أزلا إذ لو لم يصدق لكان اتصافه به موجباً للتغير. وقال قوم: لا يصدق إذ لا خلق في الأزل، فكيف خالقاً: والكاشف. هذا أن السيف في الغمد عن للغطاء یسیمی صارما، وعند حصول القطع به، وفي
As for the second category of attributes, by which we mean the attributes of action, such as bringing into existence, mercy, provision, and others—some investigators from the theologians of the Ash'aris and Maturidis have imagined that the gap of disagreement between the two schools of Ahl al-Sunnah in this matter is wide, because the Maturidis consider the active attributes to be real, eternal attributes subsisting through God's essence, while the Ash'aris see them as merely relative, temporal attributes. But this imagination dissipates, just as the gap of disagreement between the two schools of Ahl al-Sunnah in this matter narrows when we compare the views of al-Maturidi with the views of the Ash'aris who came after him, such as al-Baqillani and al-Ghazali. Al-Maturidi says in Kitab al-Tawhid: 'The principle is that when a description is absolutely applied to God, He is described by what He describes from action and knowledge and the like; the description is incumbent in pre-eternity. And when with Him that which is under His description from the known, the empowered, the willed, and the brought-into-existence, He remembers therein the times of those things to avoid imagining their pre-eternity.' This is the meaning that al-Baqillani intended when he says: 'As for the attributes of action, they are every attribute for which there was [a state] before His act, although His description of Himself by that is eternal.' As for al-Ghazali, although he uses the Aristotelian concepts of potentiality and actuality to resolve this problem, he does not depart from the meaning intended by al-Maturidi. Al-Ghazali says: 'As for what is derived for Him from actions, such as the Provider and the Creator—it is disputed whether it is true in pre-eternity or not. Some said: It is true eternally, for if it were not true, His being described with it would necessitate change. Others said: It is not true, because there is no creation in pre-eternity, so how could He be a creator? The unveiling is that the sword in the sheath is called a sword, and when cutting obtains with it, ...'
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage addresses attributes of action (صفات الفعل) and the supposed conflict between Maturidis and Ash'aris. The author argues that the gap is not as wide as some imagine. He quotes Maturidi from his Kitab al-Tawhid to show that in the pre-eternal state, God is described with action attributes, but there is a distinction between the description being eternal and the actual occurrence of the act in time. Al-Baqillani similarly holds that though the attribute is predicated of God before He performs the act, His description with it is eternal. Al-Ghazali uses the Aristotelian distinction between potency and act, but his conclusion aligns with Maturidi's. The passage then begins a quote from Ghazali discussing the dispute over whether names like 'Creator' apply eternally; one view says yes lest His description change, the other says no because there was no creation. The passage ends with a metaphor of the sword in the sheath.

Key Terms
صفات فعلية
التكوين
الرحمة
الرزق
قدم الصفات
باقلاني
غزالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidis and Ash'aris have a wide and irreconcilable disagreement over the nature of God's action attributes.
Correction: While Maturidis consider these attributes real and eternal and Ash'aris see them as relative, later Ash'aris like Baqillani and Ghazali affirm that the descriptive attribution is eternal, narrowing the gap significantly.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the passage on attributes of action?
A) To show a wide disagreement between Maturidis and Ash'aris
B) To prove that attributes of action are not eternal
C) To argue that the disagreement is actually narrow and harmonizable
D) To reject the Ash'ari view completely

Key Takeaway
The apparent disagreement on action attributes between Maturidis and Ash'aris is largely resolved when later Ash'ari scholars are considered alongside Maturidi's original formulation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you tend to focus on differences rather than common ground when dealing with others? Based on this lesson, what is one relationship you will work on this week by looking for underlying agreement before assuming disagreement?
?
This lesson shows that even theological disputes can be resolved by careful reading of original sources. When you hear a controversial claim about faith, do you verify it yourself or accept it at face value? What step will you take to become a more active seeker of truth?
?
If God's attribute 'Provider' is eternal even before any provision was created, how does that change your trust in His provision for your future? What specific worry will you commit to placing in His hands today?

Eternal Attributes, Temporal Objects
Maturidi states the principle of action attributes in pre-eternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that Allah's action attributes are eternal, while their objects are temporal.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are eternal, but what they act upon is not.

Imam Maturidi lays down a foundational principle for understanding divine attributes: when Allah describes Himself with an attribute of action (like creating), that description applies to Him in pre-eternity. However, we must carefully distinguish between the eternal attribute and the temporal objects it relates to. This lesson examines that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the condensed style of Maturidi.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has two main parts: first the principle that Allah's action attributes apply in pre-eternity, then the clarification that the objects of these attributes are not pre-eternal. Find the transition phrase 'وإذا معه' which signals the second part.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: why does Maturidi feel the need to specify that the objects are temporal? What misconception is he guarding against? The commentary will help you see this.
This connects to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أن الله تعالى إذا أطلق الوصف له، وُصف بما يوصف من الفعل والعلم ونحوه، يلزم الوصف به في الأزل. وإذا معه الذي هو تحت وصفه به من المعلوم والمقدور عليه والمراد والمكون يتذكر فيه أوقات تلك الأشياء لئلا يتوهم قدم تلك الأشياء
The principle is that when description is given to Allah, He is described with what He describes of action and knowledge and similar things, the description of Him with it is necessary in pre-eternity. And when with Him that which is under His description of the known, the capable-of-being, the intended, and the brought-into-existence, one recalls in it the times of those things lest one imagine the pre-eternity of those things.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage defines Maturidi's principle regarding Allah's attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fi‘l). He states that when Allah is described with attributes like creating, providing, etc., these descriptions apply to Him in pre-eternity (al-azal). However, one must distinguish between the attribute itself and its object. The objects of these actions (the created things) are not pre-eternal; they exist in time. The purpose is to avoid the misconception that the objects themselves are eternal. Thus, Allah is eternally the Creator, but creation occurs in time.

Key Terms
صفات الفعل
الأزل
المعلوم
المقدور عليه
المراد
المكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if Allah is described as 'Creator' in pre-eternity, then creation must also be pre-eternal.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the attribute is eternal but its object is temporal. Allah is eternally Creator, but creation occurs in time.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the purpose of recalling the times of the objects (such as the known and the capable-of-being) when describing Allah in pre-eternity?
A) To prove that those objects are also pre-eternal
B) To avoid imagining that those objects are pre-eternal
C) To show that Allah's attributes change over time
D) To emphasize that only action attributes are eternal

Key Takeaway
Allah's action attributes are eternal in their description of Him, but the objects they relate to exist in time and are not pre-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Creator' in your daily prayers, do you truly affirm that He was always Creator, even before creation existed? What does that mean for your understanding of His independence from His creation?
?
If you believed that Allah's attributes of action were created in time (like some theologians claim), how would that affect your trust in His eternal power to hear your prayers? Has this lesson challenged that assumption?
?
The lesson warns against imagining that objects are pre-eternal. Do you ever unconsciously treat created things (like your health, wealth, or status) as if they have always been or as if they are necessary? How does this principle call you to reorient your heart towards Allah alone?

The Principle of Tawqif in Allah's Names
Maturidi states the rule of tawqif in divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that Allah's names are restricted to those revealed in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Core Idea
We cannot name Allah except by what He named Himself.

Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational rule for how Muslims speak about Allah: we must restrict ourselves to names that Allah has revealed. This principle, called tawqif, prevents human speculation and innovation in theology. The passage below states this rule clearly, and then applies it to a specific controversial term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on tawqif
Read the Arabic text and translation for the first part: 'وقال الماتريدي بالتوقيف في أسماء الله، فلا يجوز أن نسميه إلا بما سمى به نفسه وجاء به الشرع.'
Notice the absolute restriction: no name beyond revelation.
2
Understand the logical basis
Ask yourself: why would Maturidi insist on tawqif? Consider that human intellect alone cannot determine how to address Allah. Revelation is the only source.
3
Apply to 'jism'
Maturidi immediately applies the rule: since 'jism' is not in revelation, we do not use it, even if we reinterpret its meaning.
This connects to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الماتريدي بالتوقيف في أسماء الله، فلا يجوز أن نسميه إلا بما سمى به نفسه وجاء به الشرع. وعلى ذلك فلا نقول بأن الله جسم حتى أردنا به المغايرة أي أن الله جسم لا كالأجسام أو أردنا به الإثبات أو الوجود. يقول الماتريدي: وحقه السمع عن الله، إن الجسم ليس من أسمائه، ولم يرد إذن لأحد تقليده، فالقول به لا يسوغ، ولو وسع بالنحت [؟] لو هذه ولا عن أحاد ثمن أو سمعي يصح من غير دليل حسبي أو عقلي لوسع القبول بالجسد والشخص، وكل ذلك مستنكر بالسمع، وليسع القيل بكل ما يسمى به الخلق، وذلك فاسد... إذ لا يسمى به الأعراض ويقل: لا أحد يجعل الجسم من أسماء الإثبات والصفات على أحد اسم الإثبات، لذلك بطل القول به
And Maturidi said regarding restriction (tawqif) in the names of Allah: it is not permissible for us to name Him except with what He named Himself and what has come in the Law. And upon that, we do not say that Allah is a body (jism) even if we intend by it otherness, i.e., that Allah is a body unlike bodies, or we intend by it affirmation or existence. Maturidi says: And its right is that hearing from Allah, indeed 'body' is not among His names, and no permission has come for anyone to imitate it, so saying it is not permissible. And if it were allowed by carving [?] ... or by hearing it would be correct without a rational or scriptural proof, then acceptance of 'body' and 'person' would be allowed, and all that is rejected by revelation, and it is not permissible to say everything by which creation is named, and that is corrupt... because it is not used for accidents. And it is said: No one makes 'body' among the names of affirmation and attributes as one of the names of affirmation, therefore the statement is invalid.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's doctrine of tawqif (restriction) regarding the names of Allah: we may only call Allah by names that He has revealed in the Qur'an or through the Prophet. Therefore, the term 'jism' (body) is not permissible, even if one intends a meaning like 'unlike bodies' or simply affirming existence. Maturidi argues that 'jism' is not among Allah's names, and since revelation does not permit it, using it is invalid. He also refutes the possibility of analogical reasoning here: if such a name were allowed without scriptural or rational proof, then many improper names would be accepted. Furthermore, 'jism' is a name used for created bodies, not for accidents, and it cannot be considered a name of affirmation (like 'Existent') for Allah. Hence, the use of 'jism' for Allah is rejected.

Key Terms
التوقيف
أسماء الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that any name that does not contradict Allah's perfection can be used for Him, even if not revealed.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly restricts naming to revealed names only; human reasoning alone is insufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the principle of tawqif regarding Allah's names?
A) We may use any name that intellectually befits Allah.
B) We may only use names that Allah has used for Himself or that appear in revelation.
C) We may use names from created beings by analogy.
D) We may only use the 99 names mentioned in hadith.

Key Takeaway
Allah's names are restricted to those revealed; human invention of names is forbidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever called Allah by a name that you thought was beautiful but never found in the Quran or authentic hadith? How will you verify the names you use from now on?
?
When you make dua, do you consciously choose names of Allah that are authentically revealed, or do you rely on names you've heard without checking? What step will you take this week to ensure your dua uses only tawqifi names?
?
If someone tells you that a certain name for Allah is permissible because 'it has a good meaning,' will you correct them based on this lesson? How will you explain the principle of tawqif to them?

Rejecting the Term 'Jism' for Allah
Maturidi rejects calling Allah 'jism' using tawqif principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the term 'jism' (body) impermissible for Allah, even if reinterpreted.

Core Idea
Even a reinterpreted 'jism' is invalid because revelation itself does not permit it.

Having established the principle of tawqif, Maturidi applies it to a specific term used by some theologians: 'jism' (body). Some argued that if 'jism' is understood in a negative sense (unlike bodies) or as mere affirmation of existence, it might be acceptable. Maturidi refutes this by insisting on the revealed source of names and by showing the logical consequences of allowing such analogical naming.

Guided Path
1
Read the rejection argument
Read the Arabic: 'وعلى ذلك فلا نقول بأن الله جسم حتى أردنا به المغايرة...' and the rest of the passage.
Notice the structure: Maturidi first states the ruling, then refutes potential justifications.
2
Understand the principle-based refutation
Maturidi argues that even if one intends a non-literal meaning, the name itself is not revealed, so it's forbidden. He also warns that allowing one analogical name would open the door to many improper names.
This shows the danger of not adhering strictly to tawqif.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Maturidi concludes that if 'jism' were allowed, then 'person' (shakhs) would also be allowed, and names used for created things would be applied to Allah, which is corrupt.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الماتريدي بالتوقيف في أسماء الله، فلا يجوز أن نسميه إلا بما سمى به نفسه وجاء به الشرع. وعلى ذلك فلا نقول بأن الله جسم حتى أردنا به المغايرة أي أن الله جسم لا كالأجسام أو أردنا به الإثبات أو الوجود. يقول الماتريدي: وحقه السمع عن الله، إن الجسم ليس من أسمائه، ولم يرد إذن لأحد تقليده، فالقول به لا يسوغ، ولو وسع بالنحت [؟] لو هذه ولا عن أحاد ثمن أو سمعي يصح من غير دليل حسبي أو عقلي لوسع القبول بالجسد والشخص، وكل ذلك مستنكر بالسمع، وليسع القيل بكل ما يسمى به الخلق، وذلك فاسد... إذ لا يسمى به الأعراض ويقل: لا أحد يجعل الجسم من أسماء الإثبات والصفات على أحد اسم الإثبات، لذلك بطل القول به
And Maturidi said regarding restriction (tawqif) in the names of Allah: it is not permissible for us to name Him except with what He named Himself and what has come in the Law. And upon that, we do not say that Allah is a body (jism) even if we intend by it otherness, i.e., that Allah is a body unlike bodies, or we intend by it affirmation or existence. Maturidi says: And its right is that hearing from Allah, indeed 'body' is not among His names, and no permission has come for anyone to imitate it, so saying it is not permissible. And if it were allowed by carving [?] ... or by hearing it would be correct without a rational or scriptural proof, then acceptance of 'body' and 'person' would be allowed, and all that is rejected by revelation, and it is not permissible to say everything by which creation is named, and that is corrupt... because it is not used for accidents. And it is said: No one makes 'body' among the names of affirmation and attributes as one of the names of affirmation, therefore the statement is invalid.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's doctrine of tawqif (restriction) regarding the names of Allah: we may only call Allah by names that He has revealed in the Qur'an or through the Prophet. Therefore, the term 'jism' (body) is not permissible, even if one intends a meaning like 'unlike bodies' or simply affirming existence. Maturidi argues that 'jism' is not among Allah's names, and since revelation does not permit it, using it is invalid. He also refutes the possibility of analogical reasoning here: if such a name were allowed without scriptural or rational proof, then many improper names would be accepted. Furthermore, 'jism' is a name used for created bodies, not for accidents, and it cannot be considered a name of affirmation (like 'Existent') for Allah. Hence, the use of 'jism' for Allah is rejected.

Key Terms
الجسم
الإثبات
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that 'jism' can be used for Allah if it is reinterpreted to mean 'unlike bodies' or 'existent'.
Correction: Maturidi forbids it because the name itself is not revealed; the intent does not change the ruling.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is calling Allah 'jism' (body) forbidden even if one interprets it as 'body unlike bodies'?
A) Because it still implies physicality.
B) Because the name 'jism' itself is not revealed by Allah in the Qur'an or Sunnah.
C) Because it contradicts the attribute of oneness.
D) Because it is a name used only for humans.

Key Takeaway
Even reinterpreted revealed names are impermissible; only divinely revealed names may be used for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any names or descriptions you casually use for Allah that have no basis in revelation? What step will you take today to ensure you only use what is revealed?
?
When you hear others attribute names or qualities to Allah that are not from the Quran or Sunnah, do you remain silent out of social comfort? How will you respond with courage next time?
?
Maturidi argues that even a well-intentioned reinterpretation cannot justify a non-revealed name. In what areas of your faith have you prioritized 'good intentions' over explicit divine permission? What will you change starting now?

Why It Is Permissible to Call God 'Thing'
Maturidi proves the permissibility of naming God 'thing' from Quran and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why calling Allah 'thing' (shay') is permissible despite the principle that names are tawqifiyya, and how this affirms existence without implying comparison.

Core Idea
Calling God 'thing' affirms His existence without comparing Him to anything.

The principle that Allah's names are tawqifiyya—restricted to revelation—raises a natural question: how can we call Him 'thing' (shay') if the Quran itself does? In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi shows that the Quran does exactly that, and reason confirms it. We will explore the scriptural evidence and the rational justification, learning that 'thingness' here is a term of pure affirmation, not likeness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The passage begins by stating the tawqifiyya principle as a given. Recognize that this creates an apparent barrier to using 'thing' for God, which the passage then resolves.
Tawqifiyya means names must come from revelation.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the two Quranic verses (42:11 and 6:19) cited. Notice that one uses 'thing' in a negation, the other in a question. The author uses them to prove that the word 'thing' is applied to Allah in revelation.
3
Reflect on the rational argument
The passage explains that 'thingness' in custom is a term of affirmation only. Consider why this matters: it means calling God 'thing' does not imply limitation or comparison, but merely confirms His existence.
The concern that 'thing' belittles is removed by context and intent.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that 'thing' is a name of affirmation and a negation of ta'til (denial). Appreciate how this fits into the broader kalam project of defending God's existence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كانت أسماء الله توقيفية جاز لنا أن نسميه شيء، لقوله تعالى: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ، وقوله قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَادَةٌ قُلْ اللَّه شَهِيدٌ، والعقل لا يمنع هذه التسمية لأن الشيئية اسم الإثبات لا غير في العرف؛ إذ القول بلا نفي، إذا لم يرد به التصغير، فثبت أنه اسم الاثبات ونفي التعطيل.
And when the names of Allah are restricted (tawqifiyya), it is permissible for us to name Him 'thing' (shay'), due to His statement: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11), and His statement: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is witness between me and you' (6:19), and reason does not prevent this naming because 'thingness' is a name of affirmation only in custom; for the statement [that He is a thing] is not a negation, when it is not intended to belittle, so it is established that it is a name of affirmation and negation of denial (ta'til).
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: if names are tawqifiyya (restricted to revelation), how can we call God 'thing' (شيء)? The answer is that the Quran itself uses this word for God in two verses: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11) and 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is witness' (6:19). Thus, it is permitted by revelation. Moreover, reason does not forbid it, because 'thingness' (الشيئية) in language is a term of affirmation (إثبات) only; it simply confirms existence without any limitation. When we say 'Allah is a thing', we are not negating anything unless we intend belittlement. Therefore, this name affirms God's existence and refutes those who deny His reality (التعطيل).

Key Terms
شيء
إثبات
تعطيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling God 'thing' implies He is like other things, thus it is inappropriate.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'thing' here is purely affirmative, establishing existence without comparison. It does not imply similarity; it is a term of affirmation, not description of essence.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Maturidi consider it permissible to call Allah 'thing'?
A) Because the word 'thing' is not really a name but a description.
B) Because the Quran uses the word for Allah and reason does not oppose it.
C) Because reason alone allows any name that is not demeaning.
D) Because the principle of tawqifiyya does not apply to this word.

Key Takeaway
Calling Allah 'thing' is valid because it is found in the Quran and signifies pure affirmation of existence without comparison, thus refuting those who deny His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever hesitated to call Allah 'thing' because it felt too ordinary or irreverent? What does that hesitation reveal about your understanding of affirmation versus comparison? Today, consciously use the name 'Thing' in your supplication, and reflect on how it affirms His existence without limit.
?
The passage argues that 'thingness' is pure affirmation and negation of denial. In your own life, do you subtly deny Allah's attributes by limiting Him to your own concepts? How can the name 'thing' break that limitation? Commit to memorizing verse 42:11 and reciting it when you feel your mind confining Allah.
?
Al-Maturidi shows that reason does not prevent this naming. But have you ever let your own reasoning overrule a revealed truth? Is there an area where you struggle to submit your intellect to the Quran? What would it take for you to trust the Quran's language over your own preconceptions?

The Maturidi Doctrine on Seeing Allah
Affirms vision of Allah by revelation, denies modality, contrasts Maturidi and Ash'ari on rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on vision of Allah, its basis in revelation, the negation of modality, and the key difference with the Ash'ari school regarding rational proof.

Core Idea
Maturidis affirm seeing Allah in the afterlife by revelation alone, without rational proof.

This passage presents the Maturidi doctrine on the vision of Allah (ru'ya). It clarifies that while all Sunni Muslims affirm the vision in the Hereafter, the Maturidi school differs from the Ash'ari in how they justify it. Here, Imam al-Maturidi emphasizes that the vision is free from any physical or spatial attributes, and that reason cannot prove its possibility—only revelation can establish it.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the affirmation
Notice that the passage begins by stating that vision of Allah is obligatory by revelation (sam'an) and immediately negates modality (bila kayf). Understand that modality is rejected because it implies form.
The Arabic phrase 'بلا كيف' is a key principle.
2
Examine the negation list
The passage includes a long list of attributes that are negated from the vision (e.g., standing, sitting, light, darkness). This shows how Maturidi emphasizes absolute transcendence (tanzih).
3
Note the consensus and difference
The passage states that both Maturidis and Ash'aris agree that believers see Allah in the Hereafter and disbelievers are veiled. Then it highlights their difference: Maturidi says reason cannot prove the possibility, while Ash'ari uses a rational proof (dalil al-wujud).
This is a key methodological divergence.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari proof
Consider the Ash'ari argument: whatever exists can be seen in this world; God exists, so He can be seen. Maturidi does not accept this analogy because it argues from the witnessed to the unseen.
Maturidi prefers to rely solely on revelation for matters of the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورؤية الله عند الماتريدي واجبة سمعاً، بلا كيف إذ الكيفية تكون لذي صورة، بل يرى بلا وصف قيام وقعود، واتكاء وتعلق، واتصال وانفصال، ومقابلة ومدابرة، وقصير وطويل، ونور وظلمة، وساكن ومتحرك، ومماس ومباين، وخارج وداخل، ولا معنى يأخذه الوهم أو يقدره العقل لتعاليه عن ذلك. والمؤمنون يرون الله في الآخرة ولا يرونه في الدنيا، أما الكفار فيحرومون من رؤيته في الآخرة لقوله تعالى: إِنَّهُمْ عَنْ رَبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ. على ذلك كله يجمع أهل السنة والجماعة من الماتريدية والأشعرية ولكنهم يختلفون بعد ذلك فيما بينهم على جواز الرؤية عقلاً. فالماتريدي يرى أن العقل لا يستطيع البرهنة على جواز الرؤية فيؤمن بها بدون تفسير، بينما يستدل عليها الأشعري بالعقل. ودليل الأشعري على الرؤية يسمى بدليل الوجود. إن المرئيات في الشاهد قد صحت رؤيتها لأنها موجودة، والله موجود، إذن تصح رؤيته.
And the vision of Allah according to al-Maturidi is obligatory by revelation (sam'an), without modality (bila kayf), for modality pertains to something with form; rather He is seen without description of standing or sitting, leaning or attachment, connection or separation, facing or turning away, short or long, light or darkness, stationary or moving, touching or separating, outside or inside, and no meaning that imagination can take or reason can measure – due to His transcendence above that. And the believers see Allah in the Hereafter and do not see Him in this world; as for the disbelievers, they are deprived of seeing Him in the Hereafter due to His statement: 'Indeed, from their Lord that Day they will be veiled' (83:15). All of this is agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a from the Maturidis and Ash'aris, but they differ afterwards among themselves on the permissibility of vision rationally. Al-Maturidi holds that reason cannot demonstrate the permissibility of vision, so he believes in it without interpretation, while al-Ash'ari deduces it by reason. And al-Ash'ari's proof for vision is called the proof of existence (dalil al-wujud). Verily, visible things in the witnessed world have had their vision verified because they exist, and Allah exists, so His vision is valid.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage explains the Maturidi doctrine on the vision of Allah (رؤية الله). It states that vision is obligatory by revelation (سمعاً) and not by reason. It begins by negating modality (كيف) because modality applies only to bodies with form. The vision is described as free from any spatial, directional, or physical attributes. The passage then affirms that believers will see Allah in the Hereafter (as per Sunni consensus) and disbelievers will be veiled (Quran 83:15). While Maturidis and Ash'aris agree on the fact of vision, they differ on rational proof: Maturidi says reason cannot prove its possibility, so he accepts it by revelation alone; Ash'ari uses the 'proof of existence' (dalil al-wujud), arguing that since anything existing can be seen (in the physical world), God, Who exists, can also be seen. The passage thus highlights a key methodological difference between the two schools.

Key Terms
رؤية
كيف
دليل الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi denies the vision of Allah because he does not use rational proof.
Correction: Maturidi affirms the vision wholeheartedly by revelation (sam'an), but he does not claim that reason can prove its possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between Maturidis and Ash'aris regarding the vision of Allah?
A) Maturidis deny it, Ash'aris affirm it.
B) Maturidis affirm it rationally, Ash'aris by revelation.
C) Maturidis affirm it by revelation without rational proof, Ash'aris use a rational proof.
D) They agree completely on both the fact and the proof.

Key Takeaway
Maturidis affirm the vision of Allah in the afterlife by revelation alone, rejecting any modality or rational proof as unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that you will see Allah in the Hereafter — but is that belief rooted in your personal certainty in revelation, or do you secretly wish for rational proof? What does your reliance on reason versus revelation say about the strength of your iman? This lesson calls you to submit without demanding explanations. Can you?
?
When you think of seeing Allah, do you find yourself forming mental images — light, beauty, a face? The passage says 'no modality, no description of standing or sitting, light or darkness.' Can you truly worship a God whose vision you cannot picture at all? What does that demand from your heart?
?
The disbelievers are veiled from seeing Allah on that Day. If you were to die tonight, would you be among those who are veiled? Look at your deeds and your attachment to this world. Are you preparing to see your Lord, or are you content with the veil of heedlessness? What will you change today to ensure you are not among the deprived?
