5 min
The Divine Names Are Revelation-Based and the Permissibility of 'Shay''
Maturidi argues that Allah's names are tawqifiyya and defends the name 'Shay''.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that Allah's names must be based on revelation, and how the name 'Shay'' (thing) is permissible.

Core Idea
Allah's names come from revelation, not reason; 'Shay'' affirms His existence without likeness.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational principle: the names of Allah are tawqifiyya, meaning they are determined solely by revelation. This principle is crucial for avoiding both anthropomorphism and denial of attributes. He then applies it to the specific name 'Shay'' (thing), showing from the Qur'an and rational argument that it is a valid name that affirms Allah's existence without implying any createdness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of tawqifiyya
Read the passage to see how Maturidi asserts that Allah's names are based on revelation, even if reason might object to some literal meanings.
Note the examples: 'light', 'guileful', etc. come from revelation, not reason.
2
Examine the evidence for 'Shay''
Notice the two Quranic verses Maturidi cites to prove that 'Shay'' is applied to Allah. This shows the name is rooted in revelation.
The verses are key: 42:11 and 6:19.
3
Understand the rational justification
Maturidi explains that 'thingness' here simply means affirmation of existence, not a diminutive or comparison. Reason does not forbid this.
This refutes the misconception that calling Allah 'thing' implies likeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأنه نور وأنه ماكر ومستهزئ وساخر من جهة السمع، وإن كان العقل يمنع من معاني هذه الأسماء فيه؛ فدل ذلك على أن المراعي في تسميته ما ورد به الشرع والإذن دون غيره. وإذا كانت أسماء الله توقيفية جاز لنا أن نسميه شيء، لقوله تعالى: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْء، وقوله قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَادَةٌ قُلْ اللَّه شَهِيدٌ، والعقل لا يمنع هذه التسمية لأن الشيئية اسم الإثبات لا غير في العرف؛ إذ القول بلا نفي، إذا لم يرد به التصغير، فثبت أنه اسم الاثبات ونفي التعطيل.
That He is light, and that He is guileful, mocking, and scornful, from the perspective of revelation, even if reason prevents the meanings of these names in His case; so that indicates that the consideration in naming Him is what the law and permission has come with, not other than it. And if the names of Allah are tawqifiyya (revelation-based), it is permissible for us to call Him 'Shay'' (thing), because of His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11), and His saying: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is a witness' (6:19). And reason does not prevent this name because 'thingness' is a name of affirmation only in convention; since the statement without negation, if it is not intended as diminution, it is established that it is a name of affirmation and negation of ta'til (denial of attributes).
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument establishing that Allah's names are tawqifiyya, meaning they must be taken from revelation. The author begins by referring to names like 'light' and 'guileful' which are mentioned in the Qur'an but whose literal meanings reason might not attribute to Allah. He concludes that the criterion for naming Allah is what is revealed, not what reason alone deems appropriate. Then he applies this to the name 'Shay'' (thing). He cites two verses: 'There is nothing like Him' and 'What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is a witness.' These verses apply the word 'thing' to Allah. Reason does not prevent this because 'thingness' in this context simply means affirmation of existence without any negation or diminutive connotation. Thus calling Allah 'Shay'' affirms His existence and refutes ta'til (denying His attributes).

Key Terms
توقيفية (tawqifiyya)
شيء (shay')
الإثبات (al-ithbat)
التعطيل (al-ta'til)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think calling Allah 'thing' implies He is like created things or limited.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'thingness' here is simply an affirmation of existence without any connotation of similarity or limitation. It is rooted in revelation and refutes denial of attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that calling Allah 'Shay'' is permissible?
A) Because reason demands it
B) Because it is mentioned in the Qur'an
C) Because it is a name of negation
D) Because it implies limitation

Key Takeaway
Allah's names must be derived from revelation; the name 'Shay'' is permissible because it appears in the Quran and affirms Allah's existence without implying likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah is called 'guileful' and 'mocking' in the Quran, do you feel hesitation or discomfort? What does that reveal about where you place your trust—revelation or your own reason? Will you commit to accepting every name Allah gives Himself, even if your mind struggles?
?
If someone told you that calling Allah 'thing' is disrespectful, how would you respond using only what this lesson teaches? Does your heart truly submit that Allah alone decides how He is named, or do you secretly prefer names that feel more 'dignified' by human standards?
?
The author says 'Shay'' affirms existence and negates denial. Do you live as though Allah's existence is the most certain reality in your life? Or do you sometimes act as if the world is more real than He is? What concrete change will you make today to treat His existence as the ultimate fact?

Maturidi's Position on the Vision of Allah (Ru'ya)
Maturidi explains that seeing Allah in the afterlife is obligatory by revelation, without modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi doctrine that seeing Allah in the afterlife is obligatory by revelation, without modality, and why Maturidis differ from Ash'aris on rational proof.

Core Idea
Vision of Allah is affirmed by revelation, not reason; Maturidi accepts without asking how.

This lesson presents Imam al-Maturidi's position on the vision of Allah in the afterlife. He affirms it as a revealed truth, while categorically rejecting any anthropomorphic modality. The passage also clarifies the agreement between Maturidis and Ash'aris on the fact of vision, and their subtle disagreement about the role of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key phrase 'وجبت سمعاً' (obligatory by revelation).
The phrase 'بلا كيف' is central.
2
Identify what is negated from the vision
The passage lists many attributes that do not apply to the vision of Allah. Count them and understand why they are negated.
These negations preserve Allah's transcendence.
3
Compare Maturidi and Ash'ari
Notice that both agree on the occurrence, but differ on rational proof. Maturidi says reason cannot prove it; Ash'ari says it can.
This difference is methodological.
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
The passage cites verse 83:15 to show that disbelievers are veiled from seeing Allah. This is a key proof text.
Believers will see, disbelievers will not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورؤية الله عند الماتريدي واجبة سمعاً، بلا كيف إذ الكيفية تكون لذي صورة، بل يرى بلا وصف قيام وقعود، واتكاء وتعلق، واتصال وانفصال، ومقابلة ومدابرة، وقصير وطويل، ونور وظلمة، وساكن ومتحرك، ومماس ومباين، وخارج وداخل، ولا معنى يأخذه الوهم أو يقدره العقل لتعاليه عن [؟] والمؤمنون يرون الله في الآخرة ولا يرونه في الدنيا، أما الكفار فمحرومون من رؤيته في الآخرة لقوله تعالى: إِنَّهُمْ عَنْ رَبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ. على ذلك كله يجمع أهل السنة والجماعة من الماتريدية والأشعرية ولكنهم يختلفون بعد ذلك فيما بينهم على جواز الرؤية عقلاً. فالماتريدي يرى أن العقل لا يستطيع البرهنة على جواز الرؤية فيؤمن بها بدون تفسير، بينما يستدل عليها الأشعري بالعقل.
And the vision of Allah according to al-Maturidi is obligatory by revelation (sam'an), without how, for 'how-ness' belongs to that which has form; rather, He is seen without the description of standing or sitting, leaning or attaching, connection or separation, facing or turning away, short or tall, light or darkness, stationary or moving, touching or separate, outside or inside, and no meaning that imagination takes or reason can conceive, due to His transcendence [؟]. And the believers will see Allah in the afterlife and they do not see Him in this world. As for the disbelievers, they are deprived of seeing Him in the afterlife, due to His saying: 'Indeed, they from their Lord that Day will be veiled' (83:15). Upon all of that the Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a among the Maturidis and Ash'aris agree, but they differ among themselves on the permissibility of vision rationally. Al-Maturidi holds that reason cannot prove the permissibility of vision, so he believes in it without interpretation, while al-Ash'ari uses reason to argue for it.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition of the Maturidi position on ru'ya (vision of Allah). It begins by stating that the vision of Allah in the afterlife is wajib sam'an, i.e., it is a binding duty to affirm it because it is established by revelation, not by reason. The vision is without modality (bila kayf) because any 'how' would imply that Allah has form or is subject to physical limitations. The author then lists many attributes that are negated from this vision: no standing, sitting, etc. All these are negated because they belong to contingent beings. The believers will see Allah in the afterlife (akhira) but not in this world (dunya), citing the verse from Sura al-Mutaffifin. The passage then states that both Maturidis and Ash'aris agree on the actual occurrence of vision in the afterlife, but they differ on whether reason can prove its possibility. Maturidi says reason cannot prove it, so he simply accepts it based on revelation without further rational explanation. Ash'ari uses a rational proof (which will be presented in the next lesson).

Key Terms
رؤية الله (ru'ya Allah)
سمعاً (sam'an)
بلا كيف (bila kayf)
الكيفية (al-kayfiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies the possibility of ru'ya altogether.
Correction: Maturidi affirms it as a revealed truth while denying rational proof; he does not deny the vision itself.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how do we know that Allah will be seen in the afterlife?
A) Through rational proof
B) Through revelation alone
C) Through analogical reasoning
D) Through mystical experience

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms the vision of Allah in the afterlife based solely on revelation, without any modality, and sees reason as incapable of proving its possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live with the certain hope of seeing your Lord in the afterlife, or does that reality barely cross your mind? What would change in your daily actions if you truly expected that vision?
?
Maturidi accepted the vision of Allah without demanding rational proof. Are there truths in your faith that you simply submit to because Allah said them, or do you subtly require your intellect to approve first? Where is your heart's submission challenged?
?
The disbelievers are veiled from seeing Allah in the afterlife. Does that warning move you to gratitude for your faith and urgency for those who do not have it? When was the last time you sincerely prayed, 'O Allah, let me behold Your face in Paradise'?

Maturidi's Stance on Istiwa': Affirmation without Ta'wil
Maturidi affirms istiwa' and cites Salaf's responses on modality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's methodological approach to ambiguous divine attributes, specifically the verse on istiwa', and how it aligns with the Salaf's position of affirming the meaning while negating modality.

Core Idea
For Maturidi and the Salaf, affirming istiwa' means accepting the verse as true without knowing how.

This lesson presents Maturidi's own words on the verse 'al-Rahmanu 'ala al-'arshi istawa' and then shows how his approach echoes the famous responses of Rabi'a and Malik. By studying this passage, you will see how the early scholars balanced textual affirmation with rational transcendence, avoiding both anthropomorphism and outright denial.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's statement
Read the first part where Maturidi affirms the verse rationally and textually. Notice his reasoning: Allah was without place, so istiwa' cannot imply physical location.
He uses the phrase 'ka-ma thabata fi al-'aql' (as established in the intellect).
2
Examine the Salaf's responses
Read the quotes from Rabi'a and Malik. They both say the istiwa' is not unknown but the how (kayf) is not comprehensible. Notice Malik calls the question 'bid'a' (innovation).
3
Reflect on the shared method
Consider how Maturidi and the Salaf both avoid ta'wil (interpretation) and tashbih (comparison). They affirm the revealed text without specifying a modality.
This is the core of the Salafi-Maturidi consensus on mutashabihat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى العَرْشِ اسْتَوَى نقول بها على ما ثبت في العقل ، من أن الله تعالى كان ولا مكان ، ويجوز ارتفاع الأمكنة وبقاؤه على ما كان ، فهو على ما كان ، وكان على ما عليه الآن ، جل عن التغيير والزوال والاستحالة والبطلان ، ونقول بها على ما جاء به التنزيل ، ثم لا نقطع تأويله على شيء لاحتماله غيره ، ونؤمن بما أراد الله به ، وكذلك في كل أمر ثبت التنزيل فيه نحو الرؤية وغير ذلك يجب نفي الشبه عنه والايمان بما أراده من غير تحقيق على شيء دون شيء. ويذكرنا الماتريدي هنا بموقف السلف من المتشابهات . سئل ربيعة الرأي عن قوله تعالى : الرحمنُ عَلَى العَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ، كيف استوى ؟ فقال : الاستواء غير مجهول ، والكيف غير معقول ، ومن الله الرسالة ، وعلى الرسول البلاغ ، وعلينا التصديق . وروي عن مالك بن أنس أنه سئل كيف استوى ؟ فأطرق برأسه ثم قال : الاستواء غير مجهول ، والكيف غير معقول ، والايمان به واجب ، والسؤال هذه بدعة . إذن فالماتريدي يقف - كما وقف السلف عند ما جاء في النصوص من غير تفسير ولا تأويل ، ويؤمن به كما أراده الله
And the Most Merciful is established over the Throne. We affirm it as established in the intellect: that Allah, exalted, existed and there was no place, and it is permissible for places to be removed while He remains as He was. So He is as He was, and He was as He is now, exalted above change, removal, transformation, and annihilation. And we affirm it according to what came in revelation, then we do not decide its interpretation definitively due to the possibility of other interpretations, and we believe in what Allah intended by it. Likewise, in every matter that revelation established, such as vision and other things, it is necessary to negate any similarity from Him and to believe in what He intended without specifying one interpretation over another. And here Maturidi reminds us of the Salaf's position on ambiguous verses. Rabi'a al-Ra'y was asked about the verse 'The Most Merciful is established over the Throne'—how is He established? He said: 'The establishment is not unknown, the modality is not comprehensible, from Allah is the message, upon the messenger is the delivery, and upon us is affirmation.' And it is narrated from Malik ibn Anas that he was asked 'how is He established?' He lowered his head then said: 'The establishment is not unknown, the modality is not comprehensible, belief in it is obligatory, and asking about it is an innovation.' So Maturidi stands—as the Salaf stood—by what came in the texts without interpretation or ta'wil, and believes in it as Allah intended.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of Maturidi's methodological stance on the ambiguous verses of the Quran, specifically the verse about istiwa' (establishment over the Throne). The author begins by quoting Maturidi's own words: he affirms the verse in accordance with reason, which establishes that Allah existed without place and remains after places are removed. He is free from change. Then he affirms the verse as it came in revelation, without fixing a specific interpretation (ta'wil) because multiple interpretations are possible. Instead, he believes in what Allah intended. This approach applies to all revealed matters, including the vision of God (ru'ya). The author then compares this to the Salaf, citing the famous responses of Rabi'a and Malik. Their statements emphasize that the meaning (istiwa') is known (i.e., the word has a real meaning), but the modality (kayf) is unknown and cannot be conceived. Therefore, questioning 'how' is an innovation. The author concludes that Maturidi follows the Salaf's method of accepting the text without interpretation or ta'wil, believing in Allah's intended meaning without specification.

Key Terms
استوى
تأويل
السلف
تشبيه
كيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Salaf and Maturidi simply accept the literal meaning of istiwa' as a physical sitting.
Correction: In fact, they affirm a real meaning but deny any modality (kayf), avoiding both ta'til (denial) and tashbih (comparison).

Checkpoint
According to the Salaf, what is the proper response to 'how is Allah established over the Throne?'
A) The establishment is metaphorical and not real.
B) The establishment is unknown, and we should not ask.
C) The establishment is not unknown, but the modality is not comprehensible; we must believe.
D) The establishment is a physical sitting, but we cannot describe it.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi follows the Salaf in affirming the verse on istiwa' without ta'wil, accepting the meaning while negating modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'The Most Merciful is established over the Throne,' what first comes to your mind? Do you feel uneasy admitting that you do not know how? Can you honestly say, like the Salaf, 'I believe in what Allah intended' without demanding a physical picture?
?
Is there a verse or attribute of Allah that you have secretly wanted to 'figure out' or explain away because it felt uncomfortable? Will you today submit to His words and trust that His knowledge far surpasses yours, even if your mind is not satisfied?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological discussion by saying 'Allah knows best' as a way to avoid deeper reflection? Commit instead to saying 'I affirm it as true, but I don't know how'—and let that train your heart in true tawhid.

Maturidi's Salaf-Based Approach to Mutashabihat
Maturidi cites Rabi'a and Malik on handling ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's methodology for handling ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashabihat) by following the Salaf, as exemplified by the statements of Rabi'a al-Ra'y and Malik ibn Anas.

Core Idea
Accept ambiguous verses as they are, without asking 'how'.

Imam al-Maturidi, often associated with rational theology, actually grounds his approach to ambiguous verses in the way of the Salaf. In this passage, he cites two early authorities—Rabi'a al-Ra'y and Malik ibn Anas—to show that the proper response is to affirm the meaning without delving into modality. This lesson unpacks their statements and Maturidi's conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to familiarize yourself with the key statements.
2
Identify the two authorities
Notice that Maturidi uses two early scholars, Rabi'a and Malik, to support his position.
3
Analyze the common formula
Both scholars say 'istiwā is not unknown, and the 'how' is inconceivable'—what does this mean?
4
Understand Maturidi's conclusion
He says he stands with the Salaf, accepting texts without interpretation or denial.
5
Reflect on the significance
Why is this stance important for Maturidi's theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكرنا الماتريدي هنا بموقف السلف من المتشابهات . سئل ربيعة الرأي عن قوله تعالى : الرحمنُ عَلَى العَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ، كيف استوى ؟ فقال : الاستواء غير مجهول ، والكيف غير معقول ، ومن الله الرسالة ، وعلى الرسول البلاغ ، وعلينا التصديق . وروي عن مالك بن أنس أنه سئل كيف استوى ؟ فأطرق برأسه ثم قال : الاستواء غير مجهول ، والكيف غير معقول ، والايمان به واجب ، والسؤال هذه بدعة . إذن فالماتريدي يقف - كما وقف السلف عند ما جاء في النصوص من غير تفسير ولا تأويل ، ويؤمن به كما أراده الله
And al-Maturidi reminds us here of the position of the Salaf regarding ambiguous verses. Rabi'a al-Ra'y was asked about the saying of the Exalted: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne'—how did He establish? He said: 'Istiwā is not unknown, and the 'how' is inconceivable; from Allah is the message, upon the Messenger is the delivery, and upon us is the affirmation.' And it is narrated from Malik ibn Anas that he was asked 'How did He establish?', so he bowed his head then said: 'Istiwā is not unknown, and the 'how' is inconceivable, and belief in it is obligatory, and asking about it is an innovation.' Thus al-Maturidi stands—as the Salaf stood—regarding what came in the texts without interpretation or allegorical explanation, and believes in it as Allah intended.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage defines Maturidi's methodological position on the ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) by citing the statements of two early Salaf figures. The passage begins by reminding us that Maturidi draws attention to the Salaf's stance. Then it quotes the response of Rabi'a al-Ra'y: when asked about the 'how' of Allah's istiwā, he affirms that istiwā is known in meaning, but the modality is not comprehensible. He also outlines the division of responsibilities: the message is from Allah, the delivery is upon the Messenger, and upon us is affirmation. Next, the passage reports a similar answer from Malik ibn Anas, who adds that belief in it is obligatory and questioning it is an innovation. The passage then concludes that Maturidi follows this same path: he accepts the texts as they came without ta'wil (interpretation) or ta'til (denial), and believes in them as Allah intended.

Key Terms
المتشابهات
الاستواء
السلف
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi interprets all ambiguous verses metaphorically (ta'wil).
Correction: This passage shows Maturidi explicitly follows the Salaf who accepted the text without asking 'how', which is tafwid, not ta'wil.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Malik ibn Anas say about asking how Allah established Himself on the Throne?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is recommended
C) It is an innovation
D) It is permissible

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's approach to mutashabihat is to affirm their meaning while leaving the 'how' to Allah, following the Salaf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are you truly willing to accept that some of Allah's attributes are known in meaning but beyond your mental grasp? What does that say about your humility before Allah?
?
Malik called asking 'how' an innovation. When has your desire to 'understand fully' ever led you to question or reinterpret clear texts? Will you now repent from that and adopt the Salaf's mode of affirmation?

Maturidi's Definition of Justice: Affirming Divine Power and Fairness
Maturidi defines justice as affirming divine power and fairness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of justice as the affirmation of both divine power and divine fairness, rooted in Quranic descriptions of Allah.

Core Idea
Justice in Maturidi means affirming that Allah is both all-powerful and perfectly just.

This lesson introduces a foundational passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid where he defines justice (al-ʿadl). Rather than focusing on human actions alone, Maturidi grounds justice in how we describe Allah Himself. The passage presents two complementary affirmations, each supported by Quranic verses, that together form the basis of a balanced theological understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Do not analyse yet—just let the structure and key terms sink in.
Notice that the passage opens with a heading: 'Introduction on human actions'.
2
Identify the two matters
Maturidi says justice is 'the statement affirming two matters'. Find these two matters in the text and see how they are introduced: the first concerns how Allah describes Himself, the second concerns His justice and grace.
The first matter is introduced by 'ليكون الله موصوفاً...' and the second by 'وليكون عدلا متفضلاً'.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
Each matter is supported by two Quranic verses. Identify which verses correspond to which matter and note what each verse emphasizes about Allah.
The first pair (Creator, Omnipotent) supports divine power; the second pair (no injustice, grace) supports divine fairness.
4
Reflect on the balance
Consider why Maturidi insists on holding both affirmations together. What would go wrong if we emphasized only divine power without justice, or only justice without power?
This balance is central to Maturidi's approach to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة في أفعال الإنسان. يقول الماتريدي: والعدل هو القول بتحقيق الأمرين؛ ليكون الله موصوفاً بما وصف به نفسه محموداً به كما قال: خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ، وقال: فَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ، وليكون عدلا متفضلاً كما قال: وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظلام لِلْعَبِيدِ وقال: وَلَوْلا فَضلُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْكُمْ وَرَحْمَتُهُ لاتَّبَعْتُمُ الشَّيْطَانَ إِلا قَلِيلا.
Introduction on human actions. Al-Maturidi says: Justice is the statement affirming two matters: so that Allah is described as He described Himself, praised thereby, as He said: 'Creator of everything', and said: 'He is over all things competent', and so that He is just and gracious, as He said: 'And your Lord is not unjust to the servants' and said: 'Had it not been for the grace of Allah upon you and His mercy, you would have followed Satan, except a few'.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage introduces Maturidi's discussion of human actions by defining justice (al-ʿadl). Maturidi states that justice is the affirmation of two matters: first, that Allah is described with the attributes He described Himself with, such as being the Creator of all things and omnipotent; second, that He is just and gracious, meaning He does not wrong His servants and bestows grace upon them. He supports both points with Quranic verses: the first with Surah Al-An'am 6:101 and Surah Al-Baqarah 2:20 (allusion to power) and the second with Surah Fussilat 41:46 and Surah An-Nisa 4:83 (about grace). Thus, justice encompasses both divine power and fairness.

Key Terms
العدل
الفضل
خلق كل شيء
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Maturidi's definition of justice is primarily about human free will or a mechanical balance of reward and punishment.
Correction: Maturidi here roots justice in the very attributes of Allah: His power and His fairness/grace. It is not about human actions per se but about how we must describe Allah to avoid misrepresenting Him.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what are the two matters that define justice?
A) Divine power and human free will
B) Divine power and divine fairness/grace
C) Divine mercy and human responsibility
D) Divine justice and predestination

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's definition of justice requires affirming both Allah's absolute power and His perfect fairness and grace, as revealed in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you truly believe that Allah is both all-powerful over that situation and not unjust to you? Or do you secretly harbor resentment towards His decree? Before you sleep, examine a recent difficulty and ask Allah for a heart that trusts both His power and His justice.
?
Maturidi says justice includes affirming Allah's grace – that without His mercy you would follow Satan. Do you live with that awareness, or do you take your faith for granted as if it were your own achievement? Today, thank Allah for one specific act of guidance you did not earn.
?
If someone wrongs you, do you instinctively demand strict justice from Allah, forgetting that His justice includes grace? How can you mirror that balance in your own dealings? Before forgiving someone tonight, ask Allah to help you be just yet gracious, as He is.

Objection and Answer on Permanence and Pre-Eternity
An objection about the possibility of pre-eternity based on permanence in the afterlife is answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pre-eternity and permanence after creation are not analogous, and why the objection based on this analogy fails.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity and post-creation permanence belong to different logical categories.

A common objection against the possibility of pre-eternity for created things is that if God can make things last forever after they are created, why can't He make them exist from eternity? This passage records that very objection and begins to answer it. The answer focuses on the logical contradiction implied by the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Understand exactly what the objector is saying: if God can make bodies permanent in the afterlife, why can't He make them pre-eternal?
Note the key terms: baqā (permanence) and qidam (pre-eternity).
2
Identify the key terms
Permanence (baqā) refers to continuing existence after a beginning. Pre-eternity (qidam) refers to having no beginning at all. The objector tries to equate them.
3
Consider the answer's first reason
The answer begins by saying there are several reasons, and the first is 'contradiction' (al-tanāquḍ). It explains that pre-eternity implies no beginning, but bodies are created with a beginning, so claiming pre-eternity for them is contradictory.
The Arabic text is incomplete here, but the logic is clear.
4
Reflect on the difference
Think about why permanence and pre-eternity are not the same. Permanence can be granted by God after creation; pre-eternity would mean something never had a beginning, which cannot be true for created things.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : إذا جاز عندكم بقاء الأعبان في الآخرة بما لا يبقى ، فلم لا جاز قدمها بما لا يتقدم ؟ قيل : لوجوه ، أحدها للتناقض وهو لم يكن ، فمن لا يسبقه فقيه حقيقية ، فالقول فيه بالقدم ينقضه ، ومعنى البقاء
For if someone says: If you allow the permanence of bodies (al-aʿyān) in the afterlife with what does not remain, then why do you not allow their pre-eternity with what is not preceded? He was told: For reasons, one of them is contradiction – and it is that which was not, so whoever does not precede it, its reality is...? So the statement about it being pre-eternal contradicts it, and the meaning of permanence...
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and the beginning of an answer. The objector argues: You (theologians) claim that bodies will remain permanently in the afterlife, even though they are not inherently permanent (they were created). If that is possible, why can't bodies be pre-eternal even if they were not inherently pre-eternal? In other words, if God can make bodies last forever after creation, why could He not have made them exist from eternity? The reply begins by saying there are several reasons. The first is based on contradiction: the concept of pre-eternity (qidam) implies that something has no beginning, while the concept of temporal origination (ḥudūth) implies it had a beginning. To claim pre-eternity for something that began would be contradictory. The text also mentions 'the meaning of permanence' (maʿnā al-baqāʾ), but the passage is cut off. The answer likely distinguishes between permanence after creation (which is a gift from God) and pre-eternity (which is an essential attribute). The objection attempts to equate two different metaphysical categories, and the answer points out that they are not analogous.

Key Terms
البقاء (al-baqāʾ)
القدم (al-qidam)
التقدم (al-taqaddum)
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ)
الأعيان (al-aʿyān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God can make something permanent, He could also make it eternal.
Correction: Pre-eternity (qidam) is about having no beginning, while permanence (baqā) is about continuing after a beginning. They are different logical categories; one does not imply the other.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main point in this passage?
A) That permanence in the afterlife proves pre-eternity
B) That if God can make bodies last forever, why can't He make them pre-eternal?
C) That the afterlife is a myth
D) That bodies cannot remain forever

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternity (qidam) and permanence (baqā) are not analogous because pre-eternity requires having no beginning, while permanence can be granted after creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's power, do you ever assume that because He can do one thing, He must be able to do another without considering logical categories? Where in your life have you made such an assumption, and what was the consequence?
?
The objector's error was equating two different things. Is there a belief about God or about yourself that you hold tightly, even though it contains a hidden contradiction? What would it cost you to see that contradiction clearly?
?
If pre-eternity is not a property that can be granted, but rather a logical necessity, how does that reshape your understanding of God's uniqueness? Will you let this lesson change the way you speak about God's attributes from now on?

Motion, Rest, Composition, and Separation as Accidents
Maturidi proves that motion, rest, composition, and separation are accidents (a'rad) other than the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves that motion, rest, composition, and separation are accidents distinct from the body using the argument of changing states.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are not the body itself; they are accidents that can come and go.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise argument to establish that certain observable states—motion, rest, composition, and separation—are not identical to the body but are rather accidents that inhere in it. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of physical substances and the way they can receive changes. Let us examine his proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the core premise
The passage states that a body can be separated and then come together, and moving and then become still. This is the key observation: the body can have opposing states.
3
Understand the conclusion
If these states were the body itself, they could not change while the body remains. Since they do change, they must be other than the body—i.e., accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم معلوم أن تكون الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والتفرق غير الجسم ; إذ قد يكون جسما متفرقا يجتمع ، ومتحركا يسكن ، فلو كان لنفسه يكون كذلك لم يكن ليحتمل مضادات الأحوال على بقاء الجسم بحاله .
Then it is known that motion, rest, composition, and separation are other than the body; for a body may be separated and then come together, and moving and then become still. For if it were by itself, it would not be possible to endure opposing states while the body remains in its condition.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage is a proof that motion (haraka), rest (sukun), composition (ijtima'), and separation (tafarruq) are accidents (a'rad) distinct from the body (jism). Maturidi argues that a body can be separated and then come together, and can move and then become still. If these states were the body itself, they would not change; the body would be permanently in one state. Since the body remains while these states alternate, they must be other than the body. This is a standard kalam argument for the reality of accidents.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
اجتماع
تفرق
جسم
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that motion and rest are substances or inseparable parts of the body.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are accidents because they can change while the body persists. If they were the body itself, they could not alternate.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi conclude that motion, rest, composition, and separation are other than the body?
A) Because they are not visible
B) Because the body can have opposing states while remaining the same
C) Because they are eternal
D) Because they are perceived by the senses

Key Takeaway
Motion, rest, composition, and separation are accidents because the body can undergo opposing states while remaining identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is constantly in motion or at rest, sometimes together, sometimes apart. Yet you know you are the same person. If these physical states are merely accidents that come and go, then what is the unchanging 'I' that remains? Have you ever truly pondered your own identity beyond the physical?
?
You often define yourself by your actions—'I am always busy' or 'I need to be still'. But Maturidi shows that motion and rest are not you; they are temporary attachments. Will you today look past your doing and find your being?
?
If your body's states can change without changing your essence, then your worth cannot depend on how active or productive you are. Are you willing to separate your self-worth from your physical performance and anchor it in something permanent?

Annihilation and Permanence as Distinct Accidents
Maturidi extends the argument to annihilation (fana') and permanence (baqa').

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves that annihilation (fana') and permanence (baqa') are accidents distinct from the body, based on the possibility of a neutral state and differing intentions.

Core Idea
If a thing can exist without being permanent or annihilated, those states are not the thing itself.

In this brief but powerful passage, Maturidi applies the same logical method used for other accidents to the concepts of annihilation and permanence. He observes that a thing may exist in a state that is neither permanent nor annihilated, and that willing permanence is different from willing annihilation. These observations establish that permanence and annihilation are real accidents that inhere in the body, distinct from its essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise structure: a general principle, then two supporting observations.
The phrase 'على هذا' refers back to the previous argument about accidents.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two separate reasons Maturidi gives: (1) something can exist at times without being permanent or annihilated, so those must be other than the thing; (2) intending permanence is a different intention than intending annihilation, so the intended objects are distinct.
3
Connect to the broader theme
This argument is part of establishing that all changeable states are accidents, which in turn supports the doctrine that Allah alone is the creator of all events.
Reflect on how this fits into Maturidi's overall project of refuting those who deny accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا يُخرج الفناء والبقاء ; إذ قد يوجد غير باق ولا فان في أوقات ، فيلزم أن يكون غيره ، وكذلك من أراد بقاء الشيء أو فناءه يقصد إلى غير الوجه الذي يقصد بالآخر ثبت أنهما غيران يحلان .
And upon this (principle) are derived annihilation (fana') and permanence (baqa'); for something may exist neither permanent nor annihilated at times, so it necessarily follows that it is other than it. And likewise, whoever intends the permanence of a thing or its annihilation intends a different aspect than what is intended by the other. It is established that they are two different things that inhere.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage applies the same reasoning to annihilation (fana') and permanence (baqa'). Maturidi argues that a thing can exist at times without being permanent or annihilated, so these must be distinct from the thing itself. Moreover, if someone wills the permanence of an object, that intention is different from willing its annihilation. Since the intentions are different, the objects of intention—permanence and annihilation—must be distinct accidents that inhere in the body. This further supports the reality of accidents.

Key Terms
فناء
بقاء
عرض
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that annihilation and permanence are simply the absence or presence of existence, not real attributes.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are distinct accidents because a thing can exist without being either, and intentions toward them differ.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi prove that annihilation and permanence are distinct from the body?
A) By showing they are perceived by senses
B) By noting that a thing can exist without being either permanent or annihilated, and intentions differ
C) By quoting scripture
D) By consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Annihilation and permanence are real accidents distinct from the body, proven by the possibility of a neutral state and differing intentions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every attachment you hold is subject to annihilation as a real accident, what would it mean to cling to anything in this world? Can you name one thing you treat as 'permanent' that must, by this teaching, be only a transient accident?
?
When you wake tomorrow, your existence will continue by the accident of permanence—a gift renewed each moment. How would you live today differently if you truly felt that your own 'remaining' is not fixed but an accident sustained by its Creator?

Proof that Motion and Rest are Accidents Distinct from the Body
Maturidi provides a proof that motion, rest, etc. are accidents other than the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that motion, rest, conjunction, and separation are distinct accidents inhering in the body, not identical to the body itself.

Core Idea
A body's ability to receive opposite states proves those states are added to it.

Maturidi opens with a simple but powerful observation: a body can be moving then still, or separate then united, while remaining the same body. This shows that motion and rest cannot be part of the body's essence; they must be distinct entities that come and go. In this lesson, we examine that proof and its foundational role in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage closely
Read the Arabic sentence once, then read the translation. Note the logical flow from observation to conclusion.
The passage uses a hypothetical reasoning: 'if it were by itself...'.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the words for motion, rest, conjunction, separation, and body. Understand how they are used in the argument.
3
Understand the logical structure
Maturidi presents a conditional argument: if motion were intrinsic to the body, the body couldn't receive its opposite. Since it does, motion must be distinct.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider: what does this say about the relationship between a thing and its attributes? This principle applies to all created things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم معلوم أن تكون الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والتفرق غير الجسم؛ إذ قد يكون جسما متفرقا يجتمع، ومتحركا يسكن، فلو كان لنفسه يكون كذلك لم يكن ليحتمل مضادات الأحوال على بقاء الجسم بحاله. وعلى هذا يُخرج الفناء والبقاء؛ إذ قد يوجد غير باق ولا فان في أوقات، فيلزم أن يكون غيره، وكذلك من أراد بقاء الشيء أو فناءه يقصد إلى غير الوجه الذي يقصد بالآخر ثبت أنهما غيران يحلان. وكثير من المعتزلة يقولون بهذا في الأول، ويأبون في البقاء والفناء، مع ما يبطل قولهم؛ إذ كان مختلفا بنفسه فى احتمال ذلك، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز بقاء الأجسام بأنفسها لجاز كونها لا بغير. على أن هذا بالمعتزلة أولى؛ لأنهم لا يجعلون الله إلى جملة العالم غير العالم، به كان العالم؛ إذ الإرادة يجعلونها من العالم والفعل كذلك، وذاته كان موجودا عندهم ولم يكن العالم ولم يحدث شيء سوى كون العالم، فإذا كان بنفسه وبه يبقى، وفى ذلك فساد التوحيد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it is known that motion and rest, and conjunction and separation, are other than the body; for it may be a body that is separate then gathers, or moving then becomes still. For if it were by itself as such, it would not be capable of receiving opposite states while the body remains in its condition. And according to this, annihilation and permanence are also excluded (as being distinct from the body); for something may exist that is not permanent and not annihilated at times, so it is necessary that it be something else. Likewise, whoever wills the permanence of a thing or its annihilation intends a different aspect from that intended by the other, so it is established that they are two different things that inhere. Many of the Mu'tazila say this regarding the first (i.e., motion/rest), but they refuse (to apply it) to permanence and annihilation, despite what invalidates their saying; for it is different by itself in the possibility of that. And if that were permissible, and the permanence of bodies by themselves were permissible, then it would be possible for them to be without (a sustainer). Moreover, this is more applicable to the Mu'tazila; because they do not make God, in relation to the whole world, other than the world, by which the world came to be. For they make will (al-irada) from the world, and similarly action, and His essence was existing according to them and the world was not, and nothing came to be except the existence of the world. So if it (the world) is by itself and by it it remains, then in that is corruption of tawhid, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage is a proof that attributes like motion, rest, and the like are distinct from the body itself. Maturidi argues by noting that a body can receive opposite states while remaining the same body, so those states must be additional entities inhering in the body. He extends the same reasoning to annihilation and permanence, showing they too are distinct. He then criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistently accepting this for some attributes but rejecting it for permanence and annihilation, and points out that their position leads to a corruption of tawhid, because if the world persists by itself, it would be independent of God. The argument proceeds in a logical sequence: first establishing the distinction, then applying it to specific cases, then refuting a contrary view.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
اجتماع
تفرق
جسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributes like motion are identical to the body or its essence.
Correction: Maturidi argues that because a body can receive opposite states while remaining the same, the attributes must be distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Maturidi's argument that motion and rest are distinct from the body?
A) An explicit Quranic verse
B) The body's ability to receive opposite states while remaining the same
C) The consensus of the Mu'tazila
D) The impossibility of change in the body

Key Takeaway
The ability of a body to undergo opposite changes proves that motion, rest, and similar attributes are accidents distinct from the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience countless changes every day—moving, resting, meeting, parting. If these are merely accidents that come and go, what does that say about the permanence of your own self? How does this truth affect your attachment to your changing states?
?
The proof shows that a body remains the same while receiving opposites. When you sin and repent, you remain the same person. How does this understanding of constancy amidst change challenge you to take responsibility for your actions rather than excusing them as 'just passing moods'?
?
If motion and rest are not you but accidental, then the One who gives and takes these accidents is the only constant. Are you living as if you are the owner of your movements and stillness, or have you acknowledged that they are loans from your Creator? What specific step can you take today to live that acknowledgement?

Extension to Annihilation/Permanence and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Maturidi extends the proof to annihilation/permanence and refutes Mu'tazila inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's extension of the same reasoning to annihilation and permanence, and his refutation of the Mu'tazila who inconsistently accept it for some accidents but reject it for these.

Core Idea
Inconsistency in applying the same logic leads to a corruption of tawhid.

Maturidi now applies his earlier argument to the concepts of annihilation and permanence, showing they too are distinct accidents. He then critiques the Mu'tazila for accepting the argument for motion and rest but rejecting it for permanence and annihilation. Finally, he demonstrates how their position ultimately undermines the doctrine of tawhid by making the world independent of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the continuation from 'وعلى هذا' to the end. Notice how Maturidi extends the same logical pattern.
2
Identify the extension
Maturidi says 'وعلى هذا يُخرج الفناء والبقاء' – he applies the same reasoning to annihilation and permanence.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
They accept the argument for motion/rest but reject it for permanence/annihilation. Maturidi points out the inconsistency.
4
Trace the final argument
Maturidi shows that if they reject the distinction, they end up saying bodies persist by themselves, which makes God unnecessary and corrupts tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم معلوم أن تكون الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والتفرق غير الجسم؛ إذ قد يكون جسما متفرقا يجتمع، ومتحركا يسكن، فلو كان لنفسه يكون كذلك لم يكن ليحتمل مضادات الأحوال على بقاء الجسم بحاله. وعلى هذا يُخرج الفناء والبقاء؛ إذ قد يوجد غير باق ولا فان في أوقات، فيلزم أن يكون غيره، وكذلك من أراد بقاء الشيء أو فناءه يقصد إلى غير الوجه الذي يقصد بالآخر ثبت أنهما غيران يحلان. وكثير من المعتزلة يقولون بهذا في الأول، ويأبون في البقاء والفناء، مع ما يبطل قولهم؛ إذ كان مختلفا بنفسه فى احتمال ذلك، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز بقاء الأجسام بأنفسها لجاز كونها لا بغير. على أن هذا بالمعتزلة أولى؛ لأنهم لا يجعلون الله إلى جملة العالم غير العالم، به كان العالم؛ إذ الإرادة يجعلونها من العالم والفعل كذلك، وذاته كان موجودا عندهم ولم يكن العالم ولم يحدث شيء سوى كون العالم، فإذا كان بنفسه وبه يبقى، وفى ذلك فساد التوحيد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it is known that motion and rest, and conjunction and separation, are other than the body; for it may be a body that is separate then gathers, or moving then becomes still. For if it were by itself as such, it would not be capable of receiving opposite states while the body remains in its condition. And according to this, annihilation and permanence are also excluded (as being distinct from the body); for something may exist that is not permanent and not annihilated at times, so it is necessary that it be something else. Likewise, whoever wills the permanence of a thing or its annihilation intends a different aspect from that intended by the other, so it is established that they are two different things that inhere. Many of the Mu'tazila say this regarding the first (i.e., motion/rest), but they refuse (to apply it) to permanence and annihilation, despite what invalidates their saying; for it is different by itself in the possibility of that. And if that were permissible, and the permanence of bodies by themselves were permissible, then it would be possible for them to be without (a sustainer). Moreover, this is more applicable to the Mu'tazila; because they do not make God, in relation to the whole world, other than the world, by which the world came to be. For they make will (al-irada) from the world, and similarly action, and His essence was existing according to them and the world was not, and nothing came to be except the existence of the world. So if it (the world) is by itself and by it it remains, then in that is corruption of tawhid, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage is a proof that attributes like motion, rest, and the like are distinct from the body itself. Maturidi argues by noting that a body can receive opposite states while remaining the same body, so those states must be additional entities inhering in the body. He extends the same reasoning to annihilation and permanence, showing they too are distinct. He then criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistently accepting this for some attributes but rejecting it for permanence and annihilation, and points out that their position leads to a corruption of tawhid, because if the world persists by itself, it would be independent of God. The argument proceeds in a logical sequence: first establishing the distinction, then applying it to specific cases, then refuting a contrary view.

Key Terms

بقاء
عرض
توحيد
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila thought that permanence is not an accident but an essential property of the body once created.
Correction: Maturidi shows that permanence must be an accident because the body can be impermanent at times, and because the intention for permanence differs from intention for annihilation.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi criticize the Mu'tazila in this passage?
A) They deny the existence of motion and rest
B) They inconsistently accept the argument for motion/rest but reject it for permanence/annihilation
C) They believe in the eternity of the world
D) They claim that God is the only cause

Key Takeaway
Consistency in applying the same logical reasoning is crucial; inconsistency in accepting accidents leads to a denial of God's role as Sustainer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila accepted part of the logic but rejected the rest. Are there areas in your faith where you pick and choose which divine attributes to fully rely on, while ignoring or downplaying others? What would it take to be consistent in your tawhid starting today?
?
When you consider your own life, do you ever act as if some of your blessings (health, relationships, status) are permanent by themselves, forgetting that they are sustained by Allah every moment? How can you discipline yourself to acknowledge His constant preservation in your daily thoughts and prayers?
?
The passage warns that attributing permanence to bodies by themselves leads to corruption of tawhid. Examine your heart: do you ever feel that your identity or worldly possessions are 'permanent' and self-sustaining? What is one concrete step you can take this week to break that illusion and reaffirm your total dependence on Allah?

Affirming Divine Attributes Without Anthropomorphism
Maturidi explains that affirming attributes does not imply tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that affirming divine attributes does not imply similarity to creation, and why using witnessed terms is necessary yet safe from anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Using terms from creation to describe God does not make Him like creation — it's the only way we can speak of Him.

Imam al-Maturidi begins this passage with a crucial methodological clarification. He affirms that God's names and attributes are real, yet this does not entail any similarity (tashbih) to created beings. He explains why human language inevitably uses concepts from the witnessed world, and how this is a necessary limitation, not a license for anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the initial assertion to the justification.
2
Identify the key distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between affirming attributes and implying similarity. Find how he expresses this distinction.
Look for the phrase 'ليس فى إثبات الأسماء... تشابه'
3
Understand the principle of dalala al-shahid
The passage uses the idea that the unseen (gha'ib) is known only through the witnessed (shahid). This is the basis for using created terms for God.
This principle is central to Maturidi's epistemology.
4
Apply to specific attributes
The passage mentions 'ulu (highness) and jalal (majesty). See how the principle applies to these attributes.
5
Reflect on human limitation
Maturidi states our capacity does not allow other naming. Consider why this limitation is important for avoiding tashbih.
This shows that the use of witnessed terms is a necessity, not a choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : وليس فى إثبات الأسماء وتحقيق الصفات تشابه لنفي حقائق ما في الخلق عنه كالمتية والثبات ، ولكن الأسماء لما لم تحتمل التعريف ولا تحقيق الذات بحق الربوبية إلا بذلك ، إذ لا وجه لمعرفة غائب إلا بدلالة الشاهد . ثم إذا أريد الصفة بالعلو والجلال فذلك طريق المعرفة في الشاهد ، وإمكان القول ؛ إذ لا يحتمل وسعنا العرفان بالتسمية بغير الذي شاهدنا ، ولا الإشارة إلى ما لا نأخذ من الحسن وحق العيان ، لو احتمل وسعنا ذلك لقلنا ذلك
Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: There is no similarity in affirming names and verifying attributes to negate the realities of what is in creation from Him, like being dead and stable. Rather, the names do not allow definition nor verification of essence by the right of lordship except through that; for there is no way to know an absent except through indication of the witnessed. Then if one intends the attribute of highness and majesty, that is the way of knowledge in the witnessed, and the possibility of speech; for our capacity does not allow recognition by naming with other than what we have witnessed, nor indication to what we do not take from the senses and the truth of sight. If our capacity allowed that, we would say that.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a methodological clarification about how to understand divine attributes. Imam al-Maturidi begins by asserting that affirming God's names and attributes (al-asma' wa al-sifat) does not involve any similarity (tashbih) to creation that would negate the realities of created things — like their being contingent and perishable. Rather, names themselves cannot fully define or verify God's essence in a way that does justice to His lordship except through the method of using terms known from the witnessed world. This is because there is no way to know an unseen (gha'ib) being except through the indication (dalala) of the witnessed (shahid). When we intend to describe God with attributes like highness (al-'ulu) and majesty (al-jalal), that is the path of knowledge in the witnessed world and the possibility of meaningful speech. Our capacity does not permit us to name God with terms we have not witnessed, nor to indicate what we do not perceive through the senses and direct sight. If we had the ability to do so, we would use such terms. The point is that using terms from the created world to describe God does not imply that God is like those created things; it is a necessary linguistic and cognitive limitation for humans. The reality of the attributes is affirmed, but their modality (kayfiyya) is unknown, and any similarity is negated.

Key Terms
الأسماء
الصفات
التشبيه
الشاهد
الغائب
الدلالة
العلو
الجلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using terms like 'highness' for God implies He is like a physical object in space.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that using witnessed terms is a necessary linguistic tool for humans, not a literal comparison. The reality of the attribute is affirmed, but its modality is unknown and similarity is negated.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does affirming attributes of God not imply similarity to creation?
A) Because attributes are purely metaphorical and have no real meaning.
B) Because names and attributes are only for creation, not for God.
C) Because using witnessed terms is the only possible way for humans to know God, but it does not entail likeness.
D) Because we can know God directly without any terms.

Key Takeaway
Affirming divine attributes using terms from the created world is necessary for human understanding and does not imply any similarity between God and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Hearing' or 'Allah is All-Seeing', do you ever unconsciously picture hearing or seeing like your own? How does Maturidi's warning—that we only have such terms from the witnessed world but they do not imply similarity—challenge you to purify your mental image of Allah right now? Write down one concrete step you will take today to remind yourself that His attributes are beyond your imagination.
?
Maturidi says: 'If our capacity allowed, we would say that.' But Allah has not allowed it. Are you genuinely content with the limitation of human language to describe your Lord, or does it frustrate you that you cannot know Him more directly? Let this frustration drive you to seek Him through His revealed names and signs, not through your own speculation. What specific name of Allah will you meditate on tonight to draw closer without falling into tashbih?
?
You speak about Allah every day—in prayer, in conversation, in your thoughts. But Maturidi forces you to recognize that every word you use is borrowed from a world that is not His. Does that make you more cautious in your speech about Him, or more grateful that He allows you to address Him at all? Today, before you say 'Ya Rahman', pause and reflect: this name came from your human experience of mercy, but His mercy is utterly unlike. How will that awareness change your next prayer?

Refutation of Those Who Deny Names and Attributes
Maturidi refutes philosophers who deny names and attributes, showing their contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying divine names and attributes leads to logical contradictions and undermines worship.

Core Idea
Denying divine names makes worship of the unknowable impossible.

Imam Maturidi now turns to refute those who deny that God has names and attributes, particularly the philosophers. He shows that their position, which they claim avoids anthropomorphism, actually makes God unknowable and worship irrational. This passage is crucial for understanding the Islamic stance on divine attributes as necessary for a meaningful relationship with the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice Maturidi's dense style, which requires careful reading.
2
Understand the key distinction
Maturidi distinguishes similarity of essences from similarity of names. Focus on why using the same name does not imply similarity.
This is central to the refutation.
3
Analyze the rhetorical questions
Identify the rhetorical questions Maturidi poses to the philosophers. Consider what each question exposes about their position.
These questions reveal the practical impossibility of denying names.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the philosophers' denial leads to confusion and ignorance. Consider why this is a strong refutation.
The passage ends with a powerful condemnation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنا أردنا به ما يسقط الشبه من قولنا: عالم لا كالعلماء، وهذا النوع في كل ما نسميه به ونصفه، والله الموفق. مع ما كان التشابه الذي تقدره أوهامنا ليس عن قول اللسان نقدره، بل بما كنا نعرف الشبه بين الذاتين والفعلين، فإلى ذلك يرجع، وههنا عند التسمية، وذلك يحققه لو لم يكن لهما اسم عرفا به، ووصف وصفا به. فإذا كان الله سبحانه فيما اعتقدنا وحدانيته اعتقدنا غير شبيه بالمعروفين في تسمية الآحاد لم يلزمنا التسمية بما تعرفنا ما لولا الاسم لم يجب التشبيه بالاسم لذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن من نفى الأسماء والصفات من الفلسفة لم يقل بالتعطيل، وكل مثبت معناه في التحقيق نفى التعطيل، ثم لم يجب به التشابه، فمثله في الأسماء. وإذا لم يحققوا فما يقولون لو قيل لهم: ما تعبدون، وإلى ماذا تدعون، وبأي دين تدينون، ومن أمركم ونهاكم عما تنهون وتأمرون، ومن به بدء العالم العلوي والسفلى، وبمن كان أولية الأشياء؟ ليرجعوا إلى معنى يقرب إلى الفهم أو يلحقوا بمنكري حدث العالم. ويبطلوا قولهم في الأول: إنه العقل أو الأصل السابق أو الروحاني الأول، أو ما قالوا في ذلك، وفي ذلك اختيار الحيرة والتمسك بالجهل ودفع ما يعرف غير العالم به، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
But we intended by it what removes the ambiguity from our statement: 'Knowing but not like the knowers', and this type applies to everything we name Him with and describe Him by, and God is the Guide. With what similarity that our imaginations estimate is not from utterance of the tongue that we estimate it, but by what we know of similarity between two essences (dhatayn) and two actions, to that it returns, and here in the case of naming, and that would be true if there were no name by which they are known and no description by which they are described. So if God, exalted is He, in what we believe of His oneness, we believe Him not similar to the known ones in the naming of individuals, it does not necessitate for us naming with what we know, because if not for the name, similarity by the name would not be necessary, and there is no power except through God. Moreover, those who denied names and attributes from among the philosophers did not assert ta'til (negation of attributes), and every affirmer of meaning in reality negates ta'til, yet similarity does not follow from it, so the same applies to names. And if they do not verify, what would they say if it is said to them: What do you worship? And to what do you call? And with what religion do you profess? And who commanded you and forbade you from what you forbid and command? And from whom is the beginning of the higher and lower world? And from whom is the primacy of things? They should return to a meaning close to understanding or join the deniers of the temporal creation of the world. And they nullify their statement about the First: that it is the intellect, or the prior principle, or the first spiritual, or what they said about that, and in that is a choice of confusion and adherence to ignorance and rejection of what is known other than the world, and there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny divine names and attributes, particularly philosophers. Maturidi begins by clarifying that his previous statement 'Knowing but not like the knowers' is intended to remove any ambiguity of similarity (tashabbuh). He argues that the notion of similarity is not based on linguistic utterance alone but on the actual relationship between two essences or actions. In naming God, we do not imply similarity because the names are applied according to our knowledge, and the mere use of a name does not entail similarity between God and creatures. He then addresses the philosophers who deny names and attributes: they claim not to assert ta'til (negation of attributes), but their denial of names effectively negates meaning. Maturidi poses rhetorical questions: What do they worship? To what do they call? What is their religion? Who commands and forbids? If they cannot answer, they fall into confusion and join the deniers of the world's temporal creation. Their own theories about the First Principle (e.g., intellect, prior principle, first spiritual) are thus invalidated. The passage concludes that their position is a choice of confusion and adherence to ignorance, rejecting what is known beyond the physical world.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying names and attributes is a form of pure tawhid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that denial leads to an ineffable deity and contradictions in worship, proving it is not true tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does naming God with attributes not imply similarity to creatures?

D) Because the philosophers have proven that names are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Denying divine names and attributes leads to an inability to coherently worship or refer to God, undermining the very foundation of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Let this lesson strip away that doubt: if you cannot name Him, you cannot worship Him. Examine the reality of your worship—are your prayers directed to a real Person or a vague force?
?
Maturidi asks of the philosophers: 'What do you worship?'—a question that should pierce every soul. What do you actually worship? Is it the God revealed in His names and attributes, or have you substituted an idol of your own intellect? Force yourself to answer honestly: in your daily life, what takes the place of God as your ultimate concern?

Different Aspects of Evidence from the World
Maturidi enumerates four types of worldly evidence pointing to divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses distinct features of the created world—such as corruption, opposites, and consistency—to prove different attributes of Allah, not just His existence.

Core Idea
Each sign in creation points to a specific divine attribute.

Imam Maturidi (may Allah have mercy on him) teaches that the world is not just a generic proof for God; different features of creation reveal different aspects of His nature. In this passage, he lays out a sophisticated method of drawing evidence from the world's characteristics, showing how everything from its tendency to decay to its internal harmony points to specific attributes like power, wisdom, and unity. Let’s explore how he builds this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage slowly, even if you don't understand every word. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning. Notice the repeated structure of 'دل' (indicates).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the four indications
Work through the passage and find each place where 'دل' appears. There are four main indications. For each one, note what aspect of the world is being described and what attribute it proves.
You can list them in order: temporality, not self-made, unity, and attributes (power, wisdom, knowledge).
3
Understand the principle
The passage opens with a principle: different indications vary according to their evidences. Ask yourself why Maturidi starts this way. How does it set the stage for the rest?
This is key to the methodology.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final sentence says evidence leads to difference, not agreement. Think about what this means: each sign points to a specific attribute, so the evidence is diverse, not all proving the same thing.
This is a unique aspect of Maturidi's approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: والأصل أن دلالات مختلفة على اختلاف دلائله، احتماله الاستحالة والزوال واجتماع الأضداد في عين في حال دال على حدثه. ثم دل جهله بمباديه وعجزه عن إصلاح ما فسد منه أنه لم يكن بنفسه. ثم دل اجتماع الأحوال المتضادة واتساق جواهر الخلق على الاستقامة على أن مدير الكل ومحدثه واحد. ويدل أيضا اتساقه واستقامته وحفظ الأضداد في عين على قدرة مديره وحكمته وعلمه. فاختلفت جهات الدلالة فيها عليه دلالات العيان. فصار دليل إثبات المحدث عجز المحدث، ودليل علمه لما اتسق جهله بنفسه. فصار وجود الدلالة به على الخلاف لا الوفاق.
Shaykh Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle is that different indications (dalālāt) vary according to their evidences. Its possibility of corruption (istiḥāla), perishing (zawāl), and the co-existence of opposites in one entity in a state indicate its temporality (ḥudūth). Then its ignorance of its origins and inability to repair what is corrupted from it indicate that it did not come to be by itself. Then the meeting of opposing states and the consistency of the substances of creation upon rectitude indicate that the Manager (mudabbir) and Originator (muḥdith) of the whole is one. Also its consistency, rectitude, and preservation of opposites in one entity indicate the power (qudra), wisdom (ḥikma), and knowledge (ʿilm) of its Manager. Thus the aspects of indication (jihāt al-dalāla) regarding it varied as sensory evidences (dalālāt al-ʿiyān). So the evidence for establishing the originator became the inability of the originated, and the evidence for His knowledge became its consistency in ignorance of itself. Thus the existence of the indication came to be upon difference, not agreement.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of evidence from the world. The author first states that different indications correspond to different evidences. He then explains that the world's possibility of corruption, perishing, and coexistence of opposites in one entity prove its createdness. Next, its inability to know its own beginning or repair its corruption shows it did not create itself. Furthermore, the harmony of opposing states and the consistency of all created substances point to a single Manager and Originator. Finally, the preservation of opposites within one entity demonstrates the Manager's power, wisdom, and knowledge. The conclusion is that these various aspects of evidence lead to knowledge of different divine attributes, not just existence.

Key Terms
دلالات
جهات الدلالة
حدث
مدبر
قدرة
حكمة
علم
عيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All signs from the world only prove the existence of a creator, not specific attributes.
Correction: Maturidi shows that different features of the world—like corruption, opposites, consistency—point to distinct attributes such as power, wisdom, and unity.

Checkpoint
What does the consistency and preservation of opposites in one entity indicate?
A) The world is eternal
B) The Manager has power, wisdom, and knowledge
C) The world is self-sufficient
D) The Manager is ignorant

Key Takeaway
The world's diverse features provide evidence for distinct divine attributes, not just for God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
