Refuting God as a Natural Cause
Maturidi distinguishes between God as a natural cause and as a true creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects describing God as a 'natural cause' and how he proves God's free will through the world's origination.

Core Idea
God is not a natural cause; He creates by choice, not by compulsion.

Maturidi begins by addressing a common but dangerous mistake: equating God's creative act with natural causation. He first clarifies what is meant by 'cause' — if it implies natural necessity, that is impossible for God. But if it means origination, the term itself is misleading. He then provides three compelling reasons why the world must have been brought into existence by a free agent, not a blind cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a refutation, a clarification, and three proofs.
The Arabic is garbled but the reconstructed meaning is clear.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'cause'
Maturidi distinguishes two interpretations: natural causation (impossible) and origination (correct but improper term). See how he rejects the first and accepts the second with a caveat.
3
Examine the three reasons
The passage gives three arguments for why the world is originated. List them: (1) contradiction of non-existence, (2) totality of the world, (3) coexistence of opposites.
Each reason points to the need for a free Creator.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: if God were a natural cause, He would be forced, which contradicts His power and will. True agency requires choice.
This preserves tawhid and divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله البارى علة العالم إن أراد به كون المصنوع به بالطبع فهو محال؛ لأنه طريق الإضطرار، ومن ذلك وصفه لا يُحتمل به كون العالم أن العالم محدث مختلف، ومر ومن كون الشيء به بالطبع وإن أراد به أنه يحدثه فذلك مستقيم، وتسميته علة فاسدة، وذلك المعنى على كون الشيء بعد أن لم يكن لأوجه، أحدها التناقض، إذ العدم يوجد، فتقع الحاجة إلى من يوجده، فثبت أن في ذلك وجوب كونه حادثا. والثاني كون كلية العالم به، ومعلوم كون الحادث بعد أن لم يكن، والله أعلم. والثالث أن في ذلك وجود الاجتماع مع التفرق، والحركة مع السكون، والحياة وفى ذلك تناقض وتناف، ثبت أنه كان على التابع بالأول والثاني ونحوه، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And his saying: 'The Creator is the cause of the world' – if he means by it that the made is by nature, then it is impossible, because it is the way of compulsion, and from that description it is not possible that the world is originated and different... and what of something being by nature? And if he means that He originates it, then that is correct, but calling Him 'cause' is corrupt. And that meaning (of origination) has aspects: one is contradiction, because non-existence exists, so need arises for one who brings it into existence, so it is established that in that is the necessity of its being originated. The second is that the totality of the world is by Him, and it is known that the originated is after non-existence, and Allah knows best. The third is that in that is the existence of conjunction with separation, and motion with stillness, and life – and in that is contradiction and opposition. It is established that it was in the state of following the first and second and the like, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
Maturidi refutes those who say God is the 'cause' (علة) of the world in a natural sense. If by 'cause' they mean natural necessity (بالطبع), that is impossible because it would imply compulsion and deny God's free will. The world is originated (محدث) and diverse, which cannot come from a natural cause. If they mean God originates it (يحدثه), that is correct, but calling Him 'cause' is a misnomer. He then gives three reasons to prove the world is originated: (1) Contradiction: non-existence would need to exist before origination, requiring an agent. (2) The whole world is from God, and originated means after non-existence. (3) Opposites like conjunction and separation, motion and stillness, life and death exist together, which is contradictory unless they are temporal, proving origination.

Key Terms
علة
طبع
إضطرار
حادث
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God creates by necessity, like a natural cause produces its effect.
Correction: Maturidi shows this would imply compulsion, contradicting God's free will and the diversity of creation. God creates by choice, not by nature.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject calling God a 'cause' (علة) in the natural sense?
A) Because it implies God is forced to create.
B) Because 'cause' is an Arabic term.
C) Because the world is eternal.
D) Because God is not involved.

Key Takeaway
God is not a natural cause; He creates with free will, as proven by the world's origination and the impossibility of compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something goes wrong in your life, do you instinctively feel that God 'had to' let it happen because of natural laws or your past actions? How does Maturidi's rejection of natural causality in God confront that feeling, and what would it mean for you to truly believe that God creates every moment by free will? Will you consciously remind yourself today that nothing in your life happens by compulsion from God's side?
?
You say you believe God is free, but examine your prayers: do you ever pray as if you are turning a key in a lock, expecting a guaranteed result? That is treating God like a natural cause. After this lesson, what specific expectation or assumption about God will you repent of, and how will you replace it with trust in His free and wise will?
?
The passage proves the world is originated from the coexistence of opposites like motion and stillness. Look at the contradictions in your own soul – your desires for good and evil, your faith and doubt. If these prove the world is not eternal but created, do they also prove that you yourself are not a self-made person but a creature in constant need of the free Creator? How will you live today in light of that dependence?

God's Eternal Attributes: Knowledge, Power, Action, Generosity
Maturidi affirms God's eternal attributes and provides an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi affirms God's attributes of knowledge, power, action, and generosity as eternal, using rational validity and the analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
God's attributes are eternal, not dependent on creation.

Maturidi begins by affirming that God has never ceased to be knowing, powerful, acting, and generous. This affirmation is grounded in rational validity and refutes any notion that God's attributes change or are acquired. The passage then provides an analogy from human knowledge and will to illustrate how eternal attributes relate to future creations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key attributes listed and the phrase 'لم يزل'.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the eternal attributes
List the four attributes mentioned: knowledge ('عالم'), power ('قادر'), action ('فاعل'), and generosity ('جواد'). Understand that 'لم يزل' means 'has never ceased'—they are eternal.
3
Understand the rational condition
The attributes are affirmed 'on the manners that are valid in the intellect' (على الوجوه التي تصح في العقل). This means the affirmation is rationally sound.
Maturidi uses rational argumentation.
4
Examine the purpose clause
Read from 'ليكون يفعله' to understand why this is stated: to show that God acts at the proper time and to refute misconceptions about independence or inability.
The phrase 'دفع الوصف بالغنا عن التكوين' means refuting the description of being independent of formation.
5
Analyze the analogy from the witnessed world
Maturidi uses an analogy: just as our knowledge and will can relate to future things that do not yet exist, God's power, will, and generosity are similarly eternal and relate to future creations.
The analogy shows that attributes can exist before their objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ونحن نقول بأنه عز وجل لم يزل عالما قادرا فاعلا جوادا على الوجوه التي تصح في العقل، ويقوم التدبيرا - إنه لم يزل كذلك. ليكون يفعله كل شيء يكون، في وقت كونه، بوجه يصح عنه دفع الوصف بالغنا عن التكوين، والامتناع عن وقوع القدرة عليه، والغينا بنفسه في الوجود عن الباري، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وذلك معلوم في الشاهد في العلم والإرادة بأشياء ليست بكائنة لتكون، فمثله عندنا القدرة والإرادة والجود وما ذكر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

So that He does every thing that comes to be, at the time of its coming to be, in a manner that is valid from Him, repelling the description of being independent of formation, and of prevention of power occurring upon it, and of self-sufficiency in existence from the Creator, and there is no power except with Allah. And that is known in the witnessed realm concerning knowledge and will regarding things that are not yet existent so that they may come to be. Similarly with us are power, will, generosity, and what has been mentioned, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
Maturidi affirms that God is eternally (لم يزل) characterized by knowledge, power, action, and generosity. These attributes are valid in reason. God's action is not dependent on the existence of things; He creates each thing at its proper time. This refutes the notion that God is independent (غني) of creation in the sense of being unable to create, or that power cannot act upon things. In the witnessed world, we know that knowledge and will can be about things that do not yet exist; likewise, God's power, will, and generosity are eternal and relate to future creations.

Why God Is Not a 'Cause' in the Natural Sense
Maturidi analyzes the term 'illa (cause) as applied to God and refutes misunderstandings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the term 'cause' for God unless it means volitional creation, and how three proofs show the world's contingency.

Core Idea
God creates by will, not by natural necessity.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid examines a common philosophical error: applying the term 'cause' (illa) to God as if it implies natural compulsion. Maturidi clarifies that if 'cause' means natural necessity, it is impossible, but if it means volitional creation, the meaning is correct even if the term is inappropriate. He then provides three arguments demonstrating the world's contingency, reinforcing that it depends on a Creator with will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the two conditional statements at the beginning and the three numbered aspects that follow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'cause'
Find where Maturidi says 'if by that he means...' Trace the two possibilities and their judgments: natural compulsion is impossible, volitional creation is correct but the term is corrupt.
3
Examine the three aspects
The passage lists three aspects (one of them... the second... the third). For each, understand how it proves the world's contingency and need for a Creator.
Use the commentary and guided breakdown for help.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final phrase 'ثبت أنه كان على التابع بالأول والثاني ونحوه' indicates the world is subject to the first and second principles. Consider what this means for the relationship between God and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وقوله البارى علة العالم إن أراد به كون المصنوع به بالطبع فهو محال؛ لأنه طريق الإضطرار، ومن ذلك وصفه لا يحتمل به كون العالم أن العالم محدث مختلف، ومر ومن كون الشيء به بالطبع وإن أراد به أنه يحدثه فذلك مستقيم، وتسميته علة فاسدة، وذلك المعنى على كون الشيء بعد أن لم يكن لأوجه أحدها التناقض، إذ العدم يوجد، فتقع الحاجة إلى من يوجده، فثبت أن في ذلك وجوب كونه حادثا. والثاني كون كلية العالم به، ومعلوم كون الحادث بعد أن لم يكن، والله. والثالث أن في ذلك وجود الاجتماع مع التفرق، والحركة مع السكون، والحياة وفي ذلك تناقض وتناف، ثبت أنه كان على التابع بالأول والثاني ونحوه، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: And His saying that the Creator is the cause of the world—if by that he means the made thing is by nature, then it is impossible, because it is the way of compulsion, and from that His description does not bear that the world is created differently; but if by that he means that He creates it, then that is correct, but naming it 'cause' is corrupt. And that meaning (of 'cause') regarding the thing coming to be after not being has aspects: one of them is contradiction, because non-existence exists, so need arises for one who brings it into existence, so it is established that in that is the necessity of its being created. The second is that the entirety of the world is by Him, and it is known that the created thing comes to be after not being, and [by] God. The third is that in that is the existence of combination with separation, movement with rest, and life, and in that is contradiction and inconsistency; it is established that it was upon the follower of the first and the second and the like. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage is a theological analysis of the term 'cause' (ʿilla) when applied to God. The author (Shaykh, likely referring to a predecessor) considers two possible meanings. If one means that God produces the world by natural necessity (bil-ṭabʿ), that is impossible because it implies compulsion (iḍṭirār) and removes divine volition. If one means that God creates it by His will, that is correct, but using the word 'cause' is inappropriate. Then the author lists three aspects of the mistaken understanding of 'cause' as applied to creation ex nihilo: (1) contradiction: non-existence would need to exist to cause something, so it requires an external agent, showing the world must be created; (2) the entire world is from God, and the created comes from non-existence; (3) the coexistence of opposites like separation and combination in the world shows its contingency, leading to the conclusion that the world is dependent on a creator. The phrase 'على التابع بالأول والثاني' likely means the world is subject to the first and second principles (i.e., the proof of createdness).

Key Terms
البارى
علة
بالطبع
اضطرار
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God creates by natural compulsion like a cause in nature
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'cause' implies necessity and compulsion, which cannot apply to God's voluntary creation. God creates by will, not by nature.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the problem with calling God a 'cause' (ʿilla) if it means natural compulsion?
A) It implies God is forced to create
B) It denies God's power
C) It suggests the world is eternal
D) It contradicts the Quran

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that God's creation is voluntary, not by natural necessity, and the world's contingency demonstrates its dependence on a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God's actions as automatic or predictable, like a natural law? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for your trust in His personal will for your life today?
?
If every event in your life is a direct act of God's will, not a chain of random causes, then why do you often attribute outcomes to mere 'luck' or 'nature'? What would you need to change in your daily awareness to truly live in light of this truth?
?
The passage says calling God a 'cause' is corrupt because it implies compulsion. Are there areas in your life where you treat God as if He is constrained by your expectations or by the 'laws' of the universe? How will you repent from this and submit to His absolute will?

God's Eternal Attributes and Their Relation to the Future
Maturidi affirms God's eternal attributes and relates them to future creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi affirms that God's attributes are eternal and can relate to things that do not yet exist, using an analogy from human knowledge and will.

Core Idea
God's attributes are eternal and active, even before their objects exist.

Maturidi begins by firmly stating that God has always been knowing, powerful, acting, and generous in a rationally sound manner. This might raise a puzzle: if everything is created in time, how can God's attributes relate to things before they exist? Maturidi answers by drawing a simple but profound analogy from our own experience. This passage establishes the foundation for understanding God's timeless attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read and absorb the core affirmation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the key terms 'لم يزل' and the list of attributes (knowing, powerful, acting, generous).
2
Understand the theological purpose
Notice that Maturidi connects these attributes to God's creative act, refuting misconceptions about God's independence from creation or any limitation on His power.
The phrase 'يقوم التدبيرا' indicates God's ongoing governance.
3
Analyze the analogy from human experience
Maturidi uses the example of human knowledge and will for future events to explain how God's attributes relate to non-existent things. Reflect on how this strengthens the doctrine of eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ونحن نقول بأنه عز وجل لم يزل عالما قادرا فاعلا جوادا على الوجوه التي تصح في العقل، ويقوم التدبيرا - إنه لم يزل كذلك. ليكون يفعله كل شيء يكون، في وقت كونه، بوجه يصح عنه دفع الوصف بالغنا عن التكوين، والامتناع عن وقوع القدرة عليه، والغينا بنفسه في الوجود عن الباري، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وذلك معلوم في الشاهد في العلم والإرادة بأشياء ليست بكائنة لتكون، فمثله عندنا القدرة والإرادة والجود وما ذكر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: And we say that He, mighty and majestic, has never ceased to be knowing, powerful, acting, generous in the ways that are correct in reason, and the management [of affairs] is established—indeed He has never ceased to be so. So that He does every thing that comes to be, at the time of its coming to be, in a way that is valid from Him, repelling the description of being free from need of bringing into existence, and the prevention of power occurring upon it, and [repelling] our being independent in existence from the Creator. And there is no power except through God. And that is known in the witnessed [world] regarding knowledge and will for things that are not yet existent so that they may be; so similarly for us are power, will, generosity, and what is mentioned. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage is a creedal statement about God's eternal attributes. Abu Mansur (Imam Maturidi) affirms that God has always been (lam yazal) knowing, powerful, acting, and generous in ways that are rationally sound. The phrase 'يقوم التدبيرا' (management is established) indicates that God's governance of affairs is ongoing. The purpose of these attributes is that God brings everything into existence at its proper time, in a way that refutes any suggestion that God is too self-sufficient to create, or that any power could prevent His actions, or that anything could be independent of Him. Maturidi then draws an analogy from the witnessed realm: humans have knowledge and will for future things, so likewise God's power, will, and generosity relate to things before they exist. This supports the eternity of attributes despite the temporality of their objects.

Key Terms
لم يزل
عالما
قادرا
فاعلا
جوادا
التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes must have their objects existing co-eternally with Him, otherwise the attribute would be meaningless.
Correction: Maturidi shows that attributes like knowledge and will can relate to future, non-existent things, as seen in human experience. God's eternal attributes are no different.

Checkpoint

B) The existence of multiple worlds
C) The nature of light and darkness
D) The concept of causality in nature

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that God eternally possesses attributes like knowledge and power, which relate to future creation without needing the objects to exist alongside them.

?
Maturidi argues that God's attributes are eternally active—He is always acting, even before creation. How does this change the way you perceive moments when you feel God is 'silent' in your life? Will you trust that He is still working?

Answering the Objection of Evil from a Wise God — Defining Wisdom
Maturidi answers the objection that a single wise agent cannot produce foolish acts, and defines wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to the objection that a wise God cannot produce foolish acts, and his definition of wisdom as placing things in their proper place.

Core Idea
What seems foolish may be a wisdom beyond human grasp.

Maturidi addresses a critical objection: if God is wise, why does evil exist? He argues that what appears foolish may actually be an act of wisdom beyond human intellect. He then defines wisdom itself as correctness in placing everything in its proper place and giving each its due.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the objection and response unfold naturally.
Notice the structure: objection, distinction, transcendence, definition, cause of denial.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The dualist challenges: how can a wise agent produce foolish acts? Maturidi distinguishes between essential wisdom and ignorance, then asserts God's transcendence.
The answer does not deny the existence of apparent evil; it reframes it as hidden wisdom.
3
Understand the definition of wisdom
Focus on the definition: wisdom is correctness in placing everything in its proper place, giving each its due. This becomes the standard for evaluating divine actions.
Consider how this definition resolves the objection: what seems foolish might be wise in a broader context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائلهم : كيف زعمتم أنه يجوز أن يكون من الحكيم يجيء فعل السفه ؟ قلنا : هذا لا يجى ممن هو حكيم بذاته، إنما يجي ممن يجهل ، كما قلتم في النور من الجهل بعمل الظلمة ونحو ذلك . فأما الله سبحانه يتعالى عن ذلك . لكن قد يجوز أن يكون فعل حكمة لا يبلغها عقل البشر، وإلا فهو يجل عن " ذلك . وما الحكمة إلا الإصابة (فى أن يوضع كل شيء موضعه ، ويعطى كل ذي حظ حظه ، ولا يبخس بأحد حقه. وإنما أبى من يظن بالله أو بما يضيف إليه الموحدون ذلك ؛ لجهلهم بخدود الحكمة ومبلغ المحظوظ ، وايجابهم الحقوق لمن ليست لهم . وسنذكره إن شاء الله في موضع أملك به من هذا
For if their questioner says: How do you claim that it is possible that from the wise an act of foolishness comes? We say: This does not come from one who is wise in his essence, it only comes from one who is ignorant, as you say regarding light of ignorance of the action of darkness and so forth. As for God, exalted is He above that. But it may be that it is an act of wisdom that the intellect of humans does not attain, otherwise He is exalted above that. And wisdom is nothing but correctness in placing every thing in its proper place, and giving everyone their due portion, and not defrauding anyone of their right. And only he refuses who thinks about God or what the monotheists attribute to Him that; due to their ignorance of the boundaries of wisdom and the extent of the fortunate, and their requiring rights for those who do not have them. And we will mention it, God willing, in a place more appropriate than this.
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a definition of wisdom. The dualist objects: how can a single wise agent (God) produce acts that appear foolish (evil)? The answer distinguishes: such acts cannot come from One who is essentially wise, but only from one who is ignorant. However, God is transcendent above ignorance. What appears foolish may actually be an act of wisdom that human intellect cannot grasp. The author then defines wisdom: it is correctness in placing everything in its proper place, giving each their due, and not depriving anyone. The refusal to attribute such acts to God stems from ignorance about the true scope of wisdom and the rights of beings. The author promises to discuss this further elsewhere.

Key Terms
الحكيم
السفه
الحكمة
الإصابة
الموضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God produces acts that appear foolish or evil, then God must not be truly wise or good.
Correction: Maturidi explains that such acts are not from ignorance but from a wisdom beyond human intellect, and true wisdom is placing things correctly, which may not be immediately apparent.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of wisdom given in this passage?
A) Knowing what is best for everyone.
B) Correctness in placing everything in its proper place and giving each their due.
C) The ability to create both good and evil.
D) Understanding the difference between light and darkness.

Key Takeaway
Wisdom consists in placing everything in its proper place and giving each its due; apparent evil may be a wisdom humans cannot grasp.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson says that refusing to attribute wisdom to God comes from ignorance of the boundaries of wisdom. In what areas of your life do you find yourself insisting that you know what is wise for you, and how might you be overstepping those boundaries?
?
'Wisdom is giving everyone their due.' Who in your life have you judged as not deserving of something, perhaps assuming you know their due better than God? How can you surrender that judgment and trust God's allocation, even when it is painful?

The First Meaning of 'Body' and Why It Cannot Apply to Allah
Maturidi defines 'body' in the witnessed world and argues it cannot be applied to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first meaning of 'body' in the witnessed world and why attributing it to Allah leads to problematic implications of composition and contingency.

Core Idea
Allah is not a body because bodies are defined by parts, limits, and direction.

Imam Maturidi begins by defining what 'body' means in the created world: it refers to something with directions, endpoints, or three dimensions. These features necessarily imply parts and limits, which are signs of contingency. Since Allah is not contingent and nothing is like Him, applying such a term would reduce Him to the level of created things. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own argument unfold.
Notice that Maturidi first states the issue and then presents the first meaning.
2
Identify the definition of 'body' in the witnessed world
Maturidi gives three characteristics: having directions, implying extremities, or having three dimensions. List them and note why they all point to createdness.
These characteristics are all signs of physical, contingent beings.
3
Understand why this definition implies contingency
Maturidi argues that parts and limits are signs of contingency. Since Allah is not contingent, the term 'body' cannot apply. Reflect on the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like Him.'
The argument moves from definition to implication to proof from revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: لا يجوز إطلاق لفظ « الجسم » على الله تعالى. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم القول بالجسم يخرج على وجهين: أحدهما في مائية الجسم في الشاهد أنه اسم ذي الجهات، أو اسم محتمل النهايات، أو اسم ذي الأبعاد الثلاثة، فغير جائز القول به في الله سبحانه على أدلة الخلق وإمارة الحدث؛ إذ ذلك معنى الأجزاء والحدود التي من آيات الحدث، وقد بينا أن ليس كمثله شيء، وفي ذلك إيجاب جعله كأكثر الأشياء. وإن كان على التسمية به بلا تحقيق ما ذكرنا خرج الإسم عن المعروف به، فبطل تعرف ذلك من جهة العقل والاستدلال. وحقه السمع عن الله، إن الجسم من أسمائه، ولم يرد عنه ولا عن أذن لأحد تقليده، فالقول به لا يستعب ولو وضع بالجزاف من غير دليل حسي أو سمعي أو عقلي لوسع القول بالجسد والشخص، وكل ذلك مستنكر بالسمع، وليسع القول بكل ما بستی به الخلق، وذلك فاسد. وثانيها أن يكون الجسم ليست له مائية تعرف سوى الإثبات، فيجوز القبول به لو لم يراد به غيره، لكنه لا أحد يجعل الجسم من أسماء الإثبات؛ إذ لا يُسمى به الأعراض والصفات على احتمالها اسم الإثبات؛ لذلك بطل القول به.
Issue: It is not permissible to apply the term 'body' to Allah, the Exalted. Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then the statement concerning 'body' comes out in two aspects: One of them is regarding the essence of body in the witnessed world – that it is a name for that which has directions, or a name that implies extremities, or a name for that which has three dimensions. So it is not permissible to use it for Allah, the Glorified, based on the indications of creation and the mark of contingency; because that entails the meaning of parts and limits which are among the signs of contingency, and we have already shown that 'there is nothing like Him' (Quran 42:11), and in that is requiring making Him like most things. And if it is upon naming without verifying what we mentioned, the name deviates from what is known by it, so the knowledge of that by means of reason and inference is invalidated. And its right is from revelation concerning Allah: if 'body' were among His names, it has not been reported from Him nor has anyone been permitted to follow it; so speaking by it is not allowed, and even if it were used without deliberation without a sensory, revealed, or rational proof, it would allow speaking of 'corporeality' and 'personality', and all that is rejected by revelation, and it would allow speaking by everything by which creation is described, and that is corrupt. And the second (aspect) is that 'body' has no essence known except affirmation; so acceptance of it would be permissible if nothing else were intended by it, but no one makes 'body' among the names of affirmation; for accidents and attributes are not called by it even though they admit the name of affirmation; therefore speaking by it is invalidated.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. Imam Maturidi addresses the issue of applying the term 'body' (jism) to Allah. He presents two possible meanings of 'body' and demonstrates that both are inapplicable. The first meaning considers 'body' in the witnessed world: it refers to something with directions, endpoints, or three dimensions. Such a definition implies composition (ajzā') and limits (ḥudūd), which are signs of contingency (ḥadath). Since Allah is not contingent and nothing is like Him, applying such a term would make Him like created things. If one uses the term without this detailed meaning, the name becomes unrecognizable and cannot be understood rationally. Moreover, if 'body' were a divine name, it would have to be revealed, but no such revelation exists; thus using it is baseless. The second meaning treats 'body' as a pure affirmation of existence without further specification. While this might be harmless, in practice no one uses 'body' in that way for accidents or attributes, which also have existence. Therefore, the term 'body' is rejected for Allah on both counts.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
مائية (māhiyya)
جهات (jihāt)
حدث (ḥadath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling Allah a body is a harmless verbal issue with no doctrinal consequences.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the term 'body' necessarily implies parts and limits, which are signs of contingency, thereby making Allah like creation. It is not harmless.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what is the main reason that the first meaning of 'body' cannot apply to Allah?
A) The word 'body' does not appear in the Quran
B) The definition implies parts and limits, which are signs of contingency
C) The Prophet forbade the use of the term
D) The term is ambiguous and unclear

Key Takeaway
The term 'body' in its first meaning implies parts and limits, which are incompatible with Allah's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself picturing Allah as if He has a form or direction, even if only vaguely? What does Maturidi's argument reveal about the danger of such mental images? Will you consciously replace them with the recognition that He is beyond all limits?
?
When you say 'Allah is not a body,' is it just a phrase you repeat, or does it actually govern how you think of Him in your daily life? In what areas—prayer, reliance, fear—do you still treat Allah as limited like a creature? Commit to one specific way you will remind yourself of His absolute transcendence this week.
?
Maturidi warns against speaking about Allah without verification from revelation or reason. Where in your own speech or beliefs have you said something about Allah without clear evidence? Write down one such statement you have made or heard, and then search for the Quranic or rational proof that corrects it. Repent for it.

The Second Meaning of 'Body' and Its Inapplicability to Allah
Maturidi examines the second possible meaning of 'body' and concludes it is also invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second meaning of 'body' as a mere affirmation of existence and why even that cannot be applied to Allah.

Core Idea
Even if 'body' only means existence, it is not used that way, and Allah's attributes are not bodies.

Imam Maturidi considers a second possible meaning of 'body': that it has no defined essence other than simple affirmation of existence. In this case, one might accept the term if nothing else is intended. However, Maturidi points out that no one actually uses 'body' in this bare sense for accidents or attributes, which also exist. Therefore, applying it to Allah remains invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Maturidi presents a hypothetical scenario.
The key move is that even if 'body' meant mere existence, it is not used that way in practice.
2
Understand the second meaning
The second meaning treats 'body' as having no essence other than existence. Maturidi says this would be acceptable in theory if nothing else is meant.
But the term 'body' is never used this way for other existents.
3
See why the second meaning fails
Maturidi argues that no one calls accidents or attributes 'bodies', even though they are also existent. Thus, the term 'body' is not a generic name for existence, and it cannot be used for Allah.
The argument is based on linguistic usage and the specific connotations of the word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: لا يجوز إطلاق لفظ « الجسم » على الله تعالى. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم القول بالجسم يخرج على وجهين: أحدهما في مائية الجسم في الشاهد أنه اسم ذي الجهات، أو اسم محتمل النهايات، أو اسم ذي الأبعاد الثلاثة، فغير جائز القول به في الله سبحانه على أدلة الخلق وإمارة الحدث؛ إذ ذلك معنى الأجزاء والحدود التي من آيات الحدث، وقد بينا أن ليس كمثله شيء، وفي ذلك إيجاب جعله كأكثر الأشياء. وإن كان على التسمية به بلا تحقيق ما ذكرنا خرج الإسم عن المعروف به، فبطل تعرف ذلك من جهة العقل والاستدلال. وحقه السمع عن الله، إن الجسم من أسمائه، ولم يرد عنه ولا عن أذن لأحد تقليده، فالقول به لا يستعب ولو وضع بالجزاف من غير دليل حسي أو سمعي أو عقلي لوسع القول بالجسد والشخص، وكل ذلك مستنكر بالسمع، وليسع القول بكل ما بستی به الخلق، وذلك فاسد. وثانيها أن يكون الجسم ليست له مائية تعرف سوى الإثبات، فيجوز القبول به لو لم يراد به غيره، لكنه لا أحد يجعل الجسم من أسماء الإثبات؛ إذ لا يُسمى به الأعراض والصفات على احتمالها اسم الإثبات؛ لذلك بطل القول به.

Responding to the Objection from the Names 'Agent' and 'Knower'
Imam Maturidi answers the objection that we use 'Agent' and 'Knower' for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi’s twofold response to the objection that if we reject ‘body’ for Allah, we should also reject names like ‘Agent’ and ‘Knower’.

Core Idea
Revelation and meaning without contingency justify divine names.

When we say Allah is not a body, an objector might ask why we call Him 'Agent' and 'Knower' when those terms also apply to creatures. Imam Maturidi provides two clear answers. This lesson unpacks his reasoning, showing how revelation and conceptual purity allow us to affirm these names while negating any likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answers
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by two answers marked 'the first' and 'the second'.
2
Understand the first answer: reliance on revelation
The first answer says that we only use names for Allah that are established in revelation (samʿ). The name 'Agent' is revealed, whereas 'body' is not.
This shows revelation is the criterion, not rational analogy.
3
Understand the second answer: meaning without contingency
The second answer argues that the meanings of 'Agent' and 'Knower' in the witnessed world do not necessarily imply contingency; after negating likeness, they can be applied to Allah.
In contrast, 'body' implies composition and contingency.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why this distinction is crucial for Islamic theology: it guards against both anthropomorphism and excessive negation.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عورضنا باسم الفاعل أو العاليم ونحو ذلك قيل له جوابان: أحدهما أنا لو لم تعمل معنى هذا لكان يجوز النسبة به بما ثبت في السمع ولم يثبت في الأول؛ لذلك اختلفا. والثاني أن معنى الفاعل والعالم كان معقولا في الشاهد، وليس ذلك أدلة من الحدث، ولا مما في المعروف من معناه دليله وقد احتمل وصف الله به؛ لذلك لزم القول به على نفي الشبه - شبه الخلق عنه، وبالله التوفيق.
If we are objected to by the names 'al-Fa'il' (the Agent) or 'al-'Alim' (the Knowing) and the like, it is said to him: Two answers: One of them is that if we did not work with the meaning of this, it would be permissible to attribute to Him by what is established in revelation, and it is not established in the first; therefore they differ. And the second is that the meaning of 'al-Fa'il' and 'al-'Alim' was intelligible in the witnessed world, and that is not an indication of contingency, nor is there in what is known of its meaning a proof of that, and it is possible to describe Allah by it; therefore it is necessary to speak by it upon negating likeness—likeness of creation from Him. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues: if we reject 'body' for Allah, why do we accept names like 'Agent' (al-Fāʿil) and 'Knower' (al-ʿĀlim), which are also used for contingent beings? Imam Maturidi gives two answers. First, the permission to use a name for Allah depends on revelation: we only use names that are established in the revealed law (samʿ). The names 'Agent' and 'Knower' are revealed, whereas 'body' is not. Second, the meanings of 'Agent' and 'Knower' in the witnessed world do not inherently imply contingency (ḥadath); their meanings are compatible with describing Allah after negating any likeness to creation. In contrast, the meaning of 'body' necessarily implies composition and contingency. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
فاعل (fāʿil)
عالم (ʿālim)
شبه (shabah)
سمع (samʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any name applied to creatures can be applied to Allah if its meaning is intelligible.
Correction: Maturidi shows that revelation and absence of contingency are required; mere intelligibility is not sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the first answer Imam Maturidi gives to the objection about the names 'Agent' and 'Knower'?
A) These names are not found in the Quran
B) The meaning of these names is not intelligible in the witnessed world
C) We only use names that are established in revelation
D) These names imply contingency

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi shows that divine names must be either revealed or, if from the witnessed world, not imply contingency and be used after negating likeness to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you realize that your understanding of Allah's names must be guided by revelation and not by your own assumptions? Have you ever used a name for Allah without checking if it is established in the Qur'an and Sunnah? What will you do to ensure you only describe Allah as He has described Himself?

When you hear the name 'al-Alim' or 'al-Knower', do you struggle to negate any likeness to created knowledge? Does your heart affirm that Allah's knowledge is unlike any knowledge you have ever experienced? Pray that Allah purifies your understanding of His attributes from any trace of shabah.
?
The objection in this lesson is that because we use 'Agent' and 'Knower' for creatures, we should also be able to use 'body' for Allah. But the answer shows that these attributes do not imply contingency. Have you ever felt that because you have attributes like power and knowledge, Allah must have them in a similar way? How can you train yourself to always negate likeness when describing Allah?

Refuting Agency Implies Corporeality
Maturidi refutes the objection that agency implies corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical fallacy in claiming that being an agent (fāʿil) necessarily implies being a body, and how Maturidi refutes it using the witnessed-world definition of 'body'.

Core Idea
Agency does not entail corporeality, because 'body' in the witnessed world means having parts, which does not apply to Allah.

The objector argues that since every agent in the witnessed world is a body, Allah must also be a body if He is called an agent. Imam Maturidi dismantles this by showing that the term 'body' has a precise meaning—composition and divisibility—which cannot be attributed to Allah. He also warns that accepting such analogies leads to absurdities like attributing color or taste to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objector claims that calling Allah 'Agent' implies He is a body, analogizing from the witnessed world. Notice the challenge: 'Why do you not say... because no one in the witnessed world is called by that except that he is a body?'
This is a common anthropomorphic argument from analogy.
2
Examine Maturidi's first refutation
Maturidi points out that not every body in the witnessed world is called 'body' just because it is an agent. The mere existence of bodies that are agents does not make agency a sign of corporeality.
He says: 'we exist as bodies that are not called body' – so the category is not universal.
3
Understand the criteria for naming Allah
Maturidi reminds that divine names are based on revelation (samʿ) and reasoned consideration (ʿibra), not on crude analogies. The objector's argument lacks these criteria.
This distinguishes theological method from empirical generalization.
4
Analyze the definition of 'body'
Maturidi argues that in the witnessed world, 'body' applies only to things that can be divided into parts (tajziʾa and tabʿīḍ), like 'long', 'wide', and 'composite'. Allah does not admit such division.
This shows that 'body' is not a simple label but implies composition.
5
Consider the reductio ad absurdum
If we accepted 'body' for Allah, we would have to accept other physical descriptions like 'long', 'color', 'taste'—which are clearly false. Thus the objection fails.
This exposes the slippery slope of anthropomorphic thinking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم لا قلت بأنه بما سمى به فاعلا كان جسمها، وكذلك القادر و العالم؛ إذ لا أحد في الشاهد سمي به إلا وهو جسم؟ قيل: لا سمي بذلك في الشاهد لأنه لوجودنا أجساما لا تسمى جسم فلذلك لم يلزم به القول. على أنا بينا الوجوه التي أحقت التسمية بما سمى من السمع والعبرة، ولسنا نجد ذلك في الذي عارض به، ولو جاز لنا ليجوز الآخر أيضا أن يقابلنا بمثله في الجسد والشخص ونحو ذلك مع ما كان اسم الجسم غير واقع في الشاهد على ما لا يحتمل التجزئة والتبعغيض من نحو العرض والفعل والحركة والسكون، ثبت أنه اسم ذي الأجزاء كالطويل والعريض والمؤلف، ولو لم يبطل القول بالمؤلف لما يدل ظاهره على فعل به، إذ لو بطل ليبطل القول بموجود بذاته في الأزل، ولو كان كذلك ليجوز القول بطويل وجسد ولون وطعم ونحو ذلك؛ لما ليس الظاهر إلا ذلك، فإذ لم يجز لما في الحقيقة إيجابه وإن لم يكن في اللفظ دليله فمثله في الجسم، والله الموفق.
If it is said: Why do you not say that because He is called 'Fa'il' He is a body, and likewise 'al-Qadir' (the Powerful) and 'al-'Alim'; since no one in the witnessed world is called by that except that he is a body? It is said: He is not called by that in the witnessed world because we exist as bodies that are not called 'body', therefore it does not necessitate speaking by it. Moreover, we have shown the aspects that make the naming valid by what is named from revelation and consideration, and we do not find that in what he objects with. And if it were permissible for us, it would also be permissible for another to face us with the like of 'corporeality' and 'personality' and so on, given that the name 'body' does not occur in the witnessed world for that which does not admit division and partition, like accident, act, motion, and rest; thus it is established that it is a name for that which has parts, like 'long', 'wide', and 'composite'. Even if the statement about 'composite' is not invalidated because its apparent meaning indicates an action upon it, if it were invalidated, it would invalidate the statement about an existent by itself in pre-eternity, and if it were so, it would permit speaking of 'long', 'body', 'color', 'taste', and so on, since the apparent is only that. Therefore, it is not permissible because in reality it necessitates that, even if there is no proof of it in the wording. And the like applies to 'body'. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objector persists: if we accept that Allah is called 'Agent' (Fāʿil), and in the witnessed world every agent is a body, then Allah must be a body. Maturidi responds by pointing out that not every body in the witnessed world is called a 'body'—we exist as bodies but are not called by that term simply because we are agents. Moreover, the criteria for naming Allah are based on revelation (samʿ) and reasoned consideration (ʿibra), which do not support 'body'. He then argues that the term 'body' in the witnessed world applies only to things that can be divided and have parts (tajziʾa and tabʿīḍ), such as 'long', 'wide', and 'composite'. If we allowed 'body' for Allah, we would have to allow other physical descriptions like 'long' or 'color', which are clearly false. The argument about 'composite' (muʾallaf) illustrates that if we accept something composite about Allah, it would lead to absurdities like describing Him as 'long' or 'having taste'. Since these are impossible, 'body' is likewise impossible. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
تجزئة (tajziʾa)
تبعيض (tabʿīḍ)
مؤلف (muʾallaf)
فاعل (fāʿil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being an agent (fāʿil) logically implies being a body because all agents in the witnessed world are bodies.
Correction: Maturidi shows that agency does not entail corporeality because 'body' in the witnessed world is defined by composition and divisibility, not by agency. Moreover, divine names are not based on empirical analogy but on revelation and reasoned consideration.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, the name 'body' (jism) in the witnessed world applies only to things that:
A) Are eternal
B) Have no attributes
C) Are composed of parts
D) Are spiritual

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the claim that agency implies corporeality by defining 'body' as composed of parts, which cannot apply to the indivisible Creator.

How does Maturidi's definition of 'body' as having parts challenge that mental image? What specific thought can you practice today to remind yourself that Allah is beyond all physical attributes?
?
The objector relied on analogy from the witnessed world to assume Allah must be like created beings. In what areas of your life do you find yourself making similar assumptions about Allah based on your own human experience? What is one concrete step you can take today to replace that assumption with the teaching that 'there is nothing like unto Him'?
?
Maturidi argues that if we allow one term like 'body' for Allah, it opens the door to other absurd attributes like 'color' and 'taste'. Are there any attributes or descriptions of Allah that you have casually accepted without considering their implications? How can you guard your heart from slipping into comparisons that diminish His transcendence?

The First Answer: Deriving Meaning from Names
First answer to the objection about divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first answer to the objection that divine names like al-Fā‘il and al-‘Ālim imply anthropomorphism: if meaning is not derived from the name itself, then we rely solely on revelation.

Core Idea
If meaning is not derived, only revelation guides.

When opponents argue that calling Allah al-Fā‘il or al-‘Ālim implies He resembles creation, Maturidi offers two distinct responses. The first answer focuses on how we understand these names: either we derive their meaning from linguistic roots and apply it to Allah, or we do not. If we do not derive meaning, then we can only rely on revelation (sam‘) to know that Allah is called by these names. This first answer establishes that deriving meaning distinguishes reason-based knowledge from mere scriptural acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage for the first answer aloud, then read its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if we did not derive meaning... then it would be permissible...'
The passage begins with the objection and then gives the first answer.
2
Identify the logical condition
The answer hinges on the conditional: if we do not derive meaning (lam na‘mal ma‘nā), then we are left only with what revelation (sam‘) establishes. This shows that deriving meaning is what allows reason to engage.
The phrase 'ma‘nā' refers to the underlying concept or significance.
3
Understand the contrast between the two approaches
Maturidi says that if we do not derive meaning, then we could only attribute names based on revelation, and that would differ from the first principle (perhaps rational derivation). This contrast shows the importance of linguistic and rational analysis.
'al-awwal' likely refers to the original or primary method of deriving meaning.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Reflect on why Maturidi gives this answer first: it establishes that the meaning of names is not arbitrary but rooted in language, and that rational derivation is valid even for divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عورضنا باسم الفاعل أو العالم ونحو ذلك قيل له جوابان: أحدهما أنا لو لم تعمل معنى هذا لكان يجوز النسبة به بما ثبت في السمع ولم يثبت في الأول؛ لذلك اختلفا. والثاني أن معنى الفاعل والعالم كان معقولا في الشاهد، وليس ذلك أدلة من الحدث، ولا مما في المعروف من معناه دليله، وقد احتمل وصف الله به؛ لذلك لزم القول به على نفي الشبه. الشبه تشبيه الخلق عنه، وبالله التوفيق.
If we are opposed with the name al-Fā‘il or al-‘Ālim and similar, it is said to him: two answers: one is that if we did not derive meaning from this, it would be permissible to attribute by what is established in revelation and not established in the first [principle]; therefore they differ. And the second is that the meaning of al-Fā‘il and al-‘Ālim was understood in the witnessed world, but that is not evidence of createdness, nor is there in what is known of its meaning a proof, and it is possible to describe Allah with it; therefore the statement is necessary while negating likeness. Likeness is likening creation to Him. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that using names like al-Fā‘il and al-‘Ālim for Allah implies that He is a body, because in the witnessed world only bodies are called by these names. Maturidi gives two answers. First: if we did not derive the meaning of these names from their linguistic root, we could only rely on revelation (sam‘) to know that Allah is called by them, and this would not be based on reason. But we do derive meaning, so the two approaches differ. Second: the meaning of these names is known in the witnessed world (shāhid), but that does not prove that Allah is created (ḥadath). Since describing Allah with these names is possible, we must affirm them while negating any likeness (tashbīh) between Allah and creation. The phrase 'negating likeness' means denying that Allah resembles His creatures.

Key Terms
الفاعل (al-Fā‘il)
العالم (al-‘Ālim)
السمع (al-sam‘)
المعنى (al-ma‘nā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we derive the meaning of divine names from language, it necessarily leads to anthropomorphism.
Correction: Maturidi shows that deriving meaning does not force corporeality; it simply allows reason to engage without requiring physical similarity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's first answer, what would happen if we did not derive meaning from the name al-Fā‘il?
A) We would be forbidden from using the name.
B) We would rely only on revelation to attribute the name.
C) We would conclude Allah is a body.
D) We would have to invent a new name.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first answer establishes that deriving meaning from divine names is a rational exercise that distinguishes reason-based attribution from mere scriptural acceptance.

?

?
Maturidi warns that without deriving meaning, we are left with mere scriptural acceptance. Have you ever stopped to ask yourself: 'Do I rely on God's names as intellectual concepts, or do they ignite a real change in my character?' Which of Allah's names demands the most from you right now, and why are you avoiding it?

The Second Answer: Affirming Names Without Likeness
Second answer to the objection about divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second answer: that the meaning of names like al-Fā‘il and al-‘Ālim is known in the witnessed world but does not prove createdness, so we affirm them for Allah while negating all likeness to creation.

Core Idea
Witnessed meaning does not imply createdness.

Maturidi's second answer addresses the core of the objection: yes, these names have meanings in the observable world (shāhid), but that does not automatically make Allah similar to created beings. He argues that the meaning understood in created contexts is not evidence of temporal origination (ḥadath), and since describing Allah with these names is possible, we must affirm them while simultaneously denying any resemblance between Allah and His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer passage
Read the Arabic text for the second answer carefully, then its translation. Notice it starts with 'The second is that...'.
This answer builds on the first but takes a different angle.
2
Analyze the meaning in the witnessed world
Maturidi concedes that the meaning of al-Fā‘il and al-‘Ālim is understood in the shāhid (the created, observable world). But he immediately clarifies that this does not prove that Allah is created (ḥadath).
The term 'shāhid' refers to the world of creation, especially temporal beings.
3
Understand the negation of likeness (nafy al-shabah)
Even though we use the same words, we must negate any similarity (tashbīh) between Allah and His creatures. The passage ends with 'likeness is likening creation to Him' – meaning we must not liken Allah to creation.
Nafy al-shabah is a key principle in Maturidi theology.
4
Reflect on the necessity of affirming the names
Maturidi states that because it is possible to describe Allah with these names, it becomes necessary (lazima) to say them while negating likeness. This shows that affirmation and transcendence go together.

Likeness is likening creation to Him. And with Allah is success.

This passage is an objection-and-answer. First: if we did not derive the meaning of these names from their linguistic root, we could only rely on revelation (sam‘) to know that Allah is called by them, and this would not be based on reason. But we do derive meaning, so the two approaches differ. Second: the meaning of these names is known in the witnessed world (shāhid), but that does not prove that Allah is created (ḥadath). Refutation of the Claim that Divine Names Imply Corporeality
Maturidi refutes the claim that divine names necessitate corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational refutation of the claim that calling Allah 'Fā‘il', 'Qādir', and '‘Ālim' implies that He is a body.

Core Idea
Shared names do not imply shared physicality; 'body' entails composition, impossible for Allah.

Maturidi anticipates an objection from those who argue that since in the witnessed world only bodies are called agents, powerful, and knowing, Allah must be a body. He systematically dismantles this argument by examining the implications of the term 'body' and the logic of naming. This lesson walks through his key responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first response
Read the opening lines of the passage where the objector argues that divine names imply corporeality, and Maturidi's immediate reply: 'We exist as bodies but are not called bodies for that reason.'
Notice how Maturidi rejects the inference by distinguishing existence from naming.
2
Understand the second response
Maturidi states that revelation and reason have already established the correct basis for these names, and the objector has not refuted that.
Here he shifts the burden of proof back to the objector.
3
Analyze the definition of 'body'
Read where Maturidi defines 'body' as something that admits division and has parts, unlike accidents and actions. This shows that 'body' cannot apply to Allah.
Key insight: 'body' in the witness implies composition, so it is not a general term.
4
Follow the analogy with 'composed' being
Examine the final argument: if we rejected 'composed' because its apparent meaning suggests action, we'd also reject eternal existence—absurd. So by analogy, 'body' cannot be applied to Allah.
This is a reductio ad absurdum showing the flaw in the objector's method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم لا قلت بأنه بما سمى به فاعلا كان جسما، وكذلك القادر والعالم؛ إذ لا أحد في الشاهد سمي به إلا وهو جسم؟ قيل: لا يسمى بذلك في الشاهد لأنه لوجودنا أجساما لا تسمى بذلك فذلك لم يلزم به القول. على أنا بينا الوجوه التي أحقت التسمية بما سمي من السمع والعبرة، ولسنا نجد ذلك في الذي عارض به. ولو جاز لنا ليجوز الآخر أيضا أن يقابلنا بمثله في الجسد والشخص ونحو ذلك. مع ما كان اسم الجسم غير واقع في الشاهد على ما لا يحتمل التجزئة والتبغيض من نحو العرض والفعل والحركة والسكون، ثبت أنه اسم ذو الأجزاء كالطويل والعريض والمؤلف. ولو لم يبطل القول بالمؤلف لما يدل ظاهره على فعل به، إذ لو بطل ليبطل القول بموجود بذاته في الأزل، ولو كان كذلك ليجوز القول بطويل وجسد ولون وطعم ونحو ذلك؛ لما ليس الظاهر إلا ذلك، فإذ لم يجز لما في الحقيقة إيجابه وإن لم يكن في اللفظ دليله فمثله في الجسم. والله الموفق.
If it is said: Why did you not say that by what He is called Fā‘il, He is a body, and likewise al-Qādir and al-‘Ālim, since no one in the witnessed world is called by these unless he is a body? It is said: He is not called by that in the witnessed world because we exist as bodies [but] not called bodies for that reason, so it does not necessitate the statement. Moreover, we have shown the aspects that made the naming correct with what is named from revelation and reason, and we do not find that in what he opposes. And if it were permissible for us, it would also be permissible for another to oppose us with the like in body and person and similar. And since the name 'body' is not applied in the witnessed world to what does not admit division and partition, such as accident, action, motion, and rest, it is established that it is a name possessing parts, like tall, wide, and composed. And if the statement of a composed being were not invalidated because its apparent meaning indicates an action upon it, then if it were invalidated, the statement of an existent in itself in pre-eternity would be invalidated. And if that were so, it would be permissible to say tall and body and color and taste and similar, since nothing but the apparent would be there. So if it is not permissible because in reality it necessitates something even if not in the wording, then similarly for body. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of an objection. The objector argues: since Allah is called Fā‘il, Qādir, and ‘Ālim, and in the witnessed world only bodies are called these, Allah must be a body. Maturidi answers: (1) In the witness, we exist as bodies but are not called bodies because of that, so the objection does not necessitate. (2) We have already shown that revelation and reason justify these names for Allah, and the objector has not countered that. (3) If his logic were valid, he could also argue that Allah is a body from other names, but the definition of 'body' in the witness applies only to what can be divided and partitioned (e.g., accidents, actions, motions, rest are not bodies). Thus, 'body' implies having parts. (4) If we denied the term 'composed' (mu’allaf) because its apparent meaning suggests action, then we would also have to deny existence in itself in pre-eternity, which is absurd. So by analogy, the term 'body' cannot be applied to Allah because it would necessitate something not found in the wording but required by reality. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الجسم (al-jism)
التجزئة (al-tajzi'a)
التبغيض (al-tabghīḍ)
المؤلف (al-mu’allaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sharing names like 'agent' implies sharing physicality (i.e., Allah must be a body because agents in the world are bodies).
Correction: Maturidi shows that this inference is flawed: in the witnessed world, bodies exist but are not called bodies for that reason; moreover, the definition of 'body' entails division and parts, which cannot apply to Allah.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about applying the name 'body' to Allah based on the objector's reasoning?
A) It is permissible if we say 'body not like bodies'.
B) It is invalid because 'body' in the witness implies having parts, which cannot apply to Allah.
C) It is obligatory because Allah is called Fā‘il.
D) It is metaphorical without further explanation.

Key Takeaway
The claim that divine names imply corporeality is refuted by showing that 'body' entails composition, which cannot be attributed to Allah, and that the objector's logic leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah’s names like al-Qādir or al-‘Ālim, do you ever subconsciously picture a powerful human-like being? How does Maturidi’s argument that ‘body’ means divisible and composite expose that tendency? What will you do from now on to guard your heart against such imagery?
?
The objector’s logic seemed reasonable at first: we only see agents in bodies, so Allah must be a body. What hidden assumption about analogy did Maturidi uncover? Can you identify a similar assumption you have made about Allah’s attributes that you must now abandon?
?
If someone tells you, ‘Allah has a hand, so He must have a body,’ you now know Maturidi’s reductio: rejecting one apparent meaning leads to absurdity. Will you memorize this argument to defend tawhid? When will you practice explaining it to someone who holds this misconception?

Defending 'Thing' for Allah: Not a Gateway to Anthropomorphism
Maturidi responds to two objections claiming 'thing' implies 'body' or 'human'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that affirming 'thing' for Allah does not logically lead to affirming 'body' or 'human', because the reasons and implications are fundamentally different.

Core Idea
'Thing' affirms existence; 'body' implies composition – they are not parallel.

In this passage, Maturidi faces a clever objection: if we can say Allah is a 'thing not like things', why not also a 'body not like bodies' or even 'human not like humans'? He responds by showing that the motivation for affirming 'thing' (avoiding ta'til) does not apply to 'body', and that a 'body not like bodies' is a contradiction. The objections are analyzed and shown to be invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Start by reading the first objection and Maturidi's answer. Notice how he distinguishes the reason for saying 'thing' from the reason for saying 'body'.
The objector tries to force an analogy, but Maturidi points out that most things (accidents) are not bodies.
2
Examine the second objection
Now read the second objection: why not 'human not like people'? Maturidi's answer here focuses on the fact that Allah is not a body, so the analogy fails.
The response includes a subtle point about counter-arguments vs. judgments.
3
Reflect on the analysis of counter-arguments
Finally, note Maturidi's meta-argument: if we said 'body', the phrase 'thing not like things' would become 'thing not like some things' (since body is a kind of thing), destroying the intended meaning. This shows the objector's move is contradictory.
This step ties the whole dialectic together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ا يجوز إطلاق لفظ «الشيء» على الله]
فإن قيل: إذ قلتم: شيء لا كالأشياء، لم لا قلتم: جسم كالأجسام؟
قيل له: لأن السبب الذي ألزمنا القول بالشيء لم يوجد في الجسم، لذلك لم نقل.
وبعد، فإنه لا يحلو فيما يريد إلزامنا من القول بالجسمية من أن يلزمنا بقولنا بالشيء، فوجدنا أكثر الأشياء وهي الأعراض والصفات من غير لزوم القول فيها بالجسمية يمنع ذلك.
وإن كان يريد بقولنا: لا كالأشياء حرف الإثبات ليدل على مائية المثبت، فلا وجه لهذا السؤال.
يقول: إذ جاز أن يكون شيئا لا كالأشياء لم لا جاز أن يكون إنسانا لا كالناس؟
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فجواب مثله أن يقال: لأنه ليس بجسم، فيقال: لا كالأجسام، وليس هذا النوع بمعارضة إنما هو محاكمة. ونحن لا تلك إيجاد الإله حتى تقابل بمثل هذا، فيقال لنا: إذ جعلتم ذا علم لا جعلتم ذا جسم، بل يتعالى عن الجمل على جهة، بل يوصف بما هو عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
ثم المعارضة عند التحصيل تناقض؛ لأنه قال: إذ قلتم: شيء لا كالأشياء لا كالأجسام؟ فإذا قلنا: جسم يصير قولنا: شيء لا كالأشياء هو شيء لا كبعض الأشياء، إذ الجسم وفي ذلك بطلان القول بجسم لا كالأجسام. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم معنى قولنا شيء لا كالأشياء هو إسقاط مائية الأشياء، وهي نوعان: عين وهو جسم، وصفة وهي أعراض. فإذا أزلنا ذلك المعنى الذي هو الجسم من الأعيان أبطلنا التشبيه، كما إذا أزلنا معنى الإثبات ونفي التعطيل أبطلنا القول به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
[It is not permissible to apply the word 'thing' to Allah?] If it is said: Since you say 'thing not like things', why do you not say 'body not like bodies'? It is said to him: Because the reason that compelled us to say 'thing' is not present in 'body', therefore we did not say it. Moreover, it cannot be [done] in what he wants to compel us to say 'body' from our saying 'thing', for we find most things — which are accidents and attributes — without necessitating the statement of bodiliness, so that prevents it. And if he intends by our saying 'not like things' the particle of affirmation to indicate the quiddity of the affirmed, then this question has no basis. He says: If it is permissible for it to be a thing not like things, why is it not permissible for it to be a human not like people? The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The like of this is answered by saying: Because He is not a body, so it is said 'not like bodies'. And this type is not a counter-argument, but rather a judgment. And we do not [create?] the existence of God to be confronted with such. So it is said to us: If you made Him possessor of knowledge, you did not make Him possessor of a body. Rather, He is exalted above the whole in some way; rather He is described as He is. And there is no power except with Allah. Then the confrontation upon analysis is contradictory; because he said: Since you say 'thing not like things' [you should say] 'not like bodies'? So if we say 'body', our statement 'thing not like things' becomes 'thing not like some things', since [it is] a body, and in that negation of the statement 'body not like bodies'. And there is no power except with Allah. Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then the meaning of our statement 'thing not like things' is the negation of the quiddity of things. And things are of two types: 'ayn (substance) which is body, and ṣifa (attribute) which are accidents. So if we remove that meaning which is the body from substances, we negate anthropomorphism, just as if we remove the meaning of affirmation and negation of ta‘ṭīl (negation of attributes), we negate the statement. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage contains a series of objections and answers about the term 'thing' (shay') as applied to Allah. First, an objector asks: Since you say Allah is a thing unlike other things, why don't you also say He is a body unlike other bodies? Maturidi replies: The reason that compelled us to affirm 'thing' (i.e., affirming existence and avoiding ta‘ṭīl) does not apply to 'body', so we refrain. Moreover, if we look at most things in existence—accidents and attributes—they are not bodies, so being a 'thing' does not entail being a body. If the objector insists on the wording, then the phrase 'not like things' is meant to affirm the quiddity (mā'iyya) of the thing, not to imply bodily composition. Then a further objection: If it is permissible for Allah to be a thing unlike things, why not a human unlike humans? The answer (from al-Shaykh) is: Because Allah is not a body, so He is 'not like bodies'. This is not a counter-argument but a judgment. Then Maturidi (Abū Manṣūr) explains the meaning: 'thing not like things' negates the quiddity of things. Things are of two kinds: 'ayn (substance) which is body, and ṣifa (attribute) which are accidents. Thus, by saying Allah is not like things, we negate that He is like either substances or accidents. If we remove that meaning, we would fall into anthropomorphism (tashbīh). This is analogous to affirming existence while negating ta‘ṭīl. So the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الشيء (al-shay')
الجسم (al-jism)
التشبيه (al-tashbīh)
التعطيل (al-ta‘ṭīl)
المائية (al-mā'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'thing' for Allah implies that He is a body or has bodily attributes by analogy.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'thing' affirms existence without implying composition; 'body' is a specific category of thing. The two are not parallel because the reason for affirming 'thing' (to avoid ta'til) does not apply to 'body', and most things (accidents) are not bodies.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main reason for saying 'thing' but not 'body' in this passage?
A) Because 'thing' is more respectful
B) Because the reason for 'thing' (affirming existence) does not apply to 'body'
C) Because Allah is too great to be called 'body'
D) Because 'body' is a biblical term

Key Takeaway
Affirming 'thing' for Allah does not entail affirming 'body', because the former is about existence and the latter about composition – they are not parallel concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself imagining Allah in human terms—like having a form or location—what does that reveal about your heart's attachment to the familiar? How will you use the lesson's distinction between 'thing' and 'body' to discipline your imagination starting today?
?
This lesson shows that the objector's analogy seemed logical but was false. Are there analogies about God that you have unconsciously accepted without examining their assumptions? Which one will you specifically challenge this week, and how will the source's method guide you?
?
The passage ends with a note of reliance: 'There is no power except with Allah.' When you feel drawn to rationalizing God in limited terms, what concrete act of submission can you perform to assert His transcendence and your need for His guidance?
