beginner
4 min
The Meaning of 'Thing not like things': Negating Both Substance and Accident
Maturidi explains the meaning of 'thing not like things' by dividing things into substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's final clarification that 'thing not like things' negates the quiddity of all created things, which are of two kinds: substance (body) and attribute (accident).

Core Idea
Saying Allah is 'not like things' negates both bodily substance and accidental attributes.

After refuting the objections, Maturidi provides a positive definition: the phrase 'thing not like things' negates the mā'iyya (quiddity) of all things. He then divides things into two categories: 'ayn (substance/body) and ṣifa (attribute/accident). By negating both, we avoid anthropomorphism (tashbīh) just as affirming existence avoids ta'tīl. This completes the defense of the term 'thing' for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the key statement
Read Maturidi's statement: 'The meaning of our statement 'thing not like things' is the negation of the quiddity of things.' Understand that 'quiddity' means the essential nature or 'whatness'.
This is a crucial conceptual move – it defines what is being negated.
2
Identify the two types of things
Maturidi then divides things into two types: 'ayn (substance/body) and ṣifa (attribute/accident). Note that 'body' falls under the first type, and 'accidents' under the second.
This classification is comprehensive: everything created is either a substance or an attribute.
3
Consider the implications for tawhid
Reflect on how negating both types prevents anthropomorphism. If we negate being like a substance, we avoid saying Allah is a body; if we negate being like an attribute, we avoid saying He has created attributes. This is analogous to affirming existence while negating ta'til.
Maturidi uses this to show that the phrase is not arbitrary but carefully designed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ا يجوز إطلاق لفظ «الشيء» على الله]
فإن قيل: إذ قلتم: شيء لا كالأشياء، لم لا قلتم: جسم كالأجسام؟
قيل له: لأن السبب الذي ألزمنا القول بالشيء لم يوجد في الجسم، لذلك لم نقل.
وبعد، فإنه لا يحلو فيما يريد إلزامنا من القول بالجسمية من أن يلزمنا بقولنا بالشيء، فوجدنا أكثر الأشياء وهي الأعراض والصفات من غير لزوم القول فيها بالجسمية يمنع ذلك.
وإن كان يريد بقولنا: لا كالأشياء حرف الإثبات ليدل على مائية المثبت، فلا وجه لهذا السؤال.
يقول: إذ جاز أن يكون شيئا لا كالأشياء لم لا جاز أن يكون إنسانا لا كالناس؟
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فجواب مثله أن يقال: لأنه ليس بجسم، فيقال: لا كالأجسام، وليس هذا النوع بمعارضة إنما هو محاكمة. ونحن لا تلك إيجاد الإله حتى تقابل بمثل هذا، فيقال لنا: إذ جعلتم ذا علم لا جعلتم ذا جسم، بل يتعالى عن الجمل على جهة، بل يوصف بما هو عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
ثم المعارضة عند التحصيل تناقض؛ لأنه قال: إذ قلتم: شيء لا كالأشياء لا كالأجسام؟ فإذا قلنا: جسم يصير قولنا: شيء لا كالأشياء هو شيء لا كبعض الأشياء، إذ الجسم وفي ذلك بطلان القول بجسم لا كالأجسام. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم معنى قولنا شيء لا كالأشياء هو إسقاط مائية الأشياء، وهي نوعان: عين وهو جسم، وصفة وهي أعراض. فإذا أزلنا ذلك المعنى الذي هو الجسم من الأعيان أبطلنا التشبيه، كما إذا أزلنا معنى الإثبات ونفي التعطيل أبطلنا القول به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
[It is not permissible to apply the word 'thing' to Allah?] If it is said: Since you say 'thing not like things', why do you not say 'body not like bodies'? It is said to him: Because the reason that compelled us to say 'thing' is not present in 'body', therefore we did not say it. Moreover, it cannot be [done] in what he wants to compel us to say 'body' from our saying 'thing', for we find most things — which are accidents and attributes — without necessitating the statement of bodiliness, so that prevents it. And if he intends by our saying 'not like things' the particle of affirmation to indicate the quiddity of the affirmed, then this question has no basis. He says: If it is permissible for it to be a thing not like things, why is it not permissible for it to be a human not like people? The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The like of this is answered by saying: Because He is not a body, so it is said 'not like bodies'. And this type is not a counter-argument, but rather a judgment. And we do not [create?] the existence of God to be confronted with such. So it is said to us: If you made Him possessor of knowledge, you did not make Him possessor of a body. Rather, He is exalted above the whole in some way; rather He is described as He is. And there is no power except with Allah. Then the confrontation upon analysis is contradictory; because he said: Since you say 'thing not like things' [you should say] 'not like bodies'? So if we say 'body', our statement 'thing not like things' becomes 'thing not like some things', since [it is] a body, and in that negation of the statement 'body not like bodies'. And there is no power except with Allah. Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then the meaning of our statement 'thing not like things' is the negation of the quiddity of things. And things are of two types: 'ayn (substance) which is body, and ṣifa (attribute) which are accidents. So if we remove that meaning which is the body from substances, we negate anthropomorphism, just as if we remove the meaning of affirmation and negation of ta‘ṭīl (negation of attributes), we negate the statement. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage contains a series of objections and answers about the term 'thing' (shay') as applied to Allah. First, an objector asks: Since you say Allah is a thing unlike other things, why don't you also say He is a body unlike other bodies? Maturidi replies: The reason that compelled us to affirm 'thing' (i.e., affirming existence and avoiding ta‘ṭīl) does not apply to 'body', so we refrain. Moreover, if we look at most things in existence—accidents and attributes—they are not bodies, so being a 'thing' does not entail being a body. If the objector insists on the wording, then the phrase 'not like things' is meant to affirm the quiddity (mā'iyya) of the thing, not to imply bodily composition. Then a further objection: If it is permissible for Allah to be a thing unlike things, why not a human unlike humans? The answer (from al-Shaykh) is: Because Allah is not a body, so He is 'not like bodies'. This is not a counter-argument but a judgment. Then Maturidi (Abū Manṣūr) explains the meaning: 'thing not like things' negates the quiddity of things. Things are of two kinds: 'ayn (substance) which is body, and ṣifa (attribute) which are accidents. Thus, by saying Allah is not like things, we negate that He is like either substances or accidents. If we remove that meaning, we would fall into anthropomorphism (tashbīh). This is analogous to affirming existence while negating ta‘ṭīl. So the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
المائية (al-mā'iyya)
العين (al-'ayn)
الصفة (al-ṣifa)
العرض (al-'araḍ)
التشبيه (al-tashbīh)
التعطيل (al-ta‘ṭīl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'thing not like things' only negates that Allah is a body, but does not address other similarities.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states it negates the quiddity of all things, which includes both substances (bodies) and attributes (accidents). Thus it covers all possible similarities.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two types of things ('ashyā')?
A) Material and spiritual
B) 'Ayn (substance/body) and ṣifa (attribute/accident)
C) Living and non-living
D) Visible and invisible

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'thing not like things' is a precise theological formula that negates the quiddity of both substances and accidents, thereby preventing anthropomorphism while affirming existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you ever unconsciously picture a form or shape? How does this lesson challenge that tendency? What will you actively do to remind yourself that He is not like anything?
?
Are you more prone to making Allah similar to something (tashbih) or denying His attributes (ta'til)? Which extreme is a greater temptation for you, and what step will you take to avoid it starting today?
?
The phrase 'thing not like things' is a precise formula. In your daily life, when you think of Allah's names and attributes, do you settle for vague concepts or do you actively negate similarity? What concrete change in your thinking will you commit to after this lesson?

The Two Expressions for 'Shay'' and the Principle of Non-Similarity
Maturidi introduces the two senses of 'shay'' and argues that shared names do not imply shared meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of the term 'shay'' and why agreement in names between God and creation does not imply similarity.

Core Idea
Shared names do not mean shared essences.

Maturidi begins by clarifying the term 'shay'' (thing) which is applied to God and creation. Critics argue that calling God a 'thing' makes Him like created things. Maturidi counters by distinguishing between two ways of using a word: as a mere name and as a descriptor. This lesson lays the foundation for the rest of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Maturidi gives two expressions (عبارتان) for 'thing'.
2
Identify the examples
Find the examples of 'unique' (واحد) and 'unbelief/Islam' (كفر وإسلام). How do they show that same name can have different meanings?
The first example is about a person being called 'unique' while others are also called 'unique' in a different sense.
3
Understand the principle
Maturidi states that agreement in names does not necessitate similarity (التشابه). Think about why this is a crucial argument against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا في القول بالشيء عبارتان:
إحداهما أن يجعل الشيء اسما، والموافقة في الأسماء لا توجب التشابه، لما قد يستعمل في موضع نفي الموافقة في المعنى نحو أن يقال: "فلان" واحد عصره، وواحد قومه على نفي أن يكون له فيهم نظير أو شبيه من الوجه الذي أريد، وإن كانوا جميعا في تسمية الواحد شركاء. ولو كانت الموافقة في الاسم توجب التشابه لم يحتمل استعماله في موضع إرادة نفي الموافقة، وكذلك تجد قول كفر وإسلام على تحقيق الاسم لكل واحد منهما والموافقة من حيث القول ولكن المعنى متناقض، وكذا ذلك في الحركات والأفعال ونحو ذلك. ودليل إثبات القول بالشيء وجهان:
أحدهما السمع من قوله تعالى: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ، ولو لم يكن هو شيئا لم عنه شيئية الأشياء باسم الشيئية؛ إذ الشيء في التحقيق خلاف ما لا يحتمل القول بالشيء، وكذلك قوله تعالى: قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَادَةً قُلِ اللَّهُ شَهِيدٌ، فلو لم يقع عليه اسم الشيء لكان لا يحتمل تضمنه ذلك القول حتى ينسب إليه.
وأما العقل فهو أن الشيئية اسم الإثبات لا غير في العرف؛ إذ القول بلا شيء نفي [؟] لم يرد [؟]، فثبت أنه اسم الإثبات ونفي التعطيل. فثبت أنه [؟]. فإن كان قوم لا يعرفون أن معنى الشيء الإثبات والخروج من التعطيل يتقى ذلك كراهة أن يعتقد قلوبهم معنى مكروها ويقولون بالهستية، فإنه في لغتهم أوضح في معنى الإثبات، وإن كانا واحدا عند أهل العلم بهذا اللسان.

مع ما كان القول بلا شيء يستعمل في نفي الحقيقة أو تصغير الثابت، فثبت أن القول بالشيء إنما هو في إثبات الذات وتعظيمه، والله حقيق لذلك. والقول بلا شيء لا يوجب واحدا منهما، فكذلك القول بالجسم ليس تثبيت واحد مما يحمد وجوده أو يعظم؛ لذلك اختلفا. وعلى ذلك القول بلا عالم ولا قادر اسم ينفي العظمة والجلال، فمثله في العالم والقادر يوجب الوصف بالعظمة والجلال. وبالله التوفيق. فإنه في الشاهد لا يفهم من قول الرجل "شيء" ماهية الذات، ولا من قوله "عالم" و"قادر" الصفة، وإنما يفهم من الأول الوجود والهستية، ومن الثاني أنه موصوف، لا أن فيه بيان ماهية الذات، كقول الرجل "جسم" إنه ذكر ماهية أنه ذو أبعاد أو ذو جهات أو محتمل للنهايات وقابل للأعراض، وكذا في الإنسان وسائر الأعيان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن القول بهذا كله واجب بما ثبت في السمع التسمية به، وبالله.
We have two expressions regarding the statement about a thing: One is that 'thing' is made a name, and agreement in names does not necessitate similarity, because it is sometimes used in a context of denying agreement in meaning, as when it is said: 'So-and-so is the unique one of his age' and 'the unique one of his people' to deny that he has a peer or likeness in the respect intended, even though they all share in the naming of 'unique'. If agreement in name necessitated similarity, its use in a context intending denial of agreement would not be possible. Similarly, you find the statement 'unbelief' and 'Islam' in terms of the actual name for each of them and agreement in expression, but the meaning is contradictory. Likewise in movements, actions, and the like. The proof for affirming the statement about a thing is two-fold: First, the scripture from His saying: 'There is nothing like unto Him' [42:11]; if He were not a thing, He would not have negated the thingness of things by the name 'thingness', for 'thing' in reality is the opposite of what does not admit the statement of 'thing'. And similarly His saying: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is witness' [6:19]; if the name 'thing' did not apply to Him, it would not be possible for that statement to include it so that it is attributed to Him. As for reason, it is that 'thingness' is the name of affirmation only in customary usage; for the statement 'not a thing' is a negation [?] not found [?], so it is established that it is the name of affirmation and negation of ta'til. So it is established that [?]. If some people do not know that the meaning of 'thing' is affirmation and exit from ta'til, they avoid that out of dislike that their hearts should believe a disliked meaning, and they say 'hastiyah' (existence), for it is clearer in their language in the meaning of affirmation, even though the two are one according to those learned in this language. Moreover, while the statement 'not a thing' is used for negation of reality or diminishment of what is established, so it is established that the statement 'thing' is only for affirmation of the essence and its glorification—and Allah is worthy of that. The statement 'not a thing' does not entail either of these, so likewise the statement 'body' is not an affirmation of anything that is praised for its existence or glorified; therefore they differ. Similarly, the statement 'not knowing' and 'not powerful' is a name that negates greatness and majesty, so its counterpart in 'knowing' and 'powerful' entails the description of greatness and majesty. And with Allah is success. For in the witnessed realm, one does not understand from a man's saying 'thing' the quiddity of the essence, nor from his saying 'knowing' and 'powerful' the attribute; rather, from the first one understands existence and 'hastiyah', and from the second that he is described, not that it contains a clarification of the quiddity of the essence, like a man's saying 'body'—it mentions quiddity that it has dimensions or directions or is susceptible to termini and receptive to accidents. So too in man and all other entities. And there is no power except with Allah. Thereafter, the statement about all of this is obligatory due to what is established in scripture of naming by it, and with Allah.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'shay'' (thing) and proves that it is properly affirmed of God without implying similarity to creation. Maturidi begins by stating that there are two expressions (عبارتان) regarding the statement about a thing (القول بالشيء). The first expression treats 'thing' as a name, and he argues that agreement in names does not necessitate agreement in meaning or similarity; he illustrates with the example of calling someone 'unique' even when others are also called 'unique' in a different sense. He then provides two proofs for affirming that God is called 'thing': the first is scriptural (السمع) citing two verses (42:11 and 6:19) which would be meaningless if 'thing' did not apply to God. The second proof is rational (العقل): 'thingness' (الشيئية) is the name of affirmation (الإثبات) in customary usage, and the opposite (saying 'not a thing') is negation. Therefore, calling God 'thing' affirms His existence and negates ta'til (stripping God of attributes). He then addresses those who avoid the term 'thing' for God out of fear of improper belief, preferring the Persian term 'hastiyah' (existence), noting that they are identical in meaning. Next, he argues that saying 'not a thing' implies negation or diminishment, so saying 'thing' affirms the essence and glorifies it. He contrasts this with saying 'body' (جسم) which does not affirm anything praiseworthy or majestic; similarly, saying 'not knowing' negates majesty while 'knowing' entails it. In the witnessed world (الشاهد), he explains that saying 'thing' conveys existence only, not quiddity, whereas saying 'body' conveys quiddity (dimensions, etc.). The conclusion is that affirming the term 'thing' for God is obligatory based on revelation. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that calling God 'thing' implies anthropomorphism; rather it simply affirms existence.

Key Terms
الشيء
التشبيه
الاسم
المعنى
الموافقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that because God is called 'thing' in the Qur'an, He must be similar to other things.
Correction: Maturidi shows that shared names do not imply shared essences; the term 'thing' is used simply as a name to affirm existence without implying any similarity.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument Maturidi makes in this passage?
A) That 'thing' has multiple meanings
B) That agreement in names does not imply similarity in meaning
C) That 'thing' cannot be applied to God
D) That the term 'thing' is only used for created beings

Key Takeaway
Shared names between God and creation do not imply shared attributes or similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Scriptural and Rational Proofs for Affirming 'Shay'' of Allah
Maturidi provides scriptural and rational proofs that God is called 'thing' and addresses the use of 'hastiyah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs (scriptural and rational) that Maturidi offers for affirming that God is called 'thing', and how these proofs counter the view of stripping God of attributes.

Core Idea
Both Qur'an and reason confirm that 'thing' is an affirmation of existence.

After establishing that shared names do not imply similarity, Maturidi proceeds to prove that the term 'thing' is properly applied to God. He presents two proofs: one from scripture (the Qur'anic verses that refer to God as 'thing') and one from reason (the customary use of 'thingness' as affirmation). This lesson examines these proofs and the discussion of the alternative term 'hastiyah' (existence) used by some theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural proof
Read the verses from Surat al-Shura (42:11) and Surat al-An'am (6:19) that Maturidi cites. Notice how they imply that God is a 'thing' because He negates similarity to things and because He is the greatest witness.
2
Read the rational proof
Read Maturidi's explanation that 'thingness' (الشيئية) is the name of affirmation in common usage, and saying 'not a thing' is negation. Therefore, calling God 'thing' affirms His existence.
3
Consider the alternative term 'hastiyah'
Some theologians prefer the Persian term 'hastiyah' (existence) to avoid potential misunderstanding. Maturidi explains that it means the same as 'thing'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأما العقل فهو أن الشيئية اسم الإثبات لا غير في العرف؛ إذ القول بلا شيء نفي [؟] لم يرد [؟]، فثبت أنه اسم الإثبات ونفي التعطيل. فثبت أنه [؟]. فإن كان قوم لا يعرفون أن معنى الشيء الإثبات والخروج من التعطيل يتقى ذلك كراهة أن يعتقد قلوبهم معنى مكروها ويقولون بالهستية، فإنه في لغتهم أوضح في معنى الإثبات، وإن كانا واحدا عند أهل العلم بهذا اللسان.

مع ما كان القول بلا شيء يستعمل في نفي الحقيقة أو تصغير الثابت، فثبت أن القول بالشيء إنما هو في إثبات الذات وتعظيمه، والله حقيق لذلك. والقول بلا شيء لا يوجب واحدا منهما، فكذلك القول بالجسم ليس تثبيت واحد مما يحمد وجوده أو يعظم؛ لذلك اختلفا. وعلى ذلك القول بلا عالم ولا قادر اسم ينفي العظمة والجلال، فمثله في العالم والقادر يوجب الوصف بالعظمة والجلال. وبالله التوفيق. فإنه في الشاهد لا يفهم من قول الرجل "شيء" ماهية الذات، ولا من قوله "عالم" و"قادر" الصفة، وإنما يفهم من الأول الوجود والهستية، ومن الثاني أنه موصوف، لا أن فيه بيان ماهية الذات، كقول الرجل "جسم" إنه ذكر ماهية أنه ذو أبعاد أو ذو جهات أو محتمل للنهايات وقابل للأعراض، وكذا في الإنسان وسائر الأعيان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن القول بهذا كله واجب بما ثبت في السمع التسمية به، وبالله.
We have two expressions regarding the statement about a thing: One is that 'thing' is made a name, and agreement in names does not necessitate similarity, because it is sometimes used in a context of denying agreement in meaning, as when it is said: 'So-and-so is the unique one of his age' and 'the unique one of his people' to deny that he has a peer or likeness in the respect intended, even though they all share in the naming of 'unique'. If agreement in name necessitated similarity, its use in a context intending denial of agreement would not be possible. Similarly, you find the statement 'unbelief' and 'Islam' in terms of the actual name for each of them and agreement in expression, but the meaning is contradictory. Likewise in movements, actions, and the like. The proof for affirming the statement about a thing is two-fold: First, the scripture from His saying: 'There is nothing like unto Him' [42:11]; if He were not a thing, He would not have negated the thingness of things by the name 'thingness', for 'thing' in reality is the opposite of what does not admit the statement of 'thing'. And similarly His saying: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is witness' [6:19]; if the name 'thing' did not apply to Him, it would not be possible for that statement to include it so that it is attributed to Him. As for reason, it is that 'thingness' is the name of affirmation only in customary usage; for the statement 'not a thing' is a negation [?] not found [?], so it is established that it is the name of affirmation and negation of ta'til. So it is established that [?]. If some people do not know that the meaning of 'thing' is affirmation and exit from ta'til, they avoid that out of dislike that their hearts should believe a disliked meaning, and they say 'hastiyah' (existence), for it is clearer in their language in the meaning of affirmation, even though the two are one according to those learned in this language. Moreover, while the statement 'not a thing' is used for negation of reality or diminishment of what is established, so it is established that the statement 'thing' is only for affirmation of the essence and its glorification—and Allah is worthy of that. The statement 'not a thing' does not entail either of these, so likewise the statement 'body' is not an affirmation of anything that is praised for its existence or glorified; therefore they differ. Similarly, the statement 'not knowing' and 'not powerful' is a name that negates greatness and majesty, so its counterpart in 'knowing' and 'powerful' entails the description of greatness and majesty. And with Allah is success. For in the witnessed realm, one does not understand from a man's saying 'thing' the quiddity of the essence, nor from his saying 'knowing' and 'powerful' the attribute; rather, from the first one understands existence and 'hastiyah', and from the second that he is described, not that it contains a clarification of the quiddity of the essence, like a man's saying 'body'—it mentions quiddity that it has dimensions or directions or is susceptible to termini and receptive to accidents. So too in man and all other entities. And there is no power except with Allah. Thereafter, the statement about all of this is obligatory due to what is established in scripture of naming by it, and with Allah.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'shay'' (thing) and proves that it is properly affirmed of God without implying similarity to creation. Maturidi begins by stating that there are two expressions (عبارتان) regarding the statement about a thing (القول بالشيء). The first expression treats 'thing' as a name, and he argues that agreement in names does not necessitate agreement in meaning or similarity; he illustrates with the example of calling someone 'unique' even when others are also called 'unique' in a different sense. He then provides two proofs for affirming that God is called 'thing': the first is scriptural (السمع) citing two verses (42:11 and 6:19) which would be meaningless if 'thing' did not apply to God. The second proof is rational (العقل): 'thingness' (الشيئية) is the name of affirmation (الإثبات) in customary usage, and the opposite (saying 'not a thing') is negation. Therefore, calling God 'thing' affirms His existence and negates ta'til (stripping God of attributes). He then addresses those who avoid the term 'thing' for God out of fear of improper belief, preferring the Persian term 'hastiyah' (existence), noting that they are identical in meaning. Next, he argues that saying 'not a thing' implies negation or diminishment, so saying 'thing' affirms the essence and glorifies it. He contrasts this with saying 'body' (جسم) which does not affirm anything praiseworthy or majestic; similarly, saying 'not knowing' negates majesty while 'knowing' entails it. In the witnessed world (الشاهد), he explains that saying 'thing' conveys existence only, not quiddity, whereas saying 'body' conveys quiddity (dimensions, etc.). The conclusion is that affirming the term 'thing' for God is obligatory based on revelation. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that calling God 'thing' implies anthropomorphism; rather it simply affirms existence.

Key Terms
السمع
العقل
الشيئية
الإثبات
التعطيل
الهستية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling God 'thing' is a form of tashbih (anthropomorphism) and should be avoided.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the term is used in the Qur'an and that reason shows it simply affirms existence; it does not imply any similarity.

Checkpoint
What is the scriptural proof Maturidi gives from Q. 42:11?
A) It says God is like nothing, implying He cannot be a thing
B) It says 'There is nothing like unto Him', which would be meaningless if God were not a thing
C) It commands calling God 'thing'
D) It equates God with all things

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verses and rational analysis both confirm that 'thing' is a valid name for God, affirming existence and negating ta'til.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever hesitated to describe Allah as a 'thing' because of a subtle fear that it diminishes His majesty? What does Maturidi's rational proof reveal about that hesitation, and how will you now correct your own assumption?
?
If someone argued that calling Allah 'thing' is disrespectful because things are created, how would you defend the Quranic usage using the very verses in this lesson? Are you prepared to give that answer with conviction?
?
The lesson shows that our choice of words about Allah can either affirm or obscure belief. Which words do you use carelessly about Allah, and what intentional change will you make starting today to ensure your language affirms His existence and attributes?

The Meaning of 'Thing' vs. 'Body' and the Obligation to Use Divine Names
Maturidi distinguishes 'thing' from 'body' and concludes that using revealed names for God is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between 'thing' (which affirms existence without specifying quiddity) and 'body' (which implies dimensions and quiddity), and why applying the term 'thing' to God is obligatory based on revelation.

Core Idea
'Thing' affirms existence; 'body' affirms quiddity; God is not a body.

Maturidi concludes his discussion by contrasting the term 'thing' with 'body'. He argues that while 'thing' simply affirms existence and glorification, 'body' implies dimensions and limitations which are inappropriate for God. He also emphasizes that we must use the names that revelation has taught us. This lesson clarifies the conceptual boundaries and the duty to adhere to scriptural nomenclature.

Guided Path
1
Read the contrast between 'thing' and 'body'
Maturidi explains that saying 'thing' affirms existence and glorification, while saying 'body' does not affirm anything praiseworthy or majestic. Understand the difference.
2
Understand the implication for divine attributes
He extends the argument to attributes like 'knowing' and 'powerful'—saying 'not knowing' negates magnificence, while 'knowing' affirms it.
3
Conclude with the obligation to use revealed names
The passage ends by stating that we must say what revelation has said, and that in the witnessed realm 'thing' only conveys existence, not quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا في القول بالشيء عبارتان:
إحداهما أن يجعل الشيء اسما، والموافقة في الأسماء لا توجب التشابه، لما قد يستعمل في موضع نفي الموافقة في المعنى نحو أن يقال: "فلان" واحد عصره، وواحد قومه على نفي أن يكون له فيهم نظير أو شبيه من الوجه الذي أريد، وإن كانوا جميعا في تسمية الواحد شركاء. ولو كانت الموافقة في الاسم توجب التشابه لم يحتمل استعماله في موضع إرادة نفي الموافقة، وكذلك تجد قول كفر وإسلام على تحقيق الاسم لكل واحد منهما والموافقة من حيث القول ولكن المعنى متناقض، وكذا ذلك في الحركات والأفعال ونحو ذلك. ودليل إثبات القول بالشيء وجهان:

The Terms 'Shay' and 'Jism': Affirming Allah's Essence and Majesty
Maturidi distinguishes between the terms shay' and jism in describing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains that calling Allah 'thing' (shay') affirms His essence and majesty, while calling Him 'body' (jism) does not.

Core Idea
'Thing' affirms existence and majesty; 'body' does not.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi examines the linguistic and theological implications of two key terms: 'shay'' and 'jism'. He argues that 'shay'' when applied to Allah affirms His essence and greatness, whereas 'jism' does not convey praise. This distinction is crucial for understanding how to speak about Allah without compromising His transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Get familiar with Maturidi's concise style and the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the usage of 'shay''
Notice that the author contrasts saying 'without thing' with saying 'thing' to show that 'thing' is positive.
3
Contrast with 'jism'
See how the term 'body' is not a term of praise, so it differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما كان القول بلا شيء؛ يستعمل فى نفى الحقيقة أو تصغير الثابت، فثبت أن القول بالشيء إنما هو فى إثبات الذات وتعظيمه، / والله حقيق لذلك. والقول بلا بجه هم لا يوجب واحدا منهما، فكذلك القول بالجسم، ليس تثبيت واحد مما يحمد وجوده أو يعظم؛ لذلك اختلفا. وعلى ذلك القول بلا عاليم ولا قادر اسم ينفى العظمة والجلال، فمثله [؟] في العالم والقادر اينجاب الوصف بالعظمة والجلال .. وبالله التوفيق. فإنه في الشاهد لا يفهم من قول الرجل شيء مائية الذات، ولا من قوله عالم وقادر، الصفة، وإنما يفهم من الأول الوجود والهستية، ومن الثاني أنه موصوف، لا أن فيه بيان مائية الذات كقول الرجل 'جسم، إنه ذكر مائية أنه ذو أبعاد أو ذو جهات أو محتمل للنهايات وقابل للأعراض، وكذا ذا في الإنسان وسائر الأعيان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. التوفيق : وبعد، فإن القول بهذا كله واجب بما ثبت في السمع التسمية به، وبالله
With what was the saying 'with nothing' used in denying reality or belittling the established, so it is established that the saying 'thing' is only in affirming the essence and magnifying it, / and Allah is worthy of that. And the saying 'without thing' does not necessitate one of them, so likewise the saying 'body' is not establishing one of what its existence is praised or magnified; therefore they differed. And upon that, the saying 'without knower' and 'without powerful' is a name that negates majesty and grandeur, so its like [؟] in the knower and the powerful, the description with majesty and grandeur is obligated .. and with Allah is success. For in the witnessed, it is not understood from a man's saying 'thing' the quiddity of the essence, nor from his saying 'knower' and 'powerful' the attribute; rather from the first is understood existence and being, and from the second that he is described, not that it contains a statement of the quiddity of the essence, like a man's saying 'body' – he mentioned quiddity, that it has dimensions or directions or is receptive to ends and susceptible to accidents, and likewise in man and all other entities. And there is no power except with Allah. Success: and then, the saying of all this is obligatory by what is established in scripture of naming with it, and with Allah.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage discusses the semantic implications of the terms 'thing' (shay'), 'body' (jism), and the divine names 'knower' (alim) and 'powerful' (qadir). The argument focuses on how these terms are used in the witnessed realm and their applicability to Allah. The author begins by noting that the phrase 'with nothing' is used to negate reality or belittle a positive fact, so 'thing' actually affirms existence and greatness; Allah truly is that. In contrast, saying 'without body' does not negate praise or majesty, so the term 'body' is not a term of praise. Regarding 'knower' and 'powerful', denying them removes majesty, while affirming them establishes it. In everyday language, saying 'thing' does not reveal the quiddity (māhiyya) of an essence, nor do 'knower' and 'powerful' disclose the attribute itself; they merely indicate existence and that the subject is described. But 'body' specifies quiddity – a body has dimensions, directions, ends, accidents – so applying it to Allah would be inappropriate. The passage concludes that using these terms (like 'alim', 'qadir') is obligatory because scripture uses them, following a short concluding remark.

Key Terms
شيء
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that calling Allah 'shay'' (thing) implies He is like created things, or that 'jism' (body) is acceptable because He is described in the Quran as having a face and hands.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'shay'' in this context is a term of affirmation and majesty, not of limitation. 'Jism' is not a term of praise and should not be applied to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the statement 'with nothing' (bila shay') typically express?
A) Affirmation of reality and greatness
B) Denial of reality or belittlement
C) A neutral statement
D) Only a negation

Key Takeaway
The term 'shay'' when used of Allah affirms His existence and majesty, while 'jism' is not a term of praise and should not be applied to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever use casual or careless words when describing Allah? How does this lesson challenge you to be more precise in your speech about Him, even in your thoughts?
?
This lesson shows that even a term like 'body' can diminish Allah's majesty. Are there any terms or comparisons you have used (even silently) that subtly reduce Allah's greatness? What will you replace them with starting today?
?
If calling Allah 'thing' affirms His essence and majesty, how does that change the way you utter phrases like 'Allah is the Creator' or 'Allah is the All-Knowing'? Are these just words, or do they carry the weight of affirming His majesty as taught here?

The Names 'Alim' and 'Qadir': Affirming Majesty Without Defining Essence
Maturidi explains the implications of the names 'Alim' and 'Qadir' for divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the names 'Alim' (Knower) and 'Qadir' (Powerful) affirm Allah's majesty and attributes without disclosing the quiddity (māhiyya) of His essence.

Core Idea
Divine names like 'Alim' indicate existence and description, not quiddity.

Maturidi continues by applying a similar reasoning to the divine names 'Alim' and 'Qadir'. He argues that denying these names negates majesty, while affirming them establishes it. However, in human language, these names do not reveal the essence's quiddity; they simply show that the subject is described. This distinction protects Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the relevant passage
Focus on the argument about 'alim' and 'qadir'.
2
Understand the negation-affirmation logic
Denying the names negates majesty; affirming them obligates description with majesty.
3
Note the distinction between existence and quiddity
In the witnessed world, saying 'thing' indicates existence, not quiddity; similarly, 'alim' and 'qadir' indicate being described, not the essence's nature. Contrast this with 'body' which does specify quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما كان القول بلا شيء؛ يستعمل فى نفى الحقيقة أو تصغير الثابت، فثبت أن القول بالشيء إنما هو فى إثبات الذات وتعظيمه، / والله حقيق لذلك. والقول بلا بجه هم لا يوجب واحدا منهما، فكذلك القول بالجسم، ليس تثبيت واحد مما يحمد وجوده أو يعظم؛ لذلك اختلفا. وعلى ذلك القول بلا عاليم ولا قادر اسم ينفى العظمة والجلال، فمثله [؟] في العالم والقادر اينجاب الوصف بالعظمة والجلال .. وبالله التوفيق. فإنه في الشاهد لا يفهم من قول الرجل شيء مائية الذات، ولا من قوله عالم وقادر، الصفة، وإنما يفهم من الأول الوجود والهستية، ومن الثاني أنه موصوف، لا أن فيه بيان مائية الذات كقول الرجل 'جسم، إنه ذكر مائية أنه ذو أبعاد أو ذو جهات أو محتمل للنهايات وقابل للأعراض، وكذا ذا في الإنسان وسائر الأعيان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. التوفيق : وبعد، فإن القول بهذا كله واجب بما ثبت في السمع التسمية به، وبالله
With what was the saying 'with nothing' used in denying reality or belittling the established, so it is established that the saying 'thing' is only in affirming the essence and magnifying it, / and Allah is worthy of that. And the saying 'without thing' does not necessitate one of them, so likewise the saying 'body' is not establishing one of what its existence is praised or magnified; therefore they differed. And upon that, the saying 'without knower' and 'without powerful' is a name that negates majesty and grandeur, so its like [؟] in the knower and the powerful, the description with majesty and grandeur is obligated .. and with Allah is success. For in the witnessed, it is not understood from a man's saying 'thing' the quiddity of the essence, nor from his saying 'knower' and 'powerful' the attribute; rather from the first is understood existence and being, and from the second that he is described, not that it contains a statement of the quiddity of the essence, like a man's saying 'body' – he mentioned quiddity, that it has dimensions or directions or is receptive to ends and susceptible to accidents, and likewise in man and all other entities. And there is no power except with Allah. Success: and then, the saying of all this is obligatory by what is established in scripture of naming with it, and with Allah.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage discusses the semantic implications of the terms 'thing' (shay'), 'body' (jism), and the divine names 'knower' (alim) and 'powerful' (qadir). The argument focuses on how these terms are used in the witnessed realm and their applicability to Allah. The author begins by noting that the phrase 'with nothing' is used to negate reality or belittle a positive fact, so 'thing' actually affirms existence and greatness; Allah truly is that. In contrast, saying 'without body' does not negate praise or majesty, so the term 'body' is not a term of praise. Regarding 'knower' and 'powerful', denying them removes majesty, while affirming them establishes it. In everyday language, saying 'thing' does not reveal the quiddity (māhiyya) of an essence, nor do 'knower' and 'powerful' disclose the attribute itself; they merely indicate existence and that the subject is described. But 'body' specifies quiddity – a body has dimensions, directions, ends, accidents – so applying it to Allah would be inappropriate. The passage concludes that using these terms (like 'alim', 'qadir') is obligatory because scripture uses them, following a short concluding remark.

Misconception: Some might think that by calling Allah 'Knower' or 'Powerful', we are describing His essence in detail, like how we describe a human's knowledge or power.
Correction: The passage clarifies that these names only indicate that He is described, not the quiddity of His essence or attributes.

Key Takeaway
The divine names 'Alim' and 'Qadir' affirm Allah's majesty and indicate He is described, but they do not define the quiddity of His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You regularly affirm that Allah is 'al-Qādir' over all things. Is your heart truly satisfied to say 'He is described with power' without needing to grasp the nature of that power? What would change in your worship if you accepted that His reality exceeds all your concepts?
?
This lesson says divine names only tell you that Allah is described, not what that description is. Are you willing to let go of the comfort of feeling you 'know' what Allah is like, even partially? How might this deepen your reliance on revelation alone rather than your own imagination?

Another answer: 'shay' is not a name that specifies quiddity
Maturidi gives another argument that 'shay' is not a name because names specify quiddity, unlike 'alim' and 'qadir'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that 'shay' (thing) is not a name because it does not specify quiddity, and how this protects against anthropomorphism when using attributes like 'knower' and 'powerful'.

Core Idea
Names reveal essence; 'thing' does not, so it's safe to say God is a thing.

In this passage, Maturidi offers another reason why applying the term 'thing' (shay') to Allah is not problematic. He explains that true names specify the quiddity of what they name, but 'thing' does not—it is more like the attribute terms 'knower' and 'powerful', which only negate deficiencies without defining God's essence. This careful distinction helps avoid any suggestion that God resembles created beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the examples of body and man.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify the claim: 'shay' is not a name. Notice the reasoning: names have a specific property that informs of quiddity.
Examples: body defined by three dimensions, man defined as rational mortal.
3
Compare with attribute names
Contrast with 'alim' and 'qadir' which do not define quiddity but negate ignorance. Note that 'shay' is similar.
4
Grasp the precaution
Understand why the phrase 'not like others' is added to prevent understanding attributes as human-like.
This protects against tashbih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب آخر، أن الشيء ليس باسم؛ لأن لكل اسم خاصية إذا ذكرت أعلمت مائية الشيء نحو أن يقال : ما الجسم ؟ فنقول : / ما له أبعاد ثلاثة ، وما الإنسان ؟ فنذكر حده المعروف في الشاهد من الحي الناطق الميت ، أى المحتمل لذلك . وكذلك كل جوهر له حد يذكر باسم الخاصية له ، وعلى ذلك عالم قادر ، لا يذكر خاصيته بحرف يحد ذاته أو يعلم مائيته ، إنما يذكر ارتفاع الجفاء عنه ، وتأتى الأشياء له ، ولا تذكر مائية ذاته ، فجائز القول بذلك . غيرية وليس في ذلك حرف التشبيه في مائية الذات ، فخشى أن يفهم العلم والقدرة كما هما في الشاهد ؛ فقيل : لا كغيره ممن ذكر ؟. : لا كغيره ممن ذكر ؛ ليُعلم أنه بذاته عالم لا بغيره ، قادر، وبالله التوفيق
And another answer: that 'thing' is not a name, because every name has a specific property which, when mentioned, informs of the quiddity of the thing, like when it is said: 'What is the body?' and we say: 'That which has three dimensions', and 'What is man?' we mention his known definition in the witnessed: the living, rational, mortal – meaning receptive to that. And likewise every substance has a limit mentioned by the name of its specific property. And upon that, 'knower', 'powerful' does not mention its specific property with a letter that defines its essence or informs of its quiddity; rather it mentions the elevation of ignorance from it, and that things come to it, and it does not mention the quiddity of its essence. So it is permissible to say that. Otherness: and there is not in that any letter of tashbih in the quiddity of the essence, but it was feared that knowledge and power would be understood as they are in the witnessed; so it was said: 'not like others who are mentioned'? – 'not like others who are mentioned', so that it be known that He is by His essence Knower, not by another, Powerful. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage presents an additional argument for why the term 'thing' (shay') can be applied to Allah without implying anthropomorphism. The author explains that a true 'name' specifies the quiddity (māhiyya) of the thing named, such as 'body' defined as having three dimensions, or 'man' defined as a rational, mortal animal. Likewise, every substance has a definition that reveals its essential property. By contrast, the names 'knower' (alim) and 'powerful' (qadir) do not define the essence; they only negate ignorance and affirm capability. Since 'thing' also does not define quiddity, using it is permissible. To avoid misunderstanding that God's knowledge and power are like human attributes, the phrase 'not like others' is added, affirming that He is knower and powerful by His own essence, not by anything else.

Names Do Not Define God's Essence
Al-Maturidi answers that a thing is not its name; names indicate a property, not the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine names like 'Alim and Qadir do not define God's essence but rather indicate the removal of deficiency and are free from anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Divine names point to what God is not, not what He is.

Al-Maturidi continues his defense of tawhid by addressing a critical question: if we call God 'Knowing' and 'Powerful', do we mean He has knowledge and power like ours? In this passage, he distinguishes between names that define a thing's quiddity (like 'body' or 'human') and names that merely indicate the negation of imperfection. This distinction protects God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument. Notice the flow from general principle to examples to application.
2
Identify the general principle
The passage begins by stating that a thing is not its name; names have a property that informs of quiddity. Recognize this as the starting point.
This applies to all created things.
3
Examine the created-world examples
Look at how 'body' is defined by three dimensions and 'human' by being living, rational, mortal. These examples show how names define quiddity for creatures.
4
Contrast with divine names
Notice the shift when applying names to God: 'Alim and Qadir do not delimit His essence or inform of quiddity. Instead, they indicate removal of deficiency and that things come from Him.
This is the key point.
5
Understand the anti-anthropomorphism clause
The author explains the phrase 'la ka-ghayrihi' (not like others) to prevent understanding God's knowledge and power as human-like. Reflect on how this preserves tawhid.
This guards against tashbih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب آخر، أن الشيء ليس باسم؛ لأن لكل اسم خاصية إذا ذكرت أعلمت مائية الشيء نحو أن يقال: ما الجسم؟ فنقول: ما له أبعاد ثلاثة، وما الإنسان؟ فنذكر حده المعروف في الشاهد من الحي الناطق الميت، أي المحتمل لذلك. وكذلك كل جوهر له حد يذكر باسم الخاصية له، وعلى ذلك عالم قادر، لا يذكر خاصيته بحرف يحد ذاته أو يعلم مائيته، إنما يذكر ارتفاع الجفاء عنه، وتأتي الأشياء له، ولا تذكر مائية ذاته، فجائز القول بذلك. غيرية وليس في ذلك حرف التشبيه في مائية الذات، فخشي أن يفهم العلم والقدرة كما هما في الشاهد؛ فقيل: لا كغيره ممن ذكر؛ ليُعلم أنه بذاته عالم لا بغيره، قادر، وبالله التوفيق.
And another answer: that a thing is not by its name; because every name has a property which, when mentioned, informs of the quiddity of the thing, as when it is asked: 'What is the body?' We say: 'That which has three dimensions.' And what is man? We mention his known definition in the witnessed realm: the living, rational, mortal — i.e., capable of that. And likewise, every substance has a boundary mentioned by the name of its property. And for such as 'Alim, Qadir, one does not mention His property by a letter that delimits His essence or informs of His quiddity. Rather, one mentions the removal of deficiency from Him, and that things come to Him, and one does not mention the quiddity of His essence. So it is permissible to say that. And there is no letter of resemblance in the quiddity of the essence in that, so it was feared that knowledge and power might be understood as they are in the witnessed realm; so it was said: 'Not like others who are mentioned; so that it be known that He is by His essence knowing, not by another, powerful. And with Allah is success.'
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a question about divine names. The author explains that names in the created world indicate a defining property (khassiyya) that informs of the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing. For example, 'body' is defined by having three dimensions, and 'human' by being living, rational, mortal. However, for God, names like 'Alim (knowing) and Qadir (powerful) do not define His essence or quiddity; rather, they indicate the negation of deficiency (jafa') and that He is not like created things. The passage warns against anthropomorphism (tashbih) by asserting that God's knowledge and power are not like human knowledge and power. The phrase 'la ka-ghayrihi' (not like others) is used to affirm that He is knowing by His essence, not by another. The conclusion is that it is permissible to use such names for God without defining His essence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that divine names like 'Alim define God's essence in the same way that 'body' defines a body's quiddity.
Correction: The passage explains that for God, names like 'Alim do not define quiddity but rather indicate the removal of deficiency (irtifa' al-jafa') and that He is not like others.

Checkpoint

D) It implies that God has knowledge similar to human knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Divine names like 'Alim and Qadir do not define God's essence but rather negate deficiency and emphasize His incomparability, avoiding anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You often ask Allah for things, trusting in His power. But do you secretly imagine His power as similar to a powerful king's? What would it take to strip away that image and rely on a power that is 'not like others'—a power that you cannot conceive of at all? The Four Meanings of 'The One' (al-Wahid)
Al-Maturidi classifies the meaning of 'the One' into four types, the last being exclusively for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four meanings of the term 'the One' (al-wahid) as classified by Imam al-Maturidi, and why the fourth meaning applies exclusively to Allah.

Core Idea
God's oneness is not like any created oneness; it is beyond all categories.

Imam al-Maturidi was asked about the meaning of 'the One'. His answer reveals a profound classification that distinguishes God's unity from all created oneness. In this lesson, we will unpack these four meanings and see how they culminate in the unknowable transcendence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the four-fold structure.
2
Identify the four categories
The passage enumerates four meanings: kull (whole), juz' (part), an intermediate, and a fourth that is exclusively Allah's. Find each one and notice the key Arabic terms.
The first two are straightforward; the third is more subtle.
3
Understand why the fourth is unique
The fourth meaning is described with terms like 'He is and He is not He' and then a string of negations about speech, expression, imagination, and intellect. Reflect on why such language is used.
This shows transcendence beyond all created categories.
4
Connect to tawhid
Ask yourself: How does this classification help safeguard the tawhid of Allah from being compared to created things?
It establishes that God's oneness is sui generis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وسئل واحد عن معنى الواحد؛ قال: ينصرف على أربعة: كل لا يحتمل التضعيف، وجزء لا يحتمل التنصيف، والذي بينهما يحتمل الوجهين: كارتفاعه عما لا يتنصف وانحطاطه عما لا يتضعف؛ إذ لا شيء وراء الكل، والرابع هو الذي قام به الثلاثة هو ولا هو هو أخفى من هو، والذي الخرس عنه اللسان، وانقطع دونه البيان، وانحسرت عنه الأوهام، وحارت فيه الأفهام، فذلك الله رب العالمين.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And one was asked about the meaning of 'the One' (al-wahid). He said: It applies in four ways: (1) an entirety that does not admit of doubling, (2) a part that does not admit of halving, (3) that which is between them and admits of both aspects, like its rising above what cannot be halved and its descent below what cannot be doubled, for there is nothing beyond the entirety; and the fourth is that which possesses the three: He is and He is not He, more hidden than 'He'; the tongue is speechless about it, expression falls short of it, imaginations are perplexed by it, and intellects are bewildered about it. That is Allah, the Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the term 'one' (al-wahid). The author gives four meanings. The first is an indivisible whole (kull) that cannot be doubled. The second is a part (juz') that cannot be halved. The third is something intermediate that can be considered either way, like a line with two ends? The fourth is unique to God: it is that which possesses the three attributes (perhaps existence, knowledge, life?) yet 'He is and He is not He' — meaning that the essential identity is ineffable. The passage emphasizes that God's oneness transcends all created categories, leaving the intellect bewildered.

Key Terms

كل
جزء
تضعيف
تنصيف
الخرس
البيان
الأوهام
الأفهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think God's oneness is like the numerical unity of a created object, e.g., one of a kind.
Correction: God's oneness transcends all created categories; it is the fourth meaning that is beyond comprehension.

Checkpoint
How many meanings of 'al-wahid' are given in this passage, and which one is exclusively for Allah?
A) Three; the third.
B) Four; the fourth.
C) Two; the second.
D) Five; the fifth.

Key Takeaway
The oneness of Allah is unlike any created oneness; it is a unique, ineffable reality that leaves the intellect helpless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If the tongue is speechless about Allah's oneness, what does that say about the way you speak about Him? How can you practice more silence and awe in your daily remembrance of Allah?

Divine Attributes

Divine names affirmed by revelation and reason
Al-Maturidi affirms divine names from revelation and reason, refuting those who divert them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine names are established by both revelation and reason, and that denying them leads to ta'til (negation) and false resemblance.

Core Idea
Divine names are true from both revelation and reason—denying them leads to error.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi explains that the beautiful names of Allah are not arbitrary; they are affirmed by divine revelation and rational necessity. He also refutes those who fear anthropomorphism and thus misdirect these names. The lesson clarifies the correct methodology for understanding divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: affirmation, revelation, refutation, reason.
The middle part is corrupted; read the translation for clarity.
2
Identify the two sources of divine names
Al-Maturidi states that naming Allah with attributes like Qadir, 'Alim, etc. is true from both revelation (sam') and reason ('aql). Revelation comes from the Quran and previous scriptures. Reason is based on God's distinct creation indicating His act of choice.
3
Understand the refutation
Some people divert these names to other than Allah, fearing that affirming names implies resemblance (tashbih). Al-Maturidi argues that denial itself leads to ta'til and also implies resemblance to nameless things—which is worse.
4
Reflect on the rational argument
Reason necessitates these names because God's creation differs in essence and attributes, showing His act is by choice, not nature. This confirms His attributes of power, knowledge, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم الوصف لله بأنه قادر عالم حي كريم جواد والتسمية بها حق من السمع والعقل جميعا. فالسمع ما جاء به القرآن وسائر كتب الله، وسمى بالذي ذكرت الرسل والخلائق كل منهم، إلا أن قوما وجهوا تلك الأسماء إلى غيره؛ ظنا منهم أن في إثبات الإسم تشابها بينه وبين كل مسمى، ولو كان ذلك لكان بنفي التعطيل ذلك، وبنفيه أيضا تشابه بينه وبين ما لا يدخل تحت اسم، وهو ما ليس كذلك، ولكن قد بينا بعد التشابه لموافقة الإسم، فهو مسمى بما سمى به نفسه، موصوف بما وصف به نفسه. والعقل يوجب ذلك، لأن الله سبحانه إذ ثبت عنه مختلف الخلق بجوهره وصفاته دل أن فعله ليس بفعل الطباع بل هو فعل الاختيار
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then describing Allah as Qadir, 'Alim, Hayy, Karim, Jawad — and naming Him by these is true from both revelation and reason. As for revelation, it is what the Quran and all of Allah's books have brought, and He is named by what the messengers and all creatures have mentioned, except that some people directed those names to other than Him, supposing that in affirming the name there is resemblance between Him and every named thing. And if that were the case, then by negating [the names] that would be [the case], and by its negation also there would be resemblance between Him and what does not fall under a name — and that is not so. But we have already explained the resemblance after agreement of the name? He is named by what He named Himself, described by what He described Himself. And reason necessitates that, because since Allah, may He be exalted, has established the creation as different by His essence and attributes, it indicates that His act is not the act of nature but the act of choice.
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the basis of divine names. Al-Maturidi argues that names like Qadir, 'Alim, etc., are established by both revelation (sam') and reason ('aql). Revelation provides the names through the Quran and previous scriptures. Reason also confirms them because God's distinct creation with different essences and attributes shows that His actions are by choice, not by nature. The passage refutes those who transfer these names to others (like natural causes) out of fear of anthropomorphism. The author counters that if one denies these names, that leads to ta'til (negation) and also creates a resemblance between God and things that have no names. Ultimately, God is named by what He named Himself.

Key Terms

عقل
اسم
تشبيه
تعطيل
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that affirming divine names like 'Powerful' or 'Knowing' necessarily implies that Allah resembles created beings who have similar names.
Correction: Al-Maturidi clarifies that affirmation of names does not imply tashbih; rather, denial leads to ta'til and false resemblance. Names are affirmed as Allah has named Himself, without likeness.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the consequence of denying divine names due to fear of resemblance?
A) It leads to affirming that God is like nothing.
B) It leads to ta'til (negation) and also implies resemblance to things without names.
C) It leads to perfect tawhid.
D) It leads to disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Divine names are established by both revelation and reason; denying them leads to ta'til and false resemblance, so affirm them as Allah describes Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite a name of Allah like 'Al-Qadir' (the All-Powerful), do you truly internalize that He has absolute power over every detail of your life, or do you subtly reduce it to a concept? What would change in your daily decisions if you lived as if that name were literally true?
?
Al-Maturidi says that reason itself proves Allah's attributes. Do you rely more on revealed texts or rational reflection when strengthening your faith? If there is an imbalance, how can you deliberately cultivate the other source?
?
This lesson warns that the fear of making Allah like creation can lead to denying His attributes altogether. Have you ever caught yourself hesitating to affirm a divine attribute because it felt uncomfortable? What would it cost you to embrace it fully?

Divine Names Established by Revelation and Reason
Maturidi affirms divine names via sam' and 'aql and refutes tashbih concerns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Maturidi holds that divine names are established by both revelation and reason, and how he refutes the objection that naming implies similarity.

Core Idea
God's names are true by revelation and reason; denying them leads to worse error.

Imam al-Maturidi begins this passage by declaring that naming God with attributes like Qadir, 'Alim, etc., is valid from two sources: sam' (revelation) and 'aql (reason). He then addresses a common objection that naming implies anthropomorphism, and provides rational proof that God's acts are by choice, not nature. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation together. Notice the flow from affirmation to argument to refutation.
Pay attention to the structure: the author starts with a thesis, then supports it with two sources.
2
Identify the two sources of divine names
Maturidi explicitly names sam' (revelation) and 'aql (reason) as the bases for knowing God's names. Distinguish how each is used.
Sam' includes the Qur'an and previous scriptures; 'aql provides independent rational proof.
3
Understand the objection and refutation
Some people redirect divine names to others, fearing tashbih (similarity). Maturidi refutes by showing that denying names leads to ta'til (negation) which also implies similarity to nameless things.
The key is that God names Himself, so there is no real similarity.
4
Examine the rational proofs
Maturidi gives two arguments: (1) diversity of creation from one essence shows action by choice, not nature; (2) consistency of action without corruption indicates choice.
These proofs establish that God truly creates, and thus His attributes are real.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this passage guards against both tashbih (anthropomorphism) and ta'til (denial of attributes), establishing a balanced middle path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الوصف لله بأنه قادر عالم حى كريم جواد والتسمية بها حق من السمع والعقل جميعا . فالسمع ما جاء به القرآن وسائر كتب الله ، وسمى بالذي ذكرت الرسل والخلائق كل منهم ، إلا أن قوما وجهوا تلك الأسماء إلى غيره ؛ ظنا منهم أن في إثبات الإسم تشابها بينه وبين كل مسمى ، ولو كان ولو كان به ذلك لكان بنفى التعطيل ذلك ، وبنفيه أيضا تشابه بينه وبين ما لا يدخل تحت اسم ، وهو ما ليس كذلك ، ولكن قد بينا بعد التشابه لموافقة الإسم ، فهو مُسمى بما سمى به نفسه ، موصوف بما وصف به نفسه . والعقل يوجب ذلك ، لأن الله سبحانه إذ ثبت عنه مختلف الخلق بجوهره وصفاته دل أن فعله ليس بفعل الطباع بل هو فعل الاختيار . وأيضا أن اتساق الفعل المتوالى - بلا فساد يظهر ، ولا خروج عن طريق يثبت كون المفعول بالإختيار من الفاعل ، فثبت أن الخلق كان يفعله حقيقة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the description of God as Powerful, Knowing, Living, Generous, Liberal, and naming with them is true from both revelation (sam') and reason ('aql). As for revelation (sam'), it is what the Qur'an and all the books of God brought, and He named with what the messengers and all the creatures mentioned, except that a people directed those names to others, thinking that in affirming the name there is similarity (tashbih) between Him and every named. And if that were the case, then by negating [the names] on account of ta'til (negation of attributes) it would be that, and by negating it also there would be similarity between Him and what does not fall under a name, which is not the case. But we have already explained that there is no similarity for the agreement of the name; He is named with what He named Himself, described with what He described Himself. And reason necessitates that, because God (exalted is He) when it is established that He created various creatures with His essence and attributes, indicates that His action is not the action of nature (tab'), but rather it is the action of choice (ikhtiyar). And also that the consistency of successive action—without apparent corruption nor deviation from a path—establishes that the act is by choice from the agent; thus it is established that creation is truly His action, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage defines the source and validity of divine names and attributes. Imam al-Maturidi begins by affirming that describing God with names like Qadir (Powerful), 'Alim (Knowing), Hayy (Living), Karim (Generous), and Jawad (Liberal) is correct by both revelation (sam') and reason ('aql). He explains that revelation comes from the Qur'an and previous scriptures; God has named Himself as the messengers and all creatures have mentioned. However, some people (likely the Mu'tazila or Jahmiyya) direct these names to other meanings, fearing that affirming names implies similarity (tashbih) between God and created beings. Maturidi refutes this by arguing that denying names leads to ta'til (negation of attributes), which also implies similarity to what has no name—an absurdity. He clarifies that there is no real similarity because God names Himself as He wills. Then he provides two rational proofs: First, the diversity of creation with one essence and attributes shows that God's act is not from nature (tab') but from choice (ikhtiyar). Second, the consistent and orderly sequence of actions without corruption indicates that the actor acts by choice. The conclusion is that creation is truly God's act. This lesson guards against two extremes: tashbih (anthropomorphism) and ta'til (denial of attributes).

العقل
التسمية
التشابه
التعطيل

الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming divine names implies anthropomorphism (tashbih) because names are shared with creatures.
Correction: Maturidi argues that denying names leads to an even worse error (ta'til), and that God names Himself in a way that does not entail similarity. The names are true without resembling created names in essence.

Checkpoint
What two sources does Imam al-Maturidi use to establish that the divine names are true?
A) Consensus (ijma') and analogy (qiyas)
B) Revelation (sam') and reason ('aql)
C) Intuition and experience
D) The reports of the Companions

Key Takeaway
God's names are true by revelation and reason; denying them leads to ta'til, while affirming them with correct understanding avoids tashbih.

What would change if you truly believed He has absolute power over that situation, and what is one practical step you can take today to rely on that name?
?
The lesson warns that the fear of comparing God to creation can lead to denying His attributes altogether. Is there an attribute of Allah you feel uncomfortable affirming because it challenges your assumptions? How would your relationship with Him deepen if you embraced that attribute fully, even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
Maturidi argues that the consistency and order in creation prove that God acts by choice, not by blind nature. When you face chaos or suffering, do you still see the hand of a wise Chooser, or do you doubt His wisdom? What can you do this week to remind yourself that every event is by Allah's deliberate choice?

Proof of God's Choice from Alternation and Creation Ex Nihilo
Maturidi presents two proofs for God's choice: alternation of states and creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the alternation of states and creation from nothing to prove that God's actions are by choice.

Core Idea
God's ability to reverse and create from nothing proves His choice, not nature.

Maturidi begins his rational demonstration that God acts by choice, not by necessity. He points to observable phenomena—creation, annihilation, and restoration—which cannot be the work of blind nature. He then invokes the argument from creation ex nihilo, which only a being with absolute choice can accomplish. This lesson presents those two foundational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style with multiple 'وأيضا' transitions.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two separate proofs for choice: the alternation of states (night/day) and creation from nothing. Find where each ends.
The first proof ends at 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' and the second at 'جل ربنا عن ذلك وتعالى'.
3
Analyse the alternation proof
Maturidi argues that God creates, annihilates, and restores (like night and day). Why does this show choice? Because natural forces cannot produce opposite states.
Consider how a natural force like fire only burns; it cannot unburn.
4
Analyse the creation ex nihilo proof
The world's origination from nothing is only possible for a being with utmost choice. Nature acts by compulsion, not free will.
This aligns with the kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله تعالى إذ أنشأ شيء ثم أفناه ، وفيه أيضا ما قد أعاده نحو الليل والنهار ، ثبت أن فعله بالإختيار ؛ إذ تحقق به صلاح ما قد أفسده ، وإعادة ما قد أفناه ، وإيجاد المعدوم واعدام الموجود ، فثبت أن طريق ذلك الإختيار ؛ إذ من كان الذى منه يكون بالطبع لا يجيء منه نفي ما يوجد ، وإيجاد ما يعلمه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا إنا قد بينا حدث العالم لا من شيء ، وذلك نوع ما لا يبلغه إلا فعل من هو في غاية معنى الإختيار ، وما يكون بالطبع فحقه الإضطرار ، ومحال أن يكون من يبلغ شأنه إلى إنشاء الأشياء لا من شيء ، ثم يكون ذلك بالطبع ما كان وقوع الشيء بالطبع هو تحت قهر آخر ، وجعله بحيث يسقط عنه الإمكان ، وذلك آية الحدث وأمارة الضعف ، جل ربنا عن ذلك وتعالى . ما جرى التعارف المتوارث من الخلق بالدعوات والتضرع إلى الله تعالى بالفرج مع وأنه قهر كذا ونصر كذا ، وأعان فلانا وخذل فلانا ، وأن كل ذي قوة يفعل بقوة أنشأها ، ولا ينال شيء من ذلك بالمضطر ، ولا يرغب فيه ؛ دل ذلك على أن العالم باختياره . فإذا ثبت الإختيار ثبتت له القدرة على الخلق ، والإرادة لكونه على ما هو عليه ؛ لأن من لا قدرة له يخرج الذى يكون منه مضطربا فاسدا ، ولا يملك الشيء وضده، فثبت أن ما كان منه بقدرة كان واختيار ، وذلك أمارات الفعل الحقيقية في الشاهد الذى هو أصل للعلم بالغائب ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى ما ذكرنا من تواصل الفعل - أعنى الواقع به بالفعل - وتتابعه محكما هو الدليل أنه كان فعله على العلم به، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أنه إذ خرج كل الجواهر التي لا يمتحن في مصالح الممتحنين ، وخلق كل شيء أريد به البقاء ، مع خلق ما به بقاؤه ، علم أنه كان بمن كيفية كل شيء وحاجته وما به القوام والمعاش ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن الله سبحانه خلق الخلق خلقا دل على حدثه ، وعلى أن له محدثا ، وعلى وحدانية محدثه ، فلولا أن علم بالخلق - يعلم أنه إذا خلق على ما خلق كان فيه دليل العلم به وبخلقه - لا يحتمل أن يخرج على ذلك خلقه ، وبالله التوفيق
And also that God (exalted is He) when He created a thing then annihilated it, and also in it He has restored it like night and day, it is established that His action is by choice, because by it He achieves the rectification of what He corrupted, the restoration of what He annihilated, the bringing into existence of the non-existent and the annihilating of the existent. So it is established that the way of that is choice, for he from whom [action] occurs by nature cannot bring about the negation of what exists and the existence of what he knows, and there is no power except with God. And also we have already explained the origination (huduth) of the world from nothing, and that is a kind that no one can achieve except an act of one who is in the extreme sense of choice. And that which is by nature, its right is necessity, and it is impossible that the state of the one whose affair reaches the creation of things from nothing, then that is by nature, since the occurrence of a thing by nature is under the compulsion of another, making it such that possibility falls away from it; that is a sign of origination and a mark of weakness. Our Lord is exalted above that. What has occurred of inherited customary practice among creation of supplications and earnest prayers to God (exalted is He) for relief, and that He overpowers such and such, helps such and such, and aids so-and-so and forsakes so-and-so, and that every possessor of power acts with a power that He created, and nothing of that is attained by the compelled one, nor is it desired—that indicates that the world is by His choice. So when choice is established, then power over creation is established for Him, and will (irada) for His being as He is. Because whoever has no power, his action comes out disturbed and corrupt, and he does not possess a thing and its opposite. So it is established that what comes from Him by power is by choice, and that is the indicator of true action in the witnessed world (shahid) which is the basis for knowledge of the unseen (gha'ib). And there is no power except with God. And according to what we mentioned of the continuity of action—I mean occurring by it actually—and its sequence being perfect, it is the evidence that His action was with knowledge of it. And there is no power except with God. And also that since every substance that is not tested in the interests of those tested, and He created everything intended for permanence, along with creating what is necessary for its permanence, it is known that He knows the 'how' of everything and its need and what sustains it and livelihood. And there is no power except with God. And also that God (exalted is He) created creatures in a way that indicates their origination, and that they have a creator, and the oneness of their creator. So if He had no knowledge of creation—knowing that when He creates in a certain way, there is in it evidence of knowledge of Him and His creation—it would not be possible for His creation to come out in that way. And success is from God.
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rational proofs that God's actions are by choice (ikhtiyar), then derives the attributes of power (qudra), will (irada), and knowledge ('ilm). The author begins with the observation that God creates things and then annihilates them, then restores them (like the cycle of night and day). This ability to alternate states shows choice, because natural processes cannot reverse themselves in such a purposeful manner. Next, he appeals to creation ex nihilo (huduth al-‘alam la min shay'), which is an act only possible for a being with utmost choice; nature acts by necessity and compulsion. He then points to the universal practice of supplication (du'a) and prayer to God for specific outcomes—people naturally seek help from God, not from a compelled force, indicating that God acts by choice. From choice, he infers power and will: choice implies the ability to perform or not perform an act (qudra) and the specific determination of that act (irada). He argues that without power, action would be disordered, and that true power enables a thing and its opposite. Then he uses the consistent and flawless order in creation to prove that God acts with knowledge ('ilm). The argument is based on the principle that an orderly, purposeful sequence cannot arise from ignorance; it must be known. Finally, he notes that the very fact that creation points to its own createdness and to the oneness of its Creator presupposes knowledge on the part of the Creator. This lesson demonstrates the classical kalam method of proving divine attributes through rational inference from observable phenomena.

Key Terms
الاختيار
الطبع
الاضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions could be by nature (tab') like natural forces.
Correction: Nature is constrained and cannot reverse states or create from nothing, whereas God's choice is free and all-powerful.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the alternation of night and day demonstrate?
A) That God is eternal
B) That God's action is by choice
C) That nature is powerful
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
God's alternation of states and creation from nothing are clear signs that He acts by choice, not by nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the alternation of night and day, do you see it as a mere natural cycle or as a sign of God's deliberate choice? How does this change your awareness of His control over every moment of your life? What would it mean for your trust in Him if you truly believed that each sunrise is a fresh act of His will?
?
The passage says that nature cannot reverse its effects—fire only burns, it never unburns. In your own life, when you experience something that seems irreversible (a mistake, a loss), do you despair because you see it as a fixed 'natural' outcome, or do you turn to the One who can 'rectify what He corrupted'? What area of your life today needs you to trust in God's ability to reverse and restore?
?
Creation from nothing is an act only an absolutely free being can perform. If God truly brought you into existence from nothing, then your whole being is a product of His choice, not chance. If you really internalized that, would you still live as if your time, talents, and decisions are your own? What would you need to surrender to Him today because of this truth?
