Divine Action as Perfection: Maturidi's First Proof
Maturidi proves that God's action is perfect and not confined to time or place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's action is perfect because it transcends limitations of time, place, and instruments.

Core Idea
God's action transcends all limitations; describing it otherwise is describing incapacity.

This passage from Imam Maturidi establishes a foundational principle for understanding divine action. He contrasts limited human action with God's perfect action, arguing that describing God with an action confined to a specific time or place would imply imperfection. Instead, God's action is by His essence, unrestricted and creative from nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its rhythm. Then read the literal translation to understand the argument's flow.
2
Identify the principle about exceeding reality
Maturidi begins with a principle: if an agent's action does not exceed the actual moment/place, describing them by that action is a description of incapacity. If it does exceed, it is a description of power.
This sets up a contrast between limited and unrestricted action.
3
Understand the analogy with human agents
He uses examples from the witnessed world: an agent who can produce both a thing and its opposite is more perfect; an agent whose action is confined to one sphere is inferior. This illustrates degrees of perfection.
This analogy helps apply the principle to God.
4
See the conclusion about God's essence and creation from nothing
Maturidi contrasts human action (time-bound, requiring instruments) with divine action (by God's essence, creating from nothing). This shows God's perfection and invalidates opposing views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أن الذي لا يعدو الواقع يفعله وقت الوصف له بالفعل وصف عجز، والذي يعدوه ويقع عنده وصف قدرة، كمن يكون منه فعل الشيء وضده المتمكن منه أنه أتم من جهة فعله. وكذلك من لا يعدو فعله حيزه هو دون من يقع فعله في كل حيز، كذلك وصف الله بالذي ذكرت؛ إذ هو وصف التمام. مع ما لا يقع فعل العبد لغير وقته لأنه عن شغله بالفعل يكون وبالآلات، والله سبحانه بنفسه يفعل، وذلك كما علم سبحانه بذاته وقدر بذاته، وكل من سواه بغير الذي ذكرت لولا ذلك ما قام به فعل، والله هو ينشئ من لا شيء لذلك بطل التقدير بالذي قالوا. وعلى مثل ما ذكرت أمر القدرة والإرادة وجميع ما بينا.
And the principle is that the one who does not exceed reality does it at the time of the description, so describing him by the action is a description of incapacity. And the one who exceeds it and [the action] occurs with him is a description of power, like one from whom occurs the thing and its opposite, being capable of it, that he is more perfect in terms of his action. Likewise, one whose action does not exceed its sphere is inferior to one whose action occurs in every sphere. Similarly, the description of God by what I mentioned is the description of perfection. Moreover, the action of the servant does not occur at other than its time because it is through his occupation with the action and with instruments, while God, glorified be He, acts by Himself, just as He knows by His essence and has power by His essence. And everyone other than Him is other than what I mentioned; were it not for that, no action would subsist by him. And God creates from nothing; therefore the estimation of what they said is invalid. And upon the like of what I mentioned is the matter of power and will and all that we have explained.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage contains the first proof regarding divine action. Maturidi begins by establishing a principle: describing an agent by an action that does not go beyond the realm of the actual is a description of incapacity, because it is confined. Conversely, if the action exceeds that realm and occurs in various places, it indicates power. He then draws an analogy: in the witnessed world, an agent who can produce both a thing and its opposite is more perfect than one who cannot. Similarly, an agent whose action is confined to its own sphere is inferior to one whose action pervades every sphere. Applying this to God, Maturidi argues that describing God with the kind of action that transcends all limitations is a description of perfection. He contrasts human action, which is tied to specific times and requires instruments and occupation, with divine action, which is by God's own essence, just as His knowledge and power are by His essence. If God were not like this, no action could proceed from Him. Since God creates from nothing, the opposing view is invalid. Maturidi concludes that the same reasoning applies to power, will, and all other attributes he has discussed.

Key Terms
وصف العجز
وصف القدرة
وصف التمام
ينشئ من لا شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that divine action being 'perfection' implies anthropomorphism, likening God to human agents.
Correction: Maturidi is not anthropomorphizing; he is contrasting confinement (a sign of incapacity) with transcendence (a sign of perfection). God's action is by His essence, not through instruments or time, so it is incomparable.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is describing God by an action that does not exceed reality a description of incapacity?
A) Because it limits God to a specific time and place, like a creature.
B) Because God does not act at all.
C) Because it implies God is similar to humans.
D) Because it denies God's power.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first proof shows that describing God's action as confined to specific times/places would be a description of incapacity; rather, God's action is perfect, by His essence, and creates from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of God's power as just being greater than yours. But Maturidi says describing God like that would be describing incapacity. Where in your life have you secretly imagined God as limited—perhaps waiting for you to pray, or needing your worship to be complete? How does this lesson expose that assumption, and what will you change today to repent of that limiting thought?
?
The passage says human action is tied to instruments and time, but God acts by His essence. When you feel your own actions are stretched thin or dependent on conditions, do you turn to a God who is bound by the same limitations? Have you been treating your dua as if God needs effort or time to respond? How will you shift your reliance to the God whose action is perfect and instantaneous?
?
Maturidi's proof refutes the estimation that confines God's action. In your heart, have you quietly placed boundaries on what God can do in your life—maybe thinking a habit is too strong, a relationship too broken, or a sin too great? How does knowing God creates from nothing shatter that estimation? What step will you take today to live as if God truly acts without limits?

Generated Acts and Divine Power: Maturidi's Second Proof
Maturidi argues from generated acts to divine power, addressing objections about natural causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses generated acts (al-fi'l al-mutawallid) to argue that divine power extends beyond immediate action, and how he addresses objections using analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
God's power is not limited by time or physical generation.

Imam Maturidi presents a second proof for divine power, drawing on everyday human actions like shooting an arrow. Even though the effect (e.g., hitting the target) occurs after the immediate act, the agent is still called the killer or shooter. This shows that an action can be attributed to an agent even when the result is delayed. Maturidi then extends this analogy to God's action, while carefully noting that God's action is not natural or generative. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for how we speak about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the example of shooting and crimes. Pay attention to how Maturidi presents the proof and then addresses an objection.
The passage contains a compact argument; reading twice is recommended.
2
Identify the argument structure
Break down Maturidi's reasoning: premise (generated acts in the witnessed world), analogy to the unseen (divine action), objection (God's action is not natural or generative), and resolution (the analogy holds because the existence of two aspects does not negate the reality of the act).
Look for the phrase 'أحد الوجهين' which signals the two aspects.
3
Connect to the broader theme
Relate this proof to the earlier proof about affirming 'thing' for God without corporeality. Maturidi uses similar analogical reasoning to allow terms like 'thing' for God, even though God is not an accident. See how he builds a consistent methodology.
This passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله', a typical closing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل آخر: أنه يوجد من العبد الفعل المتولد، يقع الفراغ بعده بأوقات كالرمي والجنايات، يستحق اسم القاتل والجاني والمصيب بعد انقضاء حقيقة فعله قبله مستقيم من مستقيم من الله، وإن كان لا يوصف فعله بالطباع والتولد؛ لما أن خروج أحد الوجهين في الشاهد لم يمنع من تحقيق الفعل، فمثله في الغائب، وإن لم يكن من ذلك الوجه على ما بينا من إثبات شيء ليس بجسم، على جواز القول في الله بالشيء وإن لم يكن عرضا، وكل شيء في الشاهد غير جسم فهو عرض بحق الوجود لا أن ذلك اسمه، فمثله الأول، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And another proof: that from the servant occurs the generated act, after which [the effect] occurs at times, like shooting and crimes; he deserves the name of killer, criminal, and hitting [the target] after the conclusion of the real action before it, straight from straight from God. Even if his action is not described by nature and generation, because the emergence of one of the two aspects in the witnessed [world] does not prevent the realization of the action, so likewise in the unseen. Even if it is not from that aspect, according to what we have explained about affirming a thing that is not a body, upon the permissibility of saying concerning God 'thing' even if it is not an accident. And every thing in the witnessed [world] that is not a body is an accident by right of existence, not that that is its name. So the like of that for the first [i.e., the unseen]. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof. Maturidi points to generated acts (al-fi'l al-mutawallid) in human experience, such as shooting an arrow or committing a crime. Even though the direct physical action is completed before the effect (e.g., the arrow hits the target after the release), the agent is still called 'killer' or 'hitter'. This shows that an act can be attributed to an agent even when the effect is temporally separated from the immediate act. In the same way, divine action should not be limited to a specific moment. Maturidi then addresses an objection: although God's action is not natural or generative (bi-al-tiba' wa al-tawallud), the analogy holds because the watched example of two aspects (direct act and delayed effect) does not negate the reality of the act. He then connects this to his earlier proof that we can affirm a thing for God that is not a body; similarly, we can say God is a 'thing' even if that thing is not an accident. Just as in the witnessed world, any non-body is called an accident by virtue of its existence (not as its proper name), so we can apply the term 'thing' to God analogically. The lesson concludes with the formula 'لا قوة إلا بالله'.

Key Terms
الفعل المتولد
الطباع والتولد
الشيء
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's action involves temporal delay or natural generation like human acts.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the analogy does not imply nature or generation; it only shows that an agent can be attributed an action even if the effect is not immediate.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi use the example of shooting and killing to argue about divine action?
A) To show that action can be attributed even after the immediate act.
B) To prove that God is a killer.
C) To deny that human acts are real.
D) To argue that only God acts.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses generated acts to show that divine power is not limited by temporal or physical conditions, and he reinforces the principle of analogical reasoning in theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You call yourself a believer, but do you truly live as though every outcome—every success, every failure—is directly from Allah, or do you secretly credit your own skill, timing, or luck? This lesson on generated acts forces you to ask: When you accomplish something, who do you really thank in your heart? Would your gratitude survive if the result were delayed or reversed?
?
Maturidi argues that your act and its effect are distinct but both attributed to you. Yet the ultimate power is God's. Reflect on a recent sin: Was your focus only on the immediate action, or did you consider that the very harm caused was also from Allah? Does that make you more humble in repentance, or do you still feel your sin is 'yours' in a way that excuses God's role? This lesson strips away your excuses.
?
You habitually compare God to things you know—a king, a father, a judge. But Maturidi warns that the analogy is limited and must not imply nature or generation. Honestly, do your mental images of God reduce Him to a superhuman being subject to time and emotion? This passage demands you break those idols of the mind and submit to the God who is beyond all likeness.

Third Proof: The Sign of Incapacity
Maturidi argues that opponents' view is a sign of incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the opponents' analogy reveals human incapacity and cannot be applied to God.

Core Idea
Human action depends on bodily movement; God's action does not.

This lesson introduces Maturidi's third proof against opponents who try to compare God's action to human action. Maturidi argues that their reasoning backfires: it exposes human limitation, not divine. By examining the necessity of self-motion for human acts, you will see how the analogy breaks down.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: a claim followed by a comparison.
The phrase 'أمارة العجز' (sign of incapacity) is central.
2
Identify the key logical move
Notice that Maturidi says the opponents' view is a sign of incapacity. He supports this by stating that a servant cannot act without using his own self-motion (moving and stilling). This shows that human action is tied to bodily movement.
3
Reflect on the implication for God
If God's action were analogous to human action, God would also need a body and self-motion. That would limit God. Maturidi's point is that the analogy leads to absurdity, so it must be rejected.
Read the concluding phrase: 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except with God).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإيضا إن الذي قالوا أمارة العجز، إذ لا يقدر العبد على ما لا يتحقق مفعوله معه، كما لا يقدر عليه دون استعمال نفسه بالتحريك والتسكين، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, what they said is a sign of incapacity, for the servant is not able to do something whose effect is not realized with him just as he is not able to do it without using himself by moving and stilling. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage contains the third brief but pointed proof. Maturidi asserts that the opponents' claim (probably that God's action must be like human action, i.e., immediately conjoined with the effect) is actually a sign of incapacity. He reasons that the human servant (al-'abd) cannot perform an act unless its effect is realized together with his direct effort. For example, a servant cannot move an object without simultaneously moving his own body (self-motion). This shows that human action is bound to immediate bodily movement. If one were to apply this condition to God, it would limit Him, implying incapacity. Maturidi thereby refutes the opponent's analogy.

Key Terms
أمارة العجز
التحريك والتسكين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is denying human free will by emphasizing that the servant cannot act without self-motion.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying human agency; he is contrasting human limitation (needing bodily motion) with divine transcendence. The point is that God's action is not comparable to human action.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say the opponents' view indicates?
A) A sign of incapacity.
B) A sign of power.
C) A sign of wisdom.
D) A sign of ignorance.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' analogy of God's action to human action actually demonstrates human incapacity, reinforcing that God's power is incomparable and all power is from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your daily actions—your work, your worship, your relationships—do you truly feel that 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except with God)? In what specific moment do you find yourself forgetting this dependence and acting as if your own effort is sufficient? What will you do today to remind yourself that your ability to move or still even a finger is a gift from Him?
?
The lesson teaches that the opponents' analogy revealed their own mental limitation. Is there a time when you have assumed God must act within the boundaries of your own experience or understanding? How does that assumption limit your trust in His power? Commit to one situation where you will consciously let go of your expectations and submit to His transcendent will.
?
Maturidi says the opponents' view is 'a sign of incapacity.' If you are honest, in what area of your life do you resist admitting your own incapacity? Is it in your career, your family, your spiritual struggles? What would change if you fully acknowledged that your incapacity is not a failure but a pointer to God's limitless power? What practical step can you take today to live out that acknowledgment?

Divine Command and Takwin: Eternal Bringing into Existence
Maturidi explains that divine command and takwin (bringing into existence) are eternal and not limited by time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine command and takwin (bringing into existence) are eternal attributes of God, not limited by time, and how the expression 'Be' (kun) signifies this.

Core Idea
Takwin is God's effortless, eternal act of bringing things into existence exactly at their appointed time.

Imam Maturidi addresses a key theological question: Are divine commands and acts of creation temporal or eternal? He argues that takwin—God's bringing into existence—is an eternal attribute, and even the imperative 'Be' (kun) points to an eternal reality beyond human comprehension. In this passage, Maturidi clarifies how God's command, promise, and threat are all enacted through eternal takwin, and warns against limiting God's power with temporal notions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow: Maturidi begins with a statement about divine command and then builds to the explanation of takwin.
2
Identify the main argument about command
Maturidi argues that a person is commanded at the moment without a new command arriving. This shows that the command is eternal but applied in time, which implies that takwin is eternal.
Focus on the phrase 'ما ينكر أن يكون للحال كائنا بالتكوين في الأزل'.
3
Understand the meaning of 'Be' (kun)
Maturidi says the meaning of takwin can be expressed by the word 'Be' (kun). This brings things into existence exactly as they are at their time, without repetition.
This is a simplified expression for a reality beyond full human comprehension.
4
Reflect on the limits of human understanding
Maturidi acknowledges that human capacity cannot fully grasp takwin, which is effortless and tireless. He hopes the pointers given are sufficient for the intelligent.
This humility shows that theology has limits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه لا أحد أبى القول بأنه مأمور منهى في وقته من غير مجيء أمرٍ في هذا الوقت، وكذلك الوعد والوعيد، فيصير بالمنزل على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم هو الحال مأمورا منهيا، ما ينكر أن يكون للحال كائنا بالتكوين في الأزل، وكذلك الله سبحانه يوصف بكل كائن أنه عالم به كائنا، وإن كان يوصف من قبل بعلمه، والكون والحدث كله على الكائن دونه، وبالله التوفيق. على أن معنى التكوين، وإن كان لا يبلغه فهم البشر، لأمكن الأداء بأيسر قول يحتمله من القول: كن، كل شيء مكونا كل شيء على ما عليه كونه في وقت كونه من غير تكرار، وفيه يدخل ويصير إخبارا عن كائن وعما يكون، على الأمر كله والنهي والوعد والوعيد اختلاف أحوال الكائنات بأوقاتها وأمكنتها أبدا، لكن وسع الخلق لا يحتمل درك التكوين الذي لا يشغل ولا يتعب، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وهذا باب لو استقصى فيه لشغل عن بلوغ النهاية عن المقصود، ونرجو أن يكون فيما أشار إليه مقنع لذي اللب والفهم.
And after that, no one denied the statement that he is commanded and prohibited at his time without the coming of a command at that time, and likewise the promise and threat. So it becomes by the revelation to the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, that at the moment he is commanded and prohibited. What prevents that the state be existent by takwin (bringing into existence) in eternity? Likewise, God, glorified be He, is described concerning every existent that He is knowing of it as existent, even if He is described beforehand by His knowledge. And all existence and occurrence are upon the existent without Him, and with God is success. However, the meaning of takwin, even if human understanding cannot attain it, it is possible to express it by the easiest saying that can bear it from the saying: 'Be' (kun). Everything is made to exist just as it is upon its existence at the time of its existence without repetition. And it enters and becomes an informing about an existent and about what will be, upon all of command and prohibition and promise and threat, the difference of states of existents in their times and places forever. But the capacity of creation cannot bear the perception of takwin, which does not occupy nor tire. And there is no power except with God. And this is a topic which, if one were to investigate fully, would occupy [one] from reaching the end of the intended goal. And we hope that in what we have pointed to there is sufficiency for the possessor of intellect and understanding.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage discusses the divine command and the concept of takwin (bringing into existence). Maturidi begins by noting that no one denies that a person is commanded and prohibited at the moment of revelation, even if the command itself came earlier. Similarly, the promise and threat apply at the relevant time. This shows that a state can be brought into existence by eternal takwin. God is described as knowing each existent at the moment of its existence, even though He knew it eternally. Existence and occurrence are all dependent on the existent, not on God needing anything. Maturidi then explains that the meaning of takwin, though beyond full human comprehension, can be expressed by the simple word 'Be' (kun). Everything is brought into existence exactly as it is, at its time, without repetition. This includes all commands, prohibitions, promises, threats, and the varying states of existents throughout time and space. However, limited human capacity cannot grasp the nature of takwin, which is effortless and without fatigue. Maturidi concludes by stating that this topic is vast and could occupy one endlessly; he hopes that the pointers given are sufficient for the intelligent.

Key Terms
التكوين
كن
الأمر والنهي
الوعد والوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'Be' (kun) implies a temporal command or a change in God.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that takwin is eternal and does not involve change or fatigue. The word 'Be' is only a simplified expression for an eternal act.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi describe the meaning of takwin?
A) It is a temporal process that involves change in God.
B) It can be expressed by the word 'Be' (kun) and is eternal.
C) It is identical to human generation.
D) It cannot be expressed at all.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that takwin is an eternal attribute of God, expressed by 'Be', bringing everything into existence at its specific time without change or fatigue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's act of bringing things into existence is effortless and eternal, or do you find yourself imagining Him struggling to manage the demands of creation? How would your trust in His decree change if you fully embraced this truth?
?
Maturidi says human capacity cannot grasp the nature of takwin. Are you willing to accept that your mind is too limited to fully understand God's actions, or do you subtly try to reduce His power to fit your own logic? What would it mean for your worship to admit that 'there is no power except with God' in a deeper way than you currently do?
?
If every existent—including your own mistakes, losses, and moments of weakness—is brought into existence exactly at its time by an eternal 'Be' without fatigue, can you make peace with what has happened? What would it take for you to stop resenting your past and instead see it as unmade by the One who never tires?

The Eternal Reality of Takwin
Maturidi explains the meaning and eternal nature of takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's creative act (takwin) is eternal and beyond human comprehension, and how it relates to divine command and knowledge.

Core Idea
Takwin is God's eternal act of bringing things into existence without repetition.

Maturidi tackles a subtle theological point: if God's commands and prohibitions are revealed in time, does that mean His creative act is also temporal? He argues that the moment of revelation itself is brought into being by God's eternal takwin. In this passage, he explains what takwin means and why humans cannot fully grasp it.

Guided Path
1

Note that takwin is not a temporal act.

Al-Ka'bi's Classification of Divine Attributes
Al-Ka'bi's two criteria for dividing attributes into essential and active.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's criteria for dividing attributes into essential and active, and the rationale behind his classification.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi divides attributes based on variability and scope of divine power.

Al-Ka'bi was a leading Mu'tazilite theologian who proposed a sharp division between attributes that are essential to God and those that are merely acts. His classification rests on two key criteria: whether the attribute can differ with states or persons, and whether it falls under God's power. Understanding his view is crucial before we see Maturidi's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read Ka'bi's first criterion
Read the definition: attributes that vary with state or person are active; those that do not are essential. Note the examples: providing, mercy, speech vs. power, knowledge, life.
This criterion focuses on variability.
2
Read Ka'bi's second criterion
Now read the second definition: what power can affect is active; what it cannot is essential. Example: mercy and speech are possible for power to affect, while knowledge is not.
This criterion focuses on the scope of divine power.
3
Understand Ka'bi's argument about opposites
Ka'bi argues that essential attributes cannot have opposites because the essence is unchanging. He asks why an essential attribute should not be described by its opposite, and answers that it would imply change in the essence.
This reinforces the fixed nature of essential attributes.

Source Text

مسألة آراء الكعبى فى صفات الذات وصفات الفعل والرد عليها. وتذكر بعض ما ذكر الكعبنى لتعلموا مبلغه في معرفة الله والعلم به، أو في ذلك الإحاطة بمبلغ مذهب الإعتزال؛ إذ هو عندهم إمام أهل الأرض. قال: ما احتمل اختلاف الحال والشخص فهو صفة الفعل نحو القول: يرزق فلانا، ويرحم في حال ولا يرحم في حال وكذلك الكلام، ومثله في الأشخاص، ومثله في القدرة والعلم والحياة لا يحتمل، فهو صفة الذات. وقال: كل ما يقع عليه القدرة فهو صفة الفعل، نحو الرحمة والكلام، وما لا يقع عليه فهو صفة الذات، نحو أن لا يقال أيقدر أن يعلم أو لا؟ ثم يسأل صفة الذات: أنه لِمَ لا يجب الوصف بضده؟ قال: لأنه يرجع إلى ذاته، وذاته غير مختلف، وذلك يوجب الإختلاف. ثم قال: وإذا كان ذاته غير مختلف لم يجز الإختلاف ما بقيت نفسه. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: ومن قوله: أن ليس لله في الحقيقة صفة، وإنما وصف الواصف له أو تسمية المسمى، وقد وجد الأمران جميعا في وصف الواصفين أنى وصفوه بالعلم والقدرة والفعل على غير اختلاف من حيث الوصف، ثم سمى هو في الحقيقة عالما خالقا قادرا في التحقيق، فلا وجه لتعريفه من حيث وصف؛ إذ حقيقتها ترجع إلى ما فيه الوفاق. ثم قد يقال: سمع دعاء فلان ولم يسمع دعاء فلان، ويقول الرجل: ما عليم الله ذلك منى، ويقول علم مني في وقت كذا ولم يعلم مني في وقت كذا، ثم لم يجب به أن السمع والعلم لا يكونان من صفات الذات، فما منع كذلك في التكليم والرحمة. فإن قال: يريد نفى المعلوم والمسموع. قيل له: كذلك في الأول، يريد نفى [؟] من بره وإكرامه بذكر نفى الكلام، وهو شيء يريد به بره، وذلك معروف مما بشر المؤمنين بالكلام وأيأس الكفار، وذلك عندنا على ذلك.
Question: The views of al-Ka'bi on attributes of essence and attributes of action and refutation thereof. And mention some of what al-Ka'bi mentioned so that you may know his extent in knowledge of Allah and cognizance of Him, or in that encompass the extent of the Mu'tazilite school, for he is among them the imam of the people of the earth. He said: Whatever can accept difference of state and person is an attribute of action, like the saying: He provides for so-and-so, and He shows mercy in one state and does not show mercy in another state, and likewise speech, and similarly in persons. And that which does not accept [difference] like power, knowledge, and life is an attribute of essence. And he said: Whatever power can affect is an attribute of action, like mercy and speech, and whatever it cannot affect is an attribute of essence, as it cannot be said: Is He able to know or not? Then he asked regarding the attribute of essence: Why is it not necessary to describe it by its opposite? He said: Because it refers back to His essence, and His essence is not different, and that necessitates difference. Then he said: If His essence is not different, difference is not permissible as long as His self remains. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And from his statement is that there is in reality no attribute for Allah, but rather the description of the describer or the naming of the named. Both matters have been found in the description of describers, that they described Him by knowledge, power, and action without difference in terms of description; then He is truly named knowing, creator, powerful in reality. So there is no way to define Him in terms of description, for its reality refers back to that upon which there is agreement. Then it may be said: He heard the prayer of so-and-so and did not hear the prayer of so-and-so, and a man says: Allah did not know that from me, and he says: He knew from me at such-and-such time and did not know from me at such-and-such time. Then it does not follow from this that hearing and knowledge are not from the attributes of essence. So what prevents likewise in speech and mercy? If he says: He intends the negation of the known and the heard. It is said to him: Likewise in the first, he intends the negation of [؟] from His goodness and honor by mentioning the negation of speech, and it is something by which He intends His goodness. And that is known from what He gave glad tidings to the believers with speech and caused the disbelievers to despair. And that with us is upon that.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's classification of divine attributes into attributes of essence (صفات الذات) and attributes of action (صفات الفعل). Maturidi first presents Ka'bi's view: attributes that can vary with states or persons (like providing, mercy, speech) are attributes of action; those that do not vary (like power, knowledge, life) are attributes of essence. Ka'bi also says that whatever power can affect is an attribute of action, and what power cannot affect is an attribute of essence. He argues that attributes of essence cannot have opposites because the essence is uniform. Maturidi then refutes by pointing out that in reality there is no separate attribute for Allah; rather, descriptions are from the describers. He shows that even attributes like hearing and knowledge are described in varying contexts (hearing one prayer and not another), yet they are still considered attributes of essence. Therefore, speech and mercy likewise do not have to be merely attributes of action. The objection that such variation implies negation of the known/heard is answered by analogy: in the same way, negation of speech is not intended but rather affirmation of a particular act of goodness.

صفات الذات
صفات الفعل
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi, which of the following is an attribute of action?
A) Knowledge
B) Life
C) Mercy
D) Power

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi divides attributes into essential and active based on whether they vary or are subject to divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take a specific situation where you doubted God's knowledge or power, and reflect on whether that doubt stemmed from expecting Him to change.
?
Al-Ka'bi argues that if an attribute could have an opposite, it would imply change in God's essence. In your own life, where do you try to make God fit into your changing moods or desires—treating His attribute of mercy or justice as if it fluctuates? Commit to one area in which you will affirm God's unchanging essence by acting consistently with it, even when your feelings change.
?
The passage distinguishes attributes that are subject to God's power from those that are not. Are there aspects of your character or faith that you treat as beyond God's power to change—like a bad habit or a lack of trust? How does understanding that God's power can affect all action attributes (like mercy and speech) give you hope that He can transform those areas if you turn to Him?

Maturidi's Refutation of Ka'bi's Division
Maturidi's refutation of Ka'bi's division using the examples of hearing and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Ka'bi's distinction between essential and active attributes is invalid because even seemingly variable attributes like hearing and knowledge are essential.

Core Idea
Variability in description does not make an attribute non-essential.

After presenting Ka'bi's classification, Maturidi launches a refutation. He points out that the very terms we use to describe God are not independent attributes but descriptions from creatures. He then shows that even attributes like hearing and knowledge are spoken of as varying (e.g., God hears one prayer and not another), yet they are still considered essential. If that is accepted, then speech and mercy can likewise be essential despite being described as active.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's opening critique
Read the first objection: 'From his statement that there is in reality no attribute for Allah...' Maturidi argues that what we call attributes are just descriptions or names applied by creatures.
This undercuts the reality of the division.
2
Follow the analogy with hearing and knowledge
Maturidi points out that we say 'He heard so-and-so's prayer' and 'He did not hear so-and-so's prayer', yet we do not conclude that hearing is not essential. Similarly, knowledge is spoken of as known at one time and unknown at another, yet it is essential.
This shows that variability does not exclude essentiality.
3
Understand the objection and response
Ka'bi might object that such statements refer to the negation of the object, not the attribute. Maturidi replies that the same applies to speech and mercy: we are not negating the attribute but affirming a particular act of goodness.
The refutation is consistent.

Attributes of Essence and Action According to Imam Maturidi
Maturidi defines and distinguishes between attributes of essence and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between attributes of essence and attributes of action, and why attributes of essence cannot change.

Core Idea
Attributes of essence are unchanging; attributes of action vary with state and person.

Imam Maturidi provides a clear criterion to distinguish between two fundamental categories of divine attributes: those inherent to Allah’s essence and those that relate to His actions. By examining whether an attribute admits variation or falls under divine power, we can classify each attribute correctly. This passage lays the foundation for a proper understanding of Allah’s attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage once for familiarity. Then read the literal translation to understand Maturidi's wording.
The passage uses technical terms like صفة الذات and صفة الفعل.
2
Identify the two criteria
Notice that Maturidi gives two ways to distinguish attributes of essence from action: (1) variation with state/person, (2) falling under divine power. See how each criterion leads to the same classification.
Examples: knowledge and power do not vary; mercy and speech can vary.
3
Understand the objection
Examine the question about describing an essence attribute by its opposite. Maturidi answers by linking the attribute to the unchanging essence, showing that change would contradict the essence's nature.
This reinforces that essential attributes are immutable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ما احتمل اختلاف الحال والشخص فهو صفة الفعل نحو القول : برزق فلانا ، ويرحم في حال ولا يرجم في حال وكذلك الكلام ، ومثله في الأشخاص ، ومثله في القدرة والعلم والحياة لا يحتمل ، فهو صفة الذات وقال : كل ما يقع عليه القدرة فهو صفة الفعل ، نحو الرحمة والكلام ، وما يقع عليه فهو صفة الذات ، نحو أن لا يقال أيقدر أن يعلم أو لا ؟ ثم يسأل صفة الذات : أنه ليم لا يجب الوصف بضده ؟ قال : لأنه يرجع إلى ذاته ، وذاته غير مختلف ، وذلك يوجب الإختلاف. ثم قال : وإذا كان ذاته غير مختلف لم يجز الإختلاف ما بقيت نفسه . نفسه ، كالشيء الذي يجب لعلة يدوم لا عن تلا بدوامها .
He said: What admits variation of state and person is an attribute of action, such as saying: 'He provides for so-and-so' and 'He is merciful in one state and not merciful in another', and likewise speech, and similarly for persons. And what does not admit [variation] in power, knowledge, and life is an attribute of essence. And he said: Everything upon which power falls is an attribute of action, like mercy and speech, and whatever does not fall [under power] is an attribute of essence, such that one does not say: 'Is He able to know or not?' Then he was asked about the attribute of essence: why is it not necessary to describe it by its opposite? He said: Because it refers to His Essence, and His Essence is unchanging, and that necessitates [no] change. Then he said: When His Essence is unchanging, change is not permissible as long as His essence remains, like something necessary for a cause that continues with its continuity.
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage defines the two categories of divine attributes—attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). Maturidi begins by stating that whatever can vary according to state or person is an attribute of action, giving examples like providing, mercy, and speech. In contrast, attributes like power, knowledge, and life do not admit such variation; they are attributes of essence. He then adds another criterion: whatever falls under divine power (qudra) is an attribute of action (e.g., mercy and speech in terms of their relational aspect), while whatever does not fall under power (like knowledge itself) is an attribute of essence. A question arises: why is an attribute of essence not described by its opposite (e.g., why not say God is ignorant)? The answer is that the attribute goes back to the Essence, which is unchanging; describing it by an opposite would imply change in the Essence, which is impossible. Therefore, as the Essence is invariable, no change can occur in its essential attributes, just as a thing that is necessary for a cause persists as long as the cause endures.

Key Terms
صفة الذات
صفة الفعل
اختلاف الحال
القدرة
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think attributes of action are separate, changeable entities in Allah.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that attributes of action are relational and vary in their manifestation, but they do not imply change in Allah's essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is an attribute of essence?
A) Providing
B) Mercy
C) Knowledge
D) Speech

Key Takeaway
Attributes of essence are unchanging because they are rooted in Allah's immutable essence, while attributes of action vary according to state and are subject to divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Maturidi says essential attributes like knowledge cannot change. Are there areas of your life where you act as if Allah might 'change His mind' about you—like He could stop loving you because of your sin? What does this lesson say to that fear?

If an attribute like mercy can vary according to state, does that make you more earnest in seeking His mercy in this state? What one concrete action will you take today to position yourself to receive His mercy, knowing it is an act of His will, not a change in His essence?

Divine Attributes

God's Attributes: Description, Not Reality
Maturidi argues that God's attributes are not real distinct entities but only descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's position that God has no attribute as a separate reality, only as description and naming.

Core Idea
God's attributes are not separate entities; they are descriptions of His essence.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi tackles a subtle point: are God's attributes distinct realities in His essence? He argues that they are not. Instead, what we call attributes are simply descriptions or names applied to God. Understanding this is key to avoiding anthropomorphism and affirming divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic original and its English translation once to get a feel for Maturidi's concise style and argument.
Focus on the key terms: صفة, وصف, تسمية.
2
Identify the central claim
Maturidi openly states that God in reality has no attribute (صفة). Instead, it is only the description (وصف) or naming (تسمية). Pinpoint this thesis in the text.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Notice how Maturidi supports his claim: describers unanimously describe God with knowledge, power, and action. This consistency shows that description is not adding separate entities.
The phrase 'على غير اختلاف من حيث الوصف' is key.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that defining God by description is pointless since the reality of these descriptions returns to what is agreed upon (i.e., the essence itself).
The term 'الوفاق' refers to the unanimous agreement on the essence as the reference point.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: it safeguards tawhid by denying that attributes are separate from Allah, preventing any notion of composition in the divine essence.
This aligns with the broader Maturidi emphasis on divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ومن قوله : أن ليس لله في الحقيقة صفة ، وإنما وصفُ الواصف له أو تسمية المسمى ، وقد وجد الأمران جميعا في وصف الواصفين أنى وصفوه بالعلم والقدرة والفعل على غير اختلاف من حيث الوصف ، ثم سمى هو في الحقيقة عالما خالقا قادرا في التحقيق ، فلا وجه لتعريفه من حيث وصفه؛ إذ حقيقتها ترجع إلى ما فيه الوفاق .
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: And from his saying: that God in reality has no attribute, but rather it is the description of the describer or the naming of the named. And both matters have occurred together in the description of the describers, wherever they described Him with knowledge, power, and action without difference in terms of description. Then He is named in reality as Knower, Creator, Powerful in truth. So there is no way to define Him in terms of His description, since their reality returns to that which is agreed upon.
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage presents a key theological position: God in Himself does not have 'attributes' as distinct entities; rather, what we call attributes are the descriptions given by those who describe Him or the names by which He is named. Maturidi observes that describers have consistently described God with knowledge, power, and action without any difference in the mode of description. Then He is actually called Knower, Creator, Powerful in reality. Therefore, it is pointless to define God by the description as if the description added something separate; the reality of these descriptions goes back to what is unanimously agreed upon (the Essence itself).

Key Terms
الصفة
الوصف
التسمية
التحقيق
الوفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi denies that God has attributes like knowledge and power altogether.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny that God is Knower and Powerful; he denies that these attributes are additional entities separate from the essence. They are descriptions of the essence, not added realities.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about God's attributes in reality?
A) They are multiple distinct entities in God's essence
B) God has no attribute in reality, only description or naming
C) Attributes are created
D) Attributes are accidents

Key Takeaway
For Maturidi, God's attributes are not real distinct entities; they are only ways that describers describe Him and names that He is called, ultimately referring to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'All-Knowing', do you treat that as a separate entity in your mind, or as a description of His simple essence? How might this shift affect the way you rely on His knowledge in prayer?
?
If God's attributes are not distinct realities, then your entire concept of God must be simplified. Are you willing to let go of any imagined complexity in God? What does that demand from your heart in worship?
?
This passage argues that all describers agree on describing God with knowledge, power, and action. Pause and ask yourself: In your own life, do you truly submit to God's power and knowledge, or do you still try to compartmentalize what God knows and can do?

Hearing and Knowledge: Attributes of Essence
Maturidi responds to an objection about varying attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that hearing and knowledge remain attributes of essence even though their objects vary.

Core Idea
Variation is in the object, not the attribute itself.

A common objection against divine attributes of essence is that hearing and knowledge seem to vary—God hears one prayer but not another, knows one thing but not another. If they vary, shouldn't they be attributes of action? Maturidi responds by clarifying that the variation is in what is heard or known, not in the attribute itself. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the objector's claim that hearing and knowledge vary, so they cannot be attributes of essence. Notice the examples given.
The objector uses everyday language to challenge the doctrine.
2
Identify Maturidi's response
Maturidi argues that the variation is in the objects (the known and the heard) not in the attributes. Find how he applies the same logic to speech and mercy.
3
Understand the counterargument
The objector might say phrases like 'God did not know' actually negate the known event. Maturidi responds that similarly, negating speech negates the benevolent speech to someone, not the attribute itself.
This shows consistency in Maturidi's reasoning.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ties this to the broader context of Quranic affirmations about God's speech to believers and disbelievers.
This reinforces that attributes of essence are unchanging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قد يقال : سمع دعاء فلان ولم يسمع دعاء فلان ، ويقول الرجل : ما عليم الله ذلك منى ، ويقول علم مني في وقت كذا ولم يعلم مني في وقت كذا ، ثم لم يجب به أن السمع والعلم لا يكونان من صفات الذات ، فما منع كذلك في التكليم. والرحمة فإن قال : يريد نفى المعلوم والمسموع . قيل له : كذلك فى الأول ، يريد تفى فرغون من بره وإكرامه بذكر نفى الكلام ، وهو شيء يريد به بره ، وذلك معروف مما بشر المؤمنين بالكلام وأيأس الكفار ، وذلك عندنا على ذلك
Then it may be said: He heard the prayer of so-and-so and did not hear the prayer of so-and-so, and a man says: 'God did not know that from me,' and says: 'He knew from me at such a time and did not know from me at such a time.' Yet this does not necessitate that hearing and knowledge are not attributes of essence. So what prevents the same in speech and mercy? If he says: He intends the negation of the known and the heard. It is said to him: Likewise in the first, he intends the negation of speech, and it is something by which he intends His goodness, and that is known from what he gave glad tidings to the believers with speech and disappointed the disbelievers, and that according to us is upon that.
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its response. The objector argues that because God hears one person's prayer but not another's, and knows something at one time but not at another, hearing and knowledge seem to vary—so why are they considered attributes of essence? Likewise for speech and mercy. The response clarifies that the variation is in the object (the known or the heard), not in the attribute itself. The objector suggests that phrases like 'God did not know' actually negate the known event, not the attribute. Similarly, when we say God does not speak to someone, it means He negates His benevolent speech to them, not that His attribute of speech changes. This understanding is supported by how God's speech brings glad tidings to believers and despair to disbelievers.

Key Terms

التكليم
الرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God hears some prayers and not others, His attribute of hearing must change (be an attribute of action).
Correction: Maturidi explains that the variation is in what is heard, not in the attribute of hearing itself. Hearing as an attribute of essence remains constant.

Checkpoint

A) He admits they are attributes of action
B) He says variation is in the object, not the attribute
C) He says God does not hear or know
D) He says attributes are created

Key Takeaway
Attributes of essence like hearing and knowledge remain constant; any apparent variation is in their objects, not the attributes themselves.

?
When you face a situation where God's knowledge seems to have 'missed' something (like a trial you didn't expect), do you slip into doubting His awareness? How does Maturidi's insistence that knowledge is constant—only the known changes—force you to reexamine your heart's response to life's surprises?
?
What would change in your daily relationship with God if you deeply internalized that His mercy is never diminished, even when you don't feel it? Are you willing to base your hope on the constancy of His attributes rather than on the circumstances of their objects?

Maturidi's Refutation: Attribute of Action vs. Creation
Maturidi uses a dilemma to refute identifying the attribute of action with creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the attribute of action cannot be equated with the act of creation, and how this preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Creation's imperfections cannot be attributed to God, so the attribute of action is an eternal attribute of essence.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi confronts a common theological confusion: is God's attribute of 'action' identical to the created acts we observe? He presents a powerful dilemma that shows why identifying the attribute with creation leads to absurdity. Follow along as he systematically dismantles this view and reaffirms God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice how Maturidi opens with a direct question, setting up a dilemma.
The passage is a single continuous argument, so read it in full before analyzing.
2
Identify the Two Horns of the Dilemma
The passage presents two possible responses from the opponent. Find them and see what consequences Maturidi derives from each.
The first horn: creation is the attribute. The second: creation is not the attribute.
3
Analyze the First Horn
If creation is the attribute, then creation's defects (corruption, ugliness, etc.) would be attributed to God. Why is this impossible? Reflect on divine transcendence.
Maturidi argues that even rational people avoid attributing such defects to God.
4
Analyze the Second Horn
If creation is not the attribute, then the attribute is something else—an act of God. But what does that mean? Maturidi links it back to the essence.
This leads to the conclusion that the attribute of action is a quality of the essence, not a created entity.
5
Understand the Conclusion
Maturidi ends by declaring God exalted above being described by His creation and reaffirms that the attribute of action belongs to the essence.
This preserves God's perfection and distinguishes His eternal attributes from contingent acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : تعنى بصفة الفعل الفعل نفسه وهو الخلق ، أهو عندك فعل أو غيره ؟ فإن قال : الخلق ، قيل : ليم قلت إن الخلق صفة ؟ وهو صفة من؟ ؟ ؟ إذ لا صفة إلا لموصوف فإن قال : هو صفة الله ، أعظم القول بأن يجعل الخلق لله صفة ، والخلق فساد وقبيح وضرورة وعجز وأنجاس وخبائث ، وكل بصفته موصوف ، وهذه الأوصاف مما يألى كل من [أنى؟] عقل أن يوصف بها ، فكيف يوصف بها الله وإن قال : غير الخلق ، لزمه القول أن المراد أن صفته هي فعل ، وقد بينا [ذلك] ، تعالى الله عن الوصف بخلقه ، فثبت أن صفته التي هي الفعل هي صفة ذاته
Then it is said to him: Do you mean by attribute of action the act itself, which is creation? Is it, according to you, an act or something else? If he says: Creation, it is said: Why did you say that creation is an attribute? And of what is it an attribute? Since there is no attribute except for a described thing. If he says: It is an attribute of God, then he utters a grave statement by making creation an attribute of God, while creation includes corruption, ugliness, necessity, incapacity, impurities, and filth, and everything is described by its attribute. These are descriptions that every rational person refrains from describing [God] with. So how can God be described with them? And if he says: Other than creation, then he must say that what is meant is that His attribute is an act, and we have already explained that. Exalted is God above being described by His creation. So it is established that His attribute, which is the act, is an attribute of His essence.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage presents a decisive refutation of the view that the attribute of action is identical with the act (e.g., creation). The argument takes the form of a dilemma. If the opponent says that the attribute of action is the act itself (creation), then creation contains many negative aspects like corruption, ugliness, imperfection, etc. If creation were an attribute of God, those defects would be attributed to Him, which is impossible. If, on the other hand, the opponent says the attribute of action is something other than the act, then he is forced to admit that the attribute is an 'act' (fiʿl) as a description of God, but that still requires that the attribute be connected to the essence in a way that does not imply imperfection. Maturidi concludes that God is exalted above being described by His creation, and thus the attribute that is called 'action' must be an attribute of essence (i.e., a creative power inherent in His essence), not a created thing.

Key Terms
صفة الفعل
الخلق
الفساد
القبيح
الضرورة
العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attribute of action (such as creating) is identical to the created act itself.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if the attribute were the created act, then creation's defects would apply to God, which is impossible. Therefore, the attribute of action is an eternal attribute of God's essence, not a contingent act.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the idea that creation is an attribute of God?
A) Because creation does not exist
B) Because creation includes defects that cannot be attributed to God
C) Because God does not create
D) Because creation is an attribute of action, not essence

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the attribute of action cannot be identical to creation because creation's imperfections would then be attributed to God; instead, it is an eternal attribute of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How will you actively replace those false images with the truth that His attributes are perfect and beyond comparison?
?
If God's attribute of action is not a created thing but an eternal attribute, then every action He does is from His eternal perfection. This includes His actions in your life. Do you truly believe that every trial, blessing, and decree comes from a perfectly wise and good Being? What would change in your heart if you lived by that conviction today?

Distinction between sound and knowledge in attributes
Argument about sound and knowledge; refutation of denying justice as attribute of essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that the opponent's failure to consistently apply the distinction between affirmation and negation in attributes leads to a corrupt estimation.

Core Idea
Inconsistent logic about sound and knowledge undermines the opponent's position on divine attributes.

This passage presents a second argument against those who deny that justice is an attribute of essence. Imam Maturidi points out that while sound and knowledge are treated differently in terms of 'hearing' and 'knowledge', the opponent does not require a categorical distinction based on the letter of affirmation. Then he applies the same reasoning to the negation of justice, showing the inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once carefully to get the flow of the argument. Don't worry about details yet.
Notice the repetition of 'ثم لم' which signals the opponent's inconsistency.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms 'ṣawt' (sound), 'ʿilm' (knowledge), 'tasmiʿ' (making heard), and 'nafy al-ʿadl' (negation of justice). These are central to the argument.
3
Follow the first argument
The author argues that for everything other than sound, we don't speak of it as 'heard', but we can speak of it with 'knowledge'. Yet the opponent did not differentiate between these two cases based on the letter of affirmation. This implies inconsistency.
The 'letter of affirmation' refers to the grammatical particle used to affirm an attribute.
4
Follow the second argument
Then the author brings up another point: it is not permissible to describe God with the negation of justice, yet the opponent did not say that this negation is an attribute of essence. So his reasoning is corrupt.
The conclusion 'thabata anna taqdīruhu fāsid' is a strong refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن كل غير الصوت لا يتكلم فيه بتسميع، وجائز أن يتكلم فيه بعلم ثم لم يجب التفريق بينهما بالاختلاف في حرف الإثبات، ولم يوجب في ذاته فيا كذلك في حق النفى، ولا قوة إلا بالله. منع وأيضا أنه لا يجوز وصفى الله تعالى بنفى العدل، ثم لم يقل هو صفة الذات عندهم، ثبت أن تقديره فاسد.
And also that everything other than sound is not spoken of with 'hearing' (tasmiʿ), and it is permissible to speak of it with knowledge (ʿilm). Then he did not necessitate differentiation between them by a difference in the letter of affirmation, and he did not necessitate in His essence—likewise in the case of negation. And there is no power except with God. He denied? And also that it is not permissible to describe God Almighty with the negation of justice (nafy al-ʿadl). Then he did not say it is an attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt) according to them. It is established that his estimation is corrupt.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage is a second argument against the opponent. It points out that for everything other than sound (ṣawt), one does not speak of it in terms of 'hearing' (tasmiʿ), but it is permissible to speak of it with knowledge (ʿilm). Yet the opponent did not require a differentiation between these cases based on the difference in the letter of affirmation (ḥarf al-ithbāt), and similarly in the case of negation. Then the author adds another point: it is not permissible to describe God with the negation of justice (nafy al-ʿadl), yet the opponent did not say that this is an attribute of essence according to them. Therefore, the opponent's reasoning (taqdīr) is corrupt.

Key Terms

علم
تسميع
نفى العدل
صفة الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That different modes of attribution (e.g., sound vs. knowledge) necessarily require a categorical distinction between attributes of essence and action.
Correction: The author shows that the opponent's own logic does not consistently apply such a distinction, leading to a corrupt conclusion.

Checkpoint

B) Knowledge is not permissible to attribute to God
C) The opponent failed to consistently apply the distinction between affirmation and negation
D) Sound is an attribute of essence

Key Takeaway
The opponent's reasoning is corrupt because they fail to apply the same logical standard to both affirmation and negation regarding divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Attribute of Action is Not the Act Itself
Dialectical objection and answer on the attribute of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of action (ṣifat al-fiʿl) cannot be identified with the act itself (such as creation), and how this dilemma leads to the conclusion that the attribute of action is actually an attribute of essence.

Core Idea
Creation cannot be God's attribute; action attributes reduce to essence attributes.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a dialectical argument to refute those who claim that the attribute of action is identical to the act itself, such as creation. He poses a dilemma: if creation is the attribute, then it includes imperfect things, which cannot be ascribed to God. If it is not creation, then the attribute must be something else—namely, an attribute of essence. This lesson walks through the logic of this refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialectical style: the author addresses an opponent with 'then it is said to him'.
2
Identify the dilemma
The opponent is asked: do you mean by 'attribute of action' the act itself (creation) or not? This sets up two horns.
The dilemma is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the first horn
If the opponent says creation is the attribute, then creation includes corrupt and ugly things, so it cannot be an attribute of God.
This horn leads to an absurdity, so it must be rejected.
4
Understand the second horn
If the opponent says creation is not the attribute, then the attribute must be something else—and the author claims it is the act itself, which he has already shown to be an attribute of essence.
This horn forces the opponent to accept the author's earlier proof.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the attribute of action is actually an attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt).
This is the key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : تعنى بصفة الفعل الفعل نفسه وهو الخلق ، أهو عندك فعل أو غيره ؟ فإن قال : الخلق ، قيل : ليم قلت إن الخلق صفة ؟ وهو صفة من؟ إذ لا صفة إلا لموصوف فإن قال : هو صفة الله ، أعظم القول بأن يجعل الخلق لله صفة ، والخلق فساد وقبيح وضرورة وعجز وأنجاس وخبائث ، وكل بصفته موصوف ، وهذه الأوصاف مما يألي كل من [؟] عقل أن يوصف بها ، فكيف يوصف بها الله وإن قال : غير الخلق ، لزمه القول أن المراد أن صنفته هي فعل ، وقد بينا ذلك ، تعالى الله عن الوصف بخلقه ، فثبت أن صفته التي هي الفعل هي صفة ذاته
Then it is said to him: do you mean by 'attribute of action' (ṣifat al-fiʿl) the act itself, which is creation (khalq)? Is it in your view an act or other than it? If he says: creation, it is said: why did you say that creation is an attribute? And whose attribute is it? For there is no attribute except for a described thing. If he says: it is an attribute of God, he makes a great statement by making creation an attribute of God, and creation is corruption, ugliness, necessity, impotence, filths, and impurities, and everything described by its attribute. And these descriptions are such that every [؟] intellect shies away from being described by them. So how can God be described by them? And if he says: other than creation, then he is obliged to say that what he meant is that his attribute is an act, and we have explained that. God is exalted above being described by His creation. So it is established that His attribute which is the act is an attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt).
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical objection and answer concerning the nature of the attribute of action (ṣifat al-fiʿl). The author poses a dilemma to the opponent: if by 'attribute of action' you mean the act itself, such as creation (khalq), then either you say creation is an attribute of God or not. If you say it is an attribute, then creation includes corrupt, ugly, and impure things, which cannot be attributed to God. If you say it is other than creation, then you must admit that the attribute refers to something else, and the author claims this has already been explained—namely that the attribute of action is actually an attribute of essence. Thus, the opponent's position is refuted.

Key Terms

فعل
خلق
صفة الذات
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributes of action are entirely distinct from the divine essence, existing as separate eternal acts.
Correction: This passage shows that the attribute of action reduces to an attribute of essence, because if it were the act itself (creation), it would entail imperfections in God.

Checkpoint
What problem does the author highlight if the opponent says creation is an attribute of God?
A) Creation is an act, not an attribute
B) Creation includes imperfect things like corruption and ugliness
C) Creation is eternal
D) Creation is not performed by God

Key Takeaway
The attribute of action cannot be identified with creation; rather, it is an attribute of essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Divine Names as Descriptions of Essence
Imam Maturidi uses an analogy to show that names like 'Creator' describe God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that names like 'Creator' and 'Merciful' describe God's essence, not just His actions, using the analogy of speech.

Core Idea
Names of God describe His essence, not just His acts.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi clarifies a common confusion about divine attributes. When we call God 'Creator' or 'Merciful,' are we describing His essence or just what He does? Using a simple analogy from speech, he shows that these names actually refer to His essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow: a claim, then an analogy, then a conclusion.
2
Identify the main claim and analogy
Find where Imam Maturidi states that names like 'Creator' refer to essence. Then find the analogy using speech of wisdom, truth, and falsehood.
The analogy shows that just as speech attributes qualities to the speaker, divine names reflect the essence of God.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters. It guards against the misconception that attributes like 'Creator' are temporary or separate from God.
This lesson reinforces the unity and permanence of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يقال: الله خالق رحمن رحيم، فإنما سمى به ذاته، فمثله صفة الفعل أي الفعل - وتوصف به ذاته، وذلك كما يقال: كلام حكمة وصدق وكذب على أنه كذلك، وهو صفة لصاحبه، فمثله يضاف إلى الله.
And likewise it is said: God is Creator (Khāliq), Merciful (Raḥmān), Compassionate (Raḥīm). He is only named by His essence. So similarly, the attribute of action—i.e., the act—and His essence is described by it. And that is as it is said: speech of wisdom, truth, and falsehood, it being so, and it is an attribute of its owner. So similarly, it is attributed to God.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage provides an analogy to clarify the relationship between attributes of action and essence. The author states that when we say 'God is Creator, Merciful, Compassionate,' these names refer to His essence (dhāt), not just to acts. The attribute of action (ṣifat al-fiʿl)—i.e., the act itself—is such that the essence is described by it. The analogy is like speech (kalām): we say 'speech of wisdom' or 'speech of truth and falsehood,' and that description is an attribute of the speaker. Similarly, attributes like 'Creator' are attributed to God as descriptions of His essence.

Key Terms
خالق
رحمن
رحيم
صفة الفعل
كلام
حكمة
صدق
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Names like 'Creator' refer only to actions separate from God's essence.
Correction: The passage explicitly says that God is 'only named by His essence' (فإنما سمى به ذاته), meaning these names describe His essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy, how do names like 'Creator' relate to God's essence?
A) They refer only to His actions, not His essence
B) They name His essence
C) They are metaphorical
D) They are attributes of creation

Key Takeaway
Names like 'Creator' are not merely descriptions of actions but attributes of God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is the Creator,' do you believe that this name tells you something about His very being, or do you treat it as a mere description of an action? If you only see it as an action, what does that reveal about how you think of God? Can you commit today to remembering that His names are windows into His essence?
?
The analogy compares divine attributes to speech. When you hear someone's words, you form an impression of their character. How does calling God 'Raḥmān' (the Merciful) shape your confidence in His mercy toward you personally? Is there a specific area of your life where you need to trust that His mercy is part of His essence, not just an occasional act?
?
Maturidi says that names like 'Compassionate' (Raḥīm) describe God's essence. If God is inherently compassionate, then His compassion is not conditional on your actions. How does that truth challenge places where you either feel unworthy of His compassion or take it for granted? What one change in your daily awareness of God could you make starting today?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Dilemma of Mercy and Curse as Attributes of Action
Maturidi objects to classifying mercy and curse as attributes of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that classifying both mercy and curse as attributes of action leads to a contradiction in the opponent's own theological claims.

Core Idea
Mercy and curse cannot both be attributes of action without inconsistency.

Imam Maturidi presents a sharp objection to those who consider both mercy and curse as attributes of action. He forces the opponent to specify the act corresponding to each, revealing a dilemma. If the acts are Paradise and Hell, it contradicts the opponent's earlier statements about God's mercy. If not, it leads to describing God with ugly speech. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the force of the objection come through.
Notice how the author presents a conditional argument.
2
Identify the two possible responses
The opponent has two options: either the acts are Paradise and Hell etc., or they are something else. Find both options in the text.
The first option is explicitly stated, the second is implied.
3
Understand the consequences of each response
The first response contradicts the opponent's own doctrine of al-aslah. The second leads to describing God with 'insult', which is ugly speech. Reflect on why each is problematic.
This shows the author's skill in dialectical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه يقال له: قولك رحمة ومغفرة صفة للفعل، ولعنة وشتم أيضا عندك صفة الفعل، فما الفعل الذى سمى رحمة ولعنة حتى يوصف الله به؟ فإن قال: جنة ونار، وقبول ورد ونحو ذلك بطل قوله في المسائل التي ذكر في الأصلح والتعديل والتجويز، إن الله رحيم لا يفعل بعباده ذلك، وكل ذلك مما فعل بعباده. وإن أثبت معنى سوى ذلك فصارا غير خلقه، بهما يوصف على أن قوله: يشتم، كلام قبيح لا يوصف الله به.
And after, it is said to him: your saying that mercy (raḥma) and forgiveness (maghfira) are attributes of action, and curse (laʿna) and insult (shatm) also in your view are attributes of action. So what is the act that is named mercy and curse so that God is described by it? If he says: Paradise and Hell, acceptance and rejection and the like, then his statement becomes invalid in the issues he mentioned regarding the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ), modification (al-taʿdīl), and permission (al-tajwīz), that God is merciful (Raḥīm) and does not do that to His servants, while all of that is done to His servants. And if he posits a meaning other than that, then they become other than His creation, and he describes God by them, while his saying 'He insults' is ugly speech (kalām qabīḥ) not attributed to God.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage is an objection directed at the opponent's claim that mercy and curse are both attributes of action. The author demands the opponent specify the act that corresponds to mercy and curse. If the opponent says the acts are Paradise and Hell, acceptance and rejection, then this contradicts his own earlier assertions about God's mercy and the doctrine of the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) and divine modification (taʿdīl). If he posits a different meaning, then those attributes become something other than God's creation, which leads to the problem that he describes God with 'insult' (shatm), which is ugly speech and cannot be attributed to God.

Key Terms

مغفرة
لعنة
شتم
صفة الفعل
جنة
نار
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All moral concepts like mercy and curse are equally attributes of action without further specification.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that this leads to inconsistency; one must specify the act to avoid contradictions.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the author point out if the opponent says mercy and curse are both attributes of action?
A) Mercy and curse cannot both be from God
B) If the acts are Paradise and Hell, then God does things to servants that He is said not to do
C) Curse is not an attribute of action
D) Mercy is not an attribute of action

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi refutes the notion that mercy and curse can both be attributes of action without specifying a consistent act, revealing contradictions in the opponent's theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God is both Merciful and capable of curse, do you truly trust in His mercy for yourself, or do you live in fear that His curse applies to you because of your shortcomings? How does this lesson call you to examine your own certainty in God's mercy?
?
The opponent fell into inconsistency by not specifying a coherent act for mercy and curse. Are you consistent in how you speak about God's attributes, or do you sometimes describe Him in contradictory ways without realizing it? What will you do to ensure your speech about God is sound?
?
The author refrains from attributing ugly speech to God. Have you ever, in moments of anger or frustration, spoken about God in a way that diminishes His perfection? What does this passage demand of you in your own words about God?
