Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Diversity in Usage Does Not Divide the Essence Attributes
Rebuttal: diversity in applying attributes like knowledge and power does not differentiate essence from action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why diversity in the application of divine attributes does not imply a division between essence and action attributes, but rather demonstrates their common eternal nature.

Core Idea
Variation in usage does not create division; all attributes are equally eternal.

Imam al-Maturidi now addresses a counterargument: the opponent claimed that differences in usage allow us to distinguish attributes of essence from attributes of action. Here he shows that even among essence attributes, usage varies—yet they are all considered one category. So the same logic should apply to all attributes, because God is beyond change and decay.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts with 'ثم يقال له' indicating a direct rebuttal.
2
Identify the examples of diverse application
The passage lists several essence attributes (knowledge, power, hearing, vision, generosity, bounty, wisdom) and shows how each is used for different things. List them and note the pattern.
For example, 'knowledge' is said about things where He is not described with power, etc.
3
Understand the inference
Despite the diversity in application, the attributes are still all described as eternal (في الأزل). The author asks: why did this diversity not necessitate a distinction? Then he answers: because God is exalted above change and corruption—these are signs of temporality.
The key point: variation in usage does not affect the eternal status of the attributes.
4
Apply the same logic to the opponent's position
The author concludes that if the opponent accepts that diversity among essence attributes does not divide them, then he should apply the same reasoning to all attributes—including those he wants to classify as actions.
This is a logical move: consistency demands the same treatment.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Why does this matter? It preserves the unity and transcendence of God. If attributes could be divided based on application, God's simplicity would be compromised. Here, al-Maturidi safeguards tawhid.
The underlying principle: God does not change; therefore, different uses do not indicate different natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: ليم اعتبرت بالذى ذكرت فى صفة الذات والفعل؟ وقد رأيت صفات الذات مختلفة في الاستعمال من وجه الإثبات نحو أن يقال بالعلم في أشياء لا يوصف بالقدرة فيها، وبالقدرة على أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع فيها، وبالرؤية في أشياء لا يوصف بالكرم فيها، وبالجود وبالحكمة في أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع لها، ونحو ذلك فما يكثر الإختلاف به، لم يجب بها الفرق بل هو الموصوف بها في الأزل. ليم لا قلت كذلك في جميع ما يوصف به؟؛ إذ هو يتعالى عن الاستحالة والفساد؛ إنهما آيتان للحدث، أمارتان للكون بعد أن لم يكن.
Then it is said to him: why did you rely on what you mentioned regarding attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt) and attribute of action? And you have seen that attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) differ in usage from the perspective of affirmation, such that knowledge is said about things in which He is not described with power, and power over things in which He is not described with hearing, and vision in things in which He is not described with generosity, and bounty and wisdom in things in which He is not described with hearing for them, and the like. So with all this diversity, it did not necessitate a distinction, rather He is described by them in eternity. Why did you not say the same regarding all that He is described by? For He is exalted above change (istiḥāla) and corruption (fasād); they are signs of temporality (ḥadath), indicators of coming into existence after not having been.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal to the opponent's argument that differences in usage justify distinguishing attributes of essence from attributes of action. The author points out that even among commonly accepted essence attributes (ṣifāt al-dhāt)—such as knowledge (ʿilm), power (qudra), hearing (samʿ), vision (ruʾya), generosity (karam), bounty (jūd), and wisdom (ḥikma)—their application varies: God is described by knowledge regarding things where He is not described by power, by power over things where He is not described by hearing, etc. Despite this diversity, one does not divide these attributes; rather, God is described by them in eternity (azal). The opponent should apply the same logic to all attributes. The reason is that God is transcendent above change and corruption, which are indicators of temporality (ḥadath). So variation in usage does not entail a real distinction in the attribute itself.

Key Terms
صفات الذات
علم
قدرة
سمع
بصر
كرم
جود
حكمة
أزل
استحالة
فساد
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That differing applicability of divine attributes indicates a fundamental difference in their nature or category (e.g., essence vs. action).
Correction: Even essence attributes like knowledge and power are applied differently to different things, yet they are all equally eternal. Variation in usage does not create a real distinction.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that diversity in applying essence attributes does not justify distinguishing attributes of essence from attributes of action?
A) Because essence attributes are eternal and unchanging
B) Because the diversity is only in creation, not in God
C) Because even essence attributes are applied differently, yet they are all eternal descriptions of God
D) Because action attributes have no diversity

Key Takeaway
Diversity in how we apply attributes to God does not indicate a division in the attributes themselves; all are equally eternal because God is beyond change and temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God in your daily worship, do you ever subconsciously limit His attributes based on how you experience them? How does this lesson show you that such limitation is based on your own categories, not on God's reality?
?
The passage teaches that God is described by attributes that sometimes seem to exclude each other in application. Does this diversity trouble your heart, or does it deepen your awe? Are you willing to accept that your mind cannot fully harmonize these descriptions, yet they are all true of the Eternal?
?
Consider a situation where you felt God was not 'generous' because you lacked something, but you still believed He was 'all-knowing'. How does this lesson challenge you to trust that all His attributes are equally present—even when you don't see them?

Divine Attributes

The Division of Creation and Its Implication for Attributes
Maturidi's argument from division of creation to refute the opponent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from the division of creation undermines the opponent's objection regarding attributes.

Core Idea
Creation is divided, yet God is named by some parts; this logic applies to attributes too.

Imam Maturidi presents a final argument against the opponent who denies that attributes of power are real. The opponent had claimed that God is not described as having power over His attributes. Here Maturidi points out that creation (khalq) is itself divided—God is called 'Creator' with respect to some created things but not others—yet this does not create a real difference in the attribute of power. Similarly, the opponent's objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: it begins with an address 'yukālu lahu' (it is said to him) and then presents an analogy.
The passage uses an argument form called qiyās (analogical reasoning).
2
Identify the analogy
The argument compares the division of creation to the case of attributes. God is named by some creation (e.g., 'Creator') but not by other aspects of creation (e.g., not called 'created'). Yet this does not imply a difference in the attribute itself. Likewise, the opponent's claim about power leads to an inconsistency.
3
Connect to the broader debate
This argument is a refutation of the opponent's attempt to distinguish between attributes of essence and attributes of action. The opponent claimed power over attributes is metaphorical; Maturidi shows this leads to logical problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا يقال له: رأيت الخلق أقساما، يُسمى الله عندك ببعض الخلق ولا يسمى ببعض، ثم لم يدل على اختلاف في حق الصفة، كذلك في [؟] ما منع أمر الصفات؟، وبالله التوفيق. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم قوله: ما يوصف بالقدرة عليه فليس من صفات الذات، فهو عند خصمه لا يوصف بالقدرة على شيء من صفاته إلا على مجاز اللغة من إرادة المفعول فى ذلك، كما يسمى ما يفعل بالأمر امرا ونحو ذلك
And also it is said to him: you have seen that creation is divided—God is named by some creation in your view and not named by some. Then it did not indicate a difference regarding the attribute. Likewise, regarding [؟] what prevented the matter of attributes? And with God is success. The jurist Abū Manṣūr—may God have mercy on him—said: Then his saying: 'That over which [God] is described as having power is not from the attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt),' so according to his opponent, He is not described as having power over anything of His attributes except by a linguistic metaphor (majāz al-lugha) meaning the intended effect (irādat al-mafʿūl) in that, just as what is done by command is called 'command' (amr) and the like.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, the author argues that just as creation (khalq) is divided (some parts God is named by, some not) yet this does not indicate a real difference in the attribute itself, similarly the opponent's objection about attributes fails. Then the author quotes the jurist Abū Manṣūr (Imam Maturidi) who comments on the opponent's statement: 'That over which God is described as having power is not from the attributes of essence.' According to Maturidi, this means that according to the opponent, God is not described as having power over any of His attributes except metaphorically (majāz al-lugha), meaning that the power refers to the effect (mafʿūl) rather than the attribute itself, similar to how an act done by command is called 'command' (amr). This critique shows the opponent's position reduces to a metaphorical interpretation, which is insufficient for a real attribute distinction.

Key Terms
خلق
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God is called Creator only of some things, His attribute of power must be multiple or differ.
Correction: Maturidi shows that division in created things does not affect the unity and reality of God's attribute of power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what does the division of creation indicate about God's attribute?
A) It indicates the attribute is divided.
B) It does not indicate any difference regarding the attribute.
C) It shows God's power is limited.
D) It proves attributes are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The division of creation does not imply any real division or difference in God's attributes; the same logic refutes the opponent's objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see some prayers answered and others not, do you ever doubt that God's power is complete? How does this argument that being named by some creation and not other does not diminish His attribute correct that doubt? What would change in your trust if you truly internalized this?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God's power is limited because He does not create evil in the same way as good? How does the logic of this passage challenge that assumption? What would it mean for your view of God if you accepted that His attributes are undivided?
?
The passage ends with 'And with God is success.' Are you truly relying on God's success in your understanding of Him, or do you lean on your own reasoning? When you study these arguments, do you feel a sense of humble dependence on God's guidance?

Maturidi's Critique of Metaphorical Attribution of Power
Maturidi's comment on the opponent's metaphorical interpretation of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's critique of the opponent's claim that God's power over attributes is only metaphorical.

Core Idea
The opponent reduces God's power over attributes to a mere linguistic metaphor, undermining attribute reality.

Imam Maturidi now directly addresses the opponent's statement that 'that over which God is described as having power is not from the attributes of essence.' He explains what this means from the opponent's perspective: they claim that God is not described as having power over His attributes except by a linguistic metaphor, similar to calling an act done by command a 'command.' Maturidi's comment exposes the weakness of this view, which fails to take the attributes as real.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's statement
First, understand the opponent's claim: 'That over which God is described as having power is not from the attributes of essence.' According to the opponent, God's power does not extend to His own attributes in a real sense.
This is the view being critiqued.
2
Understand Maturidi's explanation
Maturidi explains that, in the opponent's view, God's description as having power over attributes is only metaphorical (majāz al-lugha). The power is attributed to the effect, not the attribute itself.
The example of calling an act by command a 'command' illustrates metaphor.
3
Recognize the critique
Maturidi's commentary highlights that the opponent reduces real divine attributes to mere linguistic constructs, which is inconsistent with a proper understanding of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا يقال له: رأيت الخلق أقساما، يُسمى الله عندك ببعض الخلق ولا يسمى ببعض، ثم لم يدل على اختلاف في حق الصفة، كذلك في [؟] ما منع أمر الصفات؟، وبالله التوفيق. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم قوله: ما يوصف بالقدرة عليه فليس من صفات الذات، فهو عند خصمه لا يوصف بالقدرة على شيء من صفاته إلا على مجاز اللغة من إرادة المفعول فى ذلك، كما يسمى ما يفعل بالأمر امرا ونحو ذلك
And also it is said to him: you have seen that creation is divided—God is named by some creation in your view and not named by some. Then it did not indicate a difference regarding the attribute. Likewise, regarding [؟] what prevented the matter of attributes? And with God is success. The jurist Abū Manṣūr—may God have mercy on him—said: Then his saying: 'That over which [God] is described as having power is not from the attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt),' so according to his opponent, He is not described as having power over anything of His attributes except by a linguistic metaphor (majāz al-lugha) meaning the intended effect (irādat al-mafʿūl) in that, just as what is done by command is called 'command' (amr) and the like.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, the author argues that just as creation (khalq) is divided (some parts God is named by, some not) yet this does not indicate a real difference in the attribute itself, similarly the opponent's objection about attributes fails. Then the author quotes the jurist Abū Manṣūr (Imam Maturidi) who comments on the opponent's statement: 'That over which God is described as having power is not from the attributes of essence.' According to Maturidi, this means that according to the opponent, God is not described as having power over any of His attributes except metaphorically (majāz al-lugha), meaning that the power refers to the effect (mafʿūl) rather than the attribute itself, similar to how an act done by command is called 'command' (amr). This critique shows the opponent's position reduces to a metaphorical interpretation, which is insufficient for a real attribute distinction.

Key Terms
قدرة
مجاز
إرادة المفعول
أمر
صفات الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's statement that God is not described as having power over attributes of essence means that power is an attribute of essence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the opponent's view actually leads to metaphorical attribution, not a real attribute.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi use to illustrate the opponent's metaphorical attribution?
A) Calling a creation 'Creator'.
B) Calling an act done by command 'command'.
C) Calling power 'essence'.
D) Calling an effect 'cause'.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's view reduces God's power over His attributes to a mere linguistic metaphor, similar to calling an effect by the name of its cause, which undermines the reality of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's power over His attributes, do you speak of it as a real, living truth that shapes your worship, or do you treat it as a comfortable phrase that costs you nothing? What would change if you truly believed God's power over His attributes is as real as His power over creation?
?
This passage exposes a subtle danger: reducing divine realities to mere language. Search your own soul: Have you ever contented yourself with correct words about God while your heart remained distant from their meaning? What would it take for you to move from saying 'God is powerful' to living in awe of that power?
?
The opponent's view makes God's power over His attributes a metaphor—a figure of speech. Have you ever, even unknowingly, treated any of God's attributes as less than fully real, as just a way of speaking? Be honest: If you met a Muslim who denied the reality of God's attributes, would your own faith be strong enough to refute them, or have you also allowed comfortable metaphors to replace true belief?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Attributes of Action vs. Attributes of Essence
Maturidi argues that names like ar-Rahman are attributes of action, not essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that names like ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim are attributes of action, not essence, and how he refutes objections.

Core Idea
Divine names can refer to actions, not just essence — a key distinction in Maturidi kalam.

Imam al-Maturidi engages with an opponent who considers names like ar-Rahman as attributes of essence. He argues that these are actually attributes of action because they describe what Allah does, not what He eternally is. This lesson follows his dialectical reasoning and three objections he raises to defend his view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the dialectical style.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim and opponent's position
Maturidi states that names like ar-Rahman are attributes of action. The opponent likely considers them attributes of essence. Locate this claim.
3
Follow the three objections and responses
The passage contains three objections from the opponent and Maturidi's replies. List each one and understand the reasoning.
First objection is about mercy vs curse; second about diversity in attributes of essence; third about creation.
4
Connect to the broader distinction
Reflect on why this distinction matters for divine transcendence and the concept of preeternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يقال : الله خالق رحمن رحيم، فإنما سمى به ذاته، فمثله صفة الفعل أى الفعل - وتوصف به ذاته، وذلك كما يقال : كلام حكمة وصدق وكذب على أنه كذلك، وهو صفة لصاحبه، فمثله يضاف إلى الله وبعد، فإنه يقال له : قولك رحمة ومغفرة صفة للفعل، ولعنة وشتم أيضا عندك صفة الفعل، فما الفعل الذى سمى رحمة ولعنة حتى يوصف الله به ؟ فإن قال : جنة ونار، وقبول ورد ونحو ذلك بطل قوله في المسائل التي ذكر في الأصلح والتعديل والتجويز، إن الله رحيم لا يفعل بعباده ذلك، وكل ذلك مما فعل بعباده . وإن أثبت معنى سوى ذلك فصارا غير خلقه، بهما يوصف على أن قوله : يشتم، كلام قبيح كلام قبيح لا يوصف الله به، ثم يقال له : لم لا قلت بالذى ذكرت فى صفة الذات والفعل ؟ وقد رأيت صفات الذات مختلفة في الاستعمال من وجه الإثبات نحو أن يقال بالعلم في أشياء لا يوصف بالقدرة فيها، وبالقدرة على أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع فيها، وبالرؤية في أشياء لا يوصف بالكرم فيها، وبالجود وبالحكمة في أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع لها، ونحو ذلك فما يكثر الإختلاف به، لم يجب بها الفرق بل هو الموصوف بها في الأزل . لم لا قلت كذلك في جميع ما يوصف به ؟ ؛ إذ هو يتعالى عن الاستحالة والفساد ؛ إنهما آيتان للحدث، أمارتان للكون بعد أن لم يكن . وأيضا يقال له : رأيت الخلق أقساما، يُسمى الله عندك ببعض الخلق ولا يسمى ببعض، ثم لم يدل على اختلاف في حق الصفة، كذلك فيما منع أمر الصفات ؟ ، وبالله التوفيق .
And likewise it is said: Allah is Creator, Merciful, Compassionate. For He is only named by that His essence, so the like of it is an attribute of action – i.e., the action – and His essence is described by it. And that is just as it is said: speech of wisdom and truth and falsehood, as being such, and it is an attribute of its possessor. So the like of it is attributed to Allah. And after that, it is said to him: Your statement that mercy and forgiveness are attributes of action, and curse and insult also in your view are attributes of action – what is the action that is named mercy and curse such that Allah is described by it? If he says: Paradise and Hellfire, and acceptance and rejection and the like, then his statement is invalid regarding the issues he mentioned concerning the preferable, the just, and the permissible, that Allah is Merciful and does not do that to His servants, while all of that is something He does to His servants. And if he affirms a meaning other than that, then they become other than His creation, by which He is described, given that his statement: 'He insults' is ugly speech, ugly speech by which Allah is not described. Then it is said to him: Why did you not say by what you mentioned concerning attribute of essence and action? And you have seen that attributes of essence are diverse in usage from the aspect of affirmation, such that one says by knowledge regarding things in which He is not described by power, and by power over things in which He is not described by hearing, and by vision regarding things in which He is not described by generosity, and by munificence and by wisdom regarding things for which He is not described by hearing, and the like. So that which makes much difference thereby, it is not necessary to distinguish by them; rather He is described by them in preeternity. Why did you not say likewise regarding all that He is described by? For He is exalted above change and corruption; they are two signs of temporality, two indications of coming into existence after not having been. And also it is said to him: You see that creation is divided; Allah is named by you by some creations and not named by some, yet that does not indicate a difference regarding the attribute. Likewise, what prevented the matter of attributes? And success is from Allah.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument in which Imam al-Maturidi refutes an opponent's claim that names like ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim are attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt). The author first states that these names are attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl) because they refer to actions that Allah performs, not to His eternal essence. He then presents an objection: the opponent says mercy and forgiveness are attributes of action, but so are curse and insult; what then is the action named mercy or curse? If the opponent says it is Paradise and Hellfire, etc., then that contradicts his own view that Allah is merciful and does not do such things to His servants, because He actually does them. If he posits another meaning, then those actions are not His creation, which is problematic. Then a second objection is raised: why does the opponent not apply the same reasoning to attributes of essence? The attributes of essence are diverse in application (e.g., knowledge without power in some cases), yet they are all affirmed of Allah in preeternity. So the opponent should similarly accept diversity in attributes of action without implying change in Allah. Finally, a third objection: just as Allah is named by some creations but not others without affecting the attribute, so too the diversity in attributes does not necessitate a distinction in their nature. The passage concludes with 'And success is from Allah.'

Misconception: All divine names refer to eternal attributes of essence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that names like ar-Rahman refer to actions, not essence, because they describe what Allah does.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what kind of attribute is the name 'ar-Rahman' (the Merciful)?
A) Attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt)
B) Attribute of action (ṣifat al-fiʿl)
C) Attribute of essence and action simultaneously
D) Neither essence nor action

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that attributes like mercy and cursing are actions, not eternal qualities of essence, thus preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you recite 'Bismillah ir-Rahman ir-Rahim', do you consciously consider that ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim refer to actions of mercy that Allah does for you right now, or do you simply recite it as a name without connection to your life? How can you make your recitation a reminder of His ongoing mercy to you today?
?
The opponent in the passage assumed that Allah would not do things like curse or create Hellfire because He is Merciful. Can you point to any area in your life where you assume Allah will not discipline you because He is Merciful, ignoring that His mercy includes both reward and punishment? How will you adjust your understanding of His names?
?
This lesson distinguishes between attributes of essence and action. Is there an attribute of Allah that you have been treating as static (essence) when it should be dynamic (action) in your relationship with Him? For example, do you think of His guidance as something He already gave, or as an active guidance He provides each moment? How will you seek His active attribute today?

Interpreting Quranic Terms of Coming as Befitting Allah
Maturidi argues that Quranic terms like 'coming' do not imply temporality in Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that Quranic terms like 'coming' do not imply temporality in Allah, but must be interpreted according to divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Quranic terms about Allah must be interpreted transcendentally, not through human change.

Maturidi addresses a key objection: that Quranic words like 'coming' (majī') suggest Allah undergoes change. He argues that such terms are to be understood in a manner worthy of Allah's lordship, not as literal movement. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the example of Prophet Ibrahim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and the Quranic reference.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that the Quranic speech is temporal because it uses terms like 'coming' which imply change. Find where this is mentioned.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi states that such terms must be interpreted 'in the way that befits lordship' (rubūbiyya), not in the way of creation. Grasp this principle of tanzih.
4
Examine the Quranic example
Maturidi cites Prophet Ibrahim's statement 'I do not like those that set' to show that change is a sign of temporality, which does not apply to Allah.
The verse is Quran 6:76.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج في حدث الكلام بذكر الإتيان والمجيء، وهو من ذلك الوجه بحدث، وقد بينا أن الله تعالى إذ وصيف بالكلام - على تعاليه عن احتمال التغير والزوال فمثله في صفة الكلام والفعل وما ذكرت على أن الله قد أضاف المجيء إلى نفسه، ثم لم يجب أنه حدث، بل صرف إلى الوجه والذي يحق بالربوبية، فمثله الأول وكذلك وجب صرف الإتيان إلى الوجه الذى يحق بالربوبية، لا إلى ما عرف به الخلق من التغير والزوال، فمثله في حقيقة الفعل والكلام على ما قال لا أحب الأفلين، ومن يكون على حال ثم على أخرى فهو ابراهيم من الآفلين بالتحقيق، والله أعلم .
And he argued for the temporality of speech by mentioning 'coming' and 'coming' (al-ityān and al-majī'), and it is from that aspect temporality. And we have shown that Allah, exalted, when described by speech – while He is exalted above the possibility of change and passing away – so likewise in the attribute of speech and action and what was mentioned. Because Allah has attributed 'coming' to Himself, yet it does not necessitate that it is temporal, but rather it is interpreted in the way that befits lordship. So likewise the first. And similarly, it is necessary to interpret 'coming' in the way that befits lordship, not in the way known from creation of change and passing away. So likewise in the reality of action and speech according to what He said: 'I do not like those that set' (Quran 6:76). And whoever is in a state then in another, he is Ibrahim from among those who set in reality. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the claim that the Quranic speech of Allah is temporal (ḥādith). The opponent argues that Quranic terms like 'coming' and 'coming' (al-ityān and al-majī') imply temporality because coming involves movement and change. Maturidi responds that although these words are used, they must be interpreted in a way that befits Allah's lordship (rubūbiyya), not in the way of created beings who change and pass away. He cites the example of Prophet Ibrahim (peace be upon him) who said, 'I do not like those that set' (referring to celestial bodies that change). Ibrahim's statement shows that change is a sign of temporality, but Allah is beyond change. Therefore, the use of 'coming' in the Quran does not imply that Allah undergoes change; it is an expression that must be understood in a manner appropriate to His majesty. The passage ends with 'And Allah knows best.'

Correction: Maturidi insists such expressions must be interpreted in a way that befits Allah's lordship and transcendence, not in the manner of created beings.

Maturidi insists that all Quranic descriptions of Allah must be interpreted in a way that upholds His transcendence, using the example of Ibrahim to reject any implication of change.

?

Perhaps your patience with unanswered duas suggests you expect Allah to act like a human? Today, commit to one specific way you will train your heart to see Allah as unchangeable and beyond your expectations.
?

The Dilemma of Divine Power and Attributes
Maturidi presents a dilemma refuting the claim that God's creative attribute is acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological dilemma that arises from denying God's eternal attributes and the absurd consequences of saying God can choose not to create.

Core Idea
Denying God's eternal attributes leads to either necessary creation or pre-eternal creation.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument against the view that God's attributes like Creator are acquired later. Maturidi uses a dilemma to show that if God could refrain from creating, creation would have to be pre-eternal—an impossibility. Follow along as we unpack this concise objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical view
Read the beginning of the passage where Maturidi presents an opposing view: that Allah was not originally Creator or Merciful but could make His essence so. Note how this implies attributes are not eternal.
The view suggests God's attributes change, which is problematic for divine transcendence.
2
Grasp the objection posed
The opponent is asked: 'Can Allah not create creation?' This is the core of the dilemma. Identify this crucial question in the text.
3
Follow the two horns of the dilemma
If the opponent says 'No', God is forced to be Creator, contradicting his view. If 'Yes', then power falling on non-creation makes it creation, implying creation is pre-eternal. Trace each option.
Both options lead to absurdities, refuting the original claim.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power except with Allah', affirming that God's power is eternal and not subject to change or acquisition. Understand how this seals the argument.
The phrase is a humble acknowledgment of God's absolute sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ، فإنا قد بينا اختلاف أحوال الصفات في التوسيع والتضييق في أشياء ، على الاتفاق في أنها صفات الذات ، فلنقل فما ذكر كذلك. ثم من مذهبه أن الله تعالى كان غير خالق ولا رحمن، وقدر على أن يجعل ذاته خالقا رحمانا ، ويجوز أن نعبد الرحمن الخالق ، فيكون على قوله قدر على أن يجعل للخلق معبودا ، وذلك اسم تقع عليه القدرة ، فيصير في الحقيقة يعبد أيضا من وجه هذه الأسماء محدث من حيث كانت مما تقع عليه الله .. وهو غير القدرة . يقال له: أيقدر الله أن لا يخلق الخلق ؟ فإن قال : لا ، صَيّره خالقا بالضرورة أو بنفسه ، وبطل قوله ، وإن قال : يقدر ، فيلزمه أن يجعل غير المخلوق خلقا بوقوع القدرة عليه ، وفي ذلك إثبات قدم الخلق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
After this, we have explained the varying states of attributes in expansion and restriction in certain matters, by agreement that they are attributes of the essence. So let us say what was mentioned likewise. Then from his doctrine: that Allah most high was not Creator nor Merciful, and He is able to make His essence Creator and Merciful, and it is permissible that we worship the Merciful Creator. So according to his statement, He is able to make for creation an object of worship, and that is a name upon which power falls, so in reality it becomes worshipped also from one aspect—these names are originated in that they are what power falls upon... and He is not power. It is said to him: Is Allah able not to create creation? If he says: No, he makes Him necessarily or by Himself Creator, and his statement becomes false. And if he says: He is able, then it follows that He makes what is not created a creation by the falling of power upon it, and in that is the establishment of the pre-eternity of creation. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion about God's attributes and His power over creation. Maturidi first reminds that attributes are agreed to be attributes of the essence, differing only in scope. He then presents a hypothetical opposing view: that Allah was not originally Creator or Merciful, but had power to make His essence such, and that we could worship the Merciful Creator. This implies that names like 'Creator' originated later—a problem because it suggests change in God. Maturidi then poses a dilemma to this opponent: Is Allah able not to create? If the opponent says No, then he makes God necessarily Creator, contradicting his own view. If he says Yes (God is able not to create), then power falling on non-creation would turn it into creation, implying that creation is pre-eternal—an absurdity. Maturidi concludes with 'there is no power except with Allah', affirming divine transcendence over such change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's attributes like Creator are acquired through creation.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to absurdity: either God is forced to create or creation is pre-eternal. Attributes are eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if someone says God is able not to create?
A) It makes God necessarily Creator by Himself.
B) It establishes the pre-eternity of creation.
C) It denies God's power altogether.
D) It implies God is not Merciful.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that God's attributes are eternal and cannot be acquired; otherwise, creation would be either necessary or pre-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reflect on a moment when you doubted God's constancy—does this argument strengthen your conviction?

Maturidi's dilemma shows that denying eternal attributes leads to absurdities. In your own life, when you deny or forget one of God's eternal attributes—like His mercy or justice—what absurdities appear in your relationship with Him? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God's attributes are unchanging, even when your circumstances change?
?
The source concludes with 'There is no power except with Allah.' This is not just a pious phrase but a logical necessity: all power belongs to the Eternal. Do you live as if you have independent power, or do you constantly acknowledge that your ability to do anything rests entirely on the One whose power is without change or acquisition?

Preservation of the Quran: Metaphorical Speech
Maturidi argues that preservation of the Quran is metaphorical, not proof of createdness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the preservation of the Quran is metaphorical and does not imply the Quran is identical to God's eternal speech.

Core Idea
The Quran's preservation points to God's speech, but is not identical to it.

Maturidi's opponents argued that because the Quran is preserved (hifz), it must be created (since only created things are preserved). Maturidi responds by distinguishing between the Quran as a created expression and God's eternal attribute of speech. He uses the concept of metaphorical attribution (majaz) to show that the Quran is called 'speech of Allah' only by way of agreement with the real attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
The Arabic is concise; the translation will help clarify.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent uses preservation (hifz) as evidence that the Quran is created. Why would they think that?
They assume only created things can be preserved.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi says preservation is metaphorical (majaz) and points to God's real attribute of speech without being identical to it. He compares to other metaphorical terms like 'coming' (maji').
The key concept is 'majaz al-muwafaqah' – metaphor by agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بما يحفظ ، وقد يحفظ الله ، وقد يكون ذلك على حفظ حدوده ، وما اشتمل عليه الكلام ، وما يضاف إلى الله من الكلام بين الخلق ، فهو مجاز على الموافقة بما يعرف به الكلام الذي هو صفته ، وذلك كما ذكرنا من المجيء وغيره ، والعهد ونصر الرب ونحو ذلك مما لا يحقق ذلك المعنى لذاته ، فمثله القرآن.
And he argued by that which is preserved, and Allah may preserve, and that may be upon the preservation of His boundaries and what the speech (al-kalām) contains. And what is attributed to Allah of speech among creation is metaphorical, by way of agreement with what is known of the speech that is His attribute. And that is like what we mentioned of 'coming' (al-majī') and other things, and the covenant, and Allah's victory, and the like, which do not realize that meaning in their essence. So the Quran is similar.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage is a further argument about the nature of divine speech. Maturidi's opponent uses the fact that the Quran is preserved (hifz) to argue that it is created. Maturidi responds that preservation is applied to the Quran because it contains God's boundaries and messages, but this preservation is metaphorical (majāz). When speech is attributed to Allah among creatures (i.e., the Quran), it is by way of agreement (muwāfaqah) with the real attribute of speech that belongs to God eternally. Just like the terms 'coming' (majī'), 'covenant', and 'victory' are applied to God in a metaphorical sense without realizing the literal meaning in His essence, so the Quran—though recited and preserved—is not itself the eternal speech but a created expression pointing to it.

Key Terms

المجاز
الموافقة

القرآن

Correction: Maturidi explains that the Quran is attributed to God metaphorically; preservation applies to its physical form, not to God's attribute.

Checkpoint

A) The Quran is God's eternal speech preserved in a book.
B) Preservation is a real attribute of God's speech itself.
C) The preservation of the Quran is metaphorical, pointing to the real attribute of God's speech.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's preservation is metaphorical; it points to God's eternal speech but is not identical to it.

If you have been taught to interpret such terms figuratively, do you apply the same hermeneutic to the Quran's 'speech'? Is your faith in the Quran's divine origin shaken or strengthened by knowing it is metaphorically attributed?
?
Preservation of the Quran is often a pillar of your certainty. But what if preservation alone does not guarantee uncreatedness? Does this make you examine other evidences for the Quran's divine origin, or does it unsettle your confidence? How will you now approach the Quran's preservation as a sign pointing to Allah's speech rather than being that speech itself?

Refuting Objections on Divine Speech and Action
Maturidi rejects analogies between divine speech and created action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the objection that divine speech and action are comparable to created beings, and why silence implies incapacity unsuitable for God.

Core Idea
God’s speech is not like creatures’ speech; silence is only from incapacity.

This passage addresses an opponent’s attempt to equate God’s speech with human speech, arguing that if God speaks, He could also be silent. Maturidi exposes the flaw: silence in creatures arises from incapacity, which cannot apply to God. The lesson explores this reasoning and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation.
Start by reading the Arabic text carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: Maturidi first declares his opponent's view corrupt, then presents a series of objections and responses.
The text contains some unclear phrases due to OCR, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the key objection on speech and action.
The opponent argues that speech and action are similar, so if God is spoken of as having speech, He should also be subject to action. Maturidi notes this is inconsistent with the opponent’s own denial of knowledge as a real attribute.
This reveals the opponent's inconsistency.
3
Analyze the silence objection.
The opponent claims that if God can speak, He could also be mute or silent. Maturidi corrects this by stating that silence in creatures stems from incapacity, which is not true of God.
This is a key distinction between necessary and contingent existence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion about divine power.
Maturidi affirms that God, being absolutely powerful (qādir), is not subject to limitations like silence. The lesson ends with this reaffirmation.
Connect this to the broader Islamic doctrine of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه [أبو منصور رحمه الله : وما قاله فاسد لأنه عورض بقوله صفة الذات، فليقل به كما قلت في العالم بلا حقيقة ، وكذا السمع، ونحوه . ونحن قد بينا محمد الله ما يكفى ذا العقل دونه ثم عارض الكلام بالفعل ، ولا فرق بينها عند خصمه . ثم عارض بما لا يخلو في الشاهد : من يجوز منه الكلام [يجوز] منه خرس أو سكوت ، وقد أخطأ السؤال : إنما هو من عجز أو سكوت . وعارض بالفعل ، وهو كذلك عند الخصم ، مع ما ذكر فيه الفعل والشرك ، وما التشرك إلا الفعل ، لكن الخيرة تعمل به ما ذكر . ثم عارض بالصبى أنه ليس بأخرس ، وقد بينا أنه ما كان من عظم الجزئية أن لا يجد لنفسه مثلا يعرف به الرب إلا الصبيان والمجانين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأجاب لما عُورض بما لا يخلو القادر ، مما له القدرة من فعل وكلام به يعجز عن ذلك .
Al-Faqīh [Abū Manṣūr, may Allah have mercy on him] said: What he said is corrupt because he was confronted with his statement 'attribute of the essence'. So let him say it as you said of the knower without reality, and likewise hearing and the like. And we have explained [..] Allah, sufficient for the intelligent without it. Then he compared speech to action, and there is no difference between them according to his opponent. Then he argued with what is inevitable in the witnessed: one from whom speech is possible, from him is also possible muteness or silence, and he erred in the question: it is only from incapacity or silence. And he argued with action, and it is likewise according to the opponent, with what was mentioned regarding action and association, and association is nothing but action, but the choice works by what was mentioned. Then he argued with the child that he is not mute, and we have explained that it is from the great foolishness that he cannot find for himself an example by which to know the Lord except children and insane people. And there is no power except with Allah. And he answered: when confronted with what the powerful (al-qādir) cannot avoid, one who has power over action and speech [may] be incapable of that.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and a comprehensive answer regarding the nature of divine speech and action. Al-Faqīh (Imām al-Māturīdī) first dismisses the opponent's view as corrupt. The opponent had claimed that speech is an attribute of the essence, but Maturidi notes that he does not affirm knowledge as a real attribute—inconsistency. Then the opponent compares speech and action, claiming they are similar. Maturidi then presents an objection from the witnessed world: someone who can speak can also be mute or silent, so if God speaks, He could also be silent—but this is an error because silence for creatures arises from incapacity, not from God. The opponent also brings up action, but Maturidi notes that even the opponent agrees action is not an attribute of essence. Finally, the opponent argues using a child: a child is not mute (but can speak), implying that God too could be without speech? Maturidi dismisses this as foolish, saying no one should use children or insane people as analogies for God. The answer reaffirms that God, being absolutely powerful (qādir), is not subject to the limitations of creatures like silence or muteness.

Key Terms
صفة الذات
الفعل
الخرس
السكوت
الكلام
القادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since God speaks, He could also be silent, just as humans can be silent.
Correction: Silence in creatures is caused by incapacity. God, being absolutely powerful, is not subject to such limitations.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the objection that divine speech implies the possibility of silence?
A) Because God cannot be silent.
B) Because silence in creatures arises from incapacity, which does not apply to God.
C) Because the opponent's example is from the unseen world.
D) Because speech is an attribute of essence.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is not comparable to human speech because silence in creatures stems from incapacity, which is impossible for Allah.

?

The Child Analogy and the Power of Allah
Maturidi rejects the child analogy and reaffirms divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the child analogy as foolish and how he reaffirms God's absolute power beyond creaturely limitations.

Core Idea
Using children or insane people as analogies for God is a great foolishness.

The opponent brings forth an objection using a child who is not mute, implying that God could also be without speech. Maturidi dismisses this as folly and provides a final answer emphasizing that God, being absolutely powerful, is not bound by such comparisons. This lesson clarifies the proper method of understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for this lesson.
Focus on the final part of Maturidi's refutation, starting from the child objection to the final answer.
The passage includes a short answer that ties back to God's power.
2
Understand the child analogy.
The opponent says a child is not mute, implying that it can speak. The analogy suggests that if God is like a child, He could lack speech. Maturidi calls this 'great foolishness' because it uses weak examples for God.
Children and insane people are not sound analogies for the Creator.
3
Analyze the final answer.
Maturidi concludes that the powerful (al-qadir) is not subject to incapacity; one who has power over action and speech cannot be found incapable of that.
This affirms that God's power is absolute and limitless.
4
Reflect on the lesson for tawhid.
This passage teaches that analogies from creation cannot be applied to Allah without proper qualification. Divine attributes are unique.
Connect to the broader principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه [أبو منصور رحمه الله : وما قاله فاسد لأنه عورض بقوله صفة الذات، فليقل به كما قلت في العالم بلا حقيقة ، وكذا السمع، ونحوه . ونحن قد بينا محمد الله ما يكفى ذا العقل دونه ثم عارض الكلام بالفعل ، ولا فرق بينها عند خصمه . ثم عارض بما لا يخلو في الشاهد : من يجوز منه الكلام [يجوز] منه خرس أو سكوت ، وقد أخطأ السؤال : إنما هو من عجز أو سكوت . وعارض بالفعل ، وهو كذلك عند الخصم ، مع ما ذكر فيه الفعل والشرك ، وما التشرك إلا الفعل ، لكن الخيرة تعمل به ما ذكر . ثم عارض بالصبى أنه ليس بأخرس ، وقد بينا أنه ما كان من عظم الجزئية أن لا يجد لنفسه مثلا يعرف به الرب إلا الصبيان والمجانين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأجاب لما عُورض بما لا يخلو القادر ، مما له القدرة من فعل وكلام به يعجز عن ذلك .
Al-Faqīh [Abū Manṣūr, may Allah have mercy on him] said: What he said is corrupt because he was confronted with his statement 'attribute of the essence'. So let him say it as you said of the knower without reality, and likewise hearing and the like. And we have explained [..] Allah, sufficient for the intelligent without it. Then he compared speech to action, and there is no difference between them according to his opponent. Then he argued with what is inevitable in the witnessed: one from whom speech is possible, from him is also possible muteness or silence, and he erred in the question: it is only from incapacity or silence. And he argued with action, and it is likewise according to the opponent, with what was mentioned regarding action and association, and association is nothing but action, but the choice works by what was mentioned. Then he argued with the child that he is not mute, and we have explained that it is from the great foolishness that he cannot find for himself an example by which to know the Lord except children and insane people. And there is no power except with Allah. And he answered: when confronted with what the powerful (al-qādir) cannot avoid, one who has power over action and speech [may] be incapable of that.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and a comprehensive answer regarding the nature of divine speech and action. Al-Faqīh (Imām al-Māturīdī) first dismisses the opponent's view as corrupt. The opponent had claimed that speech is an attribute of the essence, but Maturidi notes that he does not affirm knowledge as a real attribute—inconsistency. Then the opponent compares speech and action, claiming they are similar. Maturidi then presents an objection from the witnessed world: someone who can speak can also be mute or silent, so if God speaks, He could also be silent—but this is an error because silence for creatures arises from incapacity, not from God. The opponent also brings up action, but Maturidi notes that even the opponent agrees action is not an attribute of essence. Finally, the opponent argues using a child: a child is not mute (but can speak), implying that God too could be without speech? Maturidi dismisses this as foolish, saying no one should use children or insane people as analogies for God. The answer reaffirms that God, being absolutely powerful (qādir), is not subject to the limitations of creatures like silence or muteness.

الفعل

Correction: Maturidi rejects this as foolish because children and insane people are not proper analogies for God. God's power is absolute and does not involve incapacity.

The Principle: Divine Attributes as Transcendence
Maturidi states the principle that divine attributes denote transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when Maturidi describes Allah with attributes like speech, knowledge, and action, he is actually describing Allah's transcendence – that He is free from defects and unlike His creation.

Core Idea
Describing Allah's attributes is describing His absolute transcendence.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by establishing a crucial principle: the way we describe Allah with attributes is not a comparison to creation. Rather, these descriptions serve to affirm His perfection and freedom from any imperfection. This foundational idea prevents both anthropomorphism and outright negation. Let's see how he argues this point in the passage below.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words sink in before interpreting.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the key terms.
2
Contrast two approaches
Notice how Maturidi contrasts describing Allah with attributes (like Merciful, Creator) with describing Him by negation (like 'O He who is not Merciful'). He says the latter is blameworthy and likens Allah to creation.
This contrast shows that affirming attributes is actually about transcendence, not similarity.
3
Reflect on the core insight
Ask yourself: why does Maturidi say that describing Allah with attributes is only a description of being free from defects? The answer is that attributes like speech, knowledge, and action, when applied to Allah, do not imply creation-like qualities. Instead, they affirm perfection.
This lesson guards against two extremes: tashbih (likening) and ta'til (denying attributes).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل في ذلك أن الوصف بالكلام والعلم والفعل الحمد عليه إنما هو وصف بالبراءة الآفات والتعالى عن العيوب من في الأزل ما لو كان بغيره خالقا رحمانا متكلما أن يجوز ، مع يصير كذلك وهو لا كذلك ، والقول بيا من ليس برحمن ولا رحيم ولا خالق اقول ذم وإلحاق بغيره من الخلائق، ثبت أنه بذاته رحمن رحيم خالق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The foundation in this is that describing [God] with speech, knowledge, and action—praise be to Him—is only a description of being free from defects and exalted above flaws from pre-eternity. If He were [described] by something other than Him as Creator, Merciful, Speaker, it would be permissible [for that to change?], while He is not such. And to say 'O He who is not Merciful nor Compassionate nor Creator' is a word of blame and likening to other creatures. It is established that He is by His essence Merciful, Compassionate, Creator. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a principle statement from Imam al-Maturidi regarding the correct method of describing Allah. He explains that when we attribute speech, knowledge, and action to Allah, we are not likening Him to creatures. Rather, we are declaring Him free from defects and exalted above all flaws. The description is not a comparison but a negation of imperfection. Maturidi argues that if Allah were described by something that can occur in others (like created speech), it would imply that He could change or be subject to deficiency. But Allah is not like that. Therefore, to describe Him as 'not Merciful' or 'not Creator' would be blameworthy and would liken Him to created beings. Instead, we affirm that He is essentially Merciful, Compassionate, and Creator. This passage guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih) and also against negation (ta'til). The key is that the attributes are real but not like those of creatures.

Key Terms
الوصف بالكلام والعلم والفعل
البراءة الآفات
التعالي عن العيوب
بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming attributes like 'speech' means God speaks with letters and sounds similar to humans.
Correction: Maturidi explains that describing Allah with attributes is actually a declaration of His transcendence and freedom from any imperfections, not a comparison to creation.

Checkpoint

B) It is a description of Allah as having the same qualities as humans.
C) It is a metaphorical way of speaking that has no real meaning.
D) It is a way to compare Allah to created beings.

Key Takeaway
Affirming Allah's attributes is a way of declaring His transcendence, not likening Him to creation.

Divine Attributes

The True Nature of Divine Attributes: Neither Essence Nor Other
Maturidi clarifies the ontological status of divine attributes via dialectical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's precise position on divine attributes: they are real, eternal, and subsist in the essence, yet are neither identical to the essence nor separate entities.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are real but not separate; they are not the essence itself.

Maturidi navigates between two extremes: those who deny attributes as real (Mu'tazila) and those who say they are separate creatures. In this passage, he clarifies that saying 'attributes are not other than Allah' does not mean they are His essence, nor does it mean they are separate things. Instead, they are attributes of the essence, eternal and real.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Realize the danger of misinterpreting 'attributes are not other than Allah' as either identity with essence or as separate entities. This sets the stage for Maturidi's clarification.
The opponent may think that denying otherness implies identity.
2
Locate Maturidi's two negations
Identify where Maturidi says he does not intend that attributes are the essence, nor that they are other (separate). These are the two key negations.
Read the first sentence carefully.
3
Contrast with created attributes
Note Maturidi's remark that for created things, attributes (like a book) are other than the essence. This shows that the divine case is unique.
This highlights the difference between Creator and creation.
4
Recognize the correct expression
Maturidi states the correct position: attributes are 'attributes of the essence' (sifat al-dhat). They are not other in the sense of separate, but they are also not the essence itself.
This phrase is the middle path.
5
Guard against extremes
Understand that this lesson guards against ta'til (denial of attributes by equating them with essence) and tashbih (likening attributes to creatures by making them separate).
The correct view is that attributes are real, eternal, and subsisting in the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : يريد بصفات أن لا يثبت ثمة غير، ولم يرد أنها ذاته، بل كل صفة لقديم أو حديث فهي غيره، وهى قول أو كتاب، وصفات الله الذى نصفه، أو قوله وكتابه، وهما محدثان. مسألته قال أبو منصور رضى الله عنه : ذكرت جملة قوله الذي [به] ختم لتعلموا مبلغ علمه بالله ثم بالصفات : مرة قال : لا يثبت ثمة غير، ولا يريد أنها هو، فإذا لم يرد بالصفات هو ولا غيره، أما يعلم أنه قول أهل الإثبات ثم قال هو قولنا، فقولنا هي ليست غيره حتى نقول ليس ثمة غير، ثم ذكر أن صفات الله ما ذكر، وقال : هى صفات الذات، فإذا ما ذكر صفات الذات، وهو لم يزل بها موصوفا، وهى أغيار له، جل ربنا عما يصفه المبطلون
Then he said: He intends by 'attributes' that no other is established there, and he does not intend that they are His essence. Rather, every attribute of an eternal or originated thing is other than it, and they are a saying or a book. And the attributes of Allah which we describe, or His speech and His book, are both originated. His question: Abu Mansur (may Allah be pleased with him) said: I mentioned the summary of his statement with which he concluded, that you may know the extent of his knowledge of Allah and then of the attributes. Once he said: No other is established there, and he does not intend that it is He. So if he does not intend by the attributes either He or other, does he not know that this is the statement of the people of affirmation? Then he said: It is our statement. So our statement is that they are not other than Him such that we say 'there is no other.' Then he mentioned that the attributes of Allah are what he mentioned, and he said: They are attributes of the essence. So when he mentioned attributes of the essence, and He has never ceased to be described by them, they are others for Him. Exalted is our Lord above what the falsifiers describe.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical clarification about the ontological status of divine attributes. Maturidi is responding to an opponent who claims that 'attributes are not other than God' means they are identical to the essence. Maturidi distinguishes: when we say attributes are not other, we do not mean they are the essence itself, nor that they are separate existents. Rather, for created things, attributes are other than the essence (e.g., a book is other than the author). For Allah, His attributes (like speech) are eternal and not created, so they are not 'other' in the sense of being separate creatures. However, they are also not identical to His essence. Maturidi then critiques a contradictory position: someone who says attributes are not other nor the essence is actually affirming attributes while denying their distinctness. The correct position of Ahl al-Ithbat (those who affirm attributes) is that attributes are real, eternal, and subsist in the essence, but they are not the essence itself. Maturidi ends by stating that those who say attributes are 'others' (aghyar) for Him are mistaken; rather, they are attributes of the essence, and He has always been described by them. This lesson guards against two extremes: ta'til (denial of attributes) and tashbih (likening attributes to creatures).

Key Terms
صفات الذات
لا يثبت ثمة غير
أهل الإثبات
أغيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'attributes are not other than God' means they are exactly His essence (like the Mu'tazila).
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that it means they are not separate entities, but they are real attributes of the essence, not identical to it.

Checkpoint
What is the correct understanding of the statement 'attributes are not other than God' according to Maturidi?
A) Attributes are identical to God's essence.
B) Attributes are separate entities from God.
C) Attributes are not separate entities, but they are also not the essence itself.
D) Attributes are merely names without reality.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are real eternal attributes of Allah's essence, neither identical to it nor separate from it.

The Principle of Describing God with Attributes
Al-Maturidi states the principle that divine attributes indicate transcendence, not temporal acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Maturidi, describing God with attributes such as speech, knowledge, and mercy is not describing temporal events but rather affirming His eternal freedom from defects and transcendence.

Core Idea
Describing God with attributes means declaring Him free from defects, not acquiring properties over time.

This passage from Imam Maturidi establishes a foundational principle for understanding divine attributes. He argues that when we say God is merciful, we are not saying He becomes merciful by creating mercy in something else, but that mercy is inherent in His essence. This refutes views that make God's attributes dependent on creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Do not try to analyze yet; let the text speak for itself.
The Arabic has some OCR issues, but the meaning is clear from context.
2
Identify the core principle
Notice that al-Maturidi states that describing God with attributes is actually describing Him with freedom from defects and transcendence. This is the key principle.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Maturidi refutes the idea that God's attributes come from His actions. He argues that if God were only merciful because He creates mercy in others, then one could address Him as 'O He who is not merciful', which is blasphemous.
This shows that the attribute must be essential, not derived.
4
Grasp the conclusive argument
The final argument is that if God were named by what occurs in others, He would have to be named by every accidental property, which is absurd. Therefore, God's attributes are eternal and essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل في ذلك أن الوصف بالكلام والعلم والفعل الحمد عليه إنما هو وصف بالبراءة الآفات والتعالى عن العيوب من في الأزل ما لو كان بغيره خالقا رحمانا متكلما أن يجوز، مع يصير كذلك وهو لا كذلك، والقول بيا من ليس برحمن ولا رحيم ولا خالق اقول ذم وإلحاق بغيره من الخلائق، ثبت أنه بذاته رحمن رحيم خالق، ولا قوة إلا بالله على أنه لو كان يُسمى بما يحل فى غيره ليجب أن يسمى بكل شيء يحل في غيره، ما لو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يكون أحد يحتمل مثله في الشاهد، وفى مع امتناع ذلك في محتمل التغير وصف له بالعلو ذلك عن... والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in this is that describing (al-wasf) with speech, knowledge, and action — praise be upon Him — is only a description of being free from defects and elevated above faults, from eternity; if He were a creator, merciful, and speaker through another, it would be permissible for He would become so while He is not so. The statement 'O He who is not merciful nor compassionate nor creator' is, I say, blame and likening to other creatures. It is established that He is in His essence merciful, compassionate, creator. And there is no power except through Allah. If He were named by what occurs in other than Him, He would have to be named by everything that occurs in other than Him; if that were permissible, it would be permissible for someone to bear its like in the witness. And in the impossibility of that in what is subject to change, description of Him with transcendence over that... And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a fundamental principle in al-Maturidi's theology regarding how God is described. Al-Maturidi begins by asserting that describing God with attributes such as speech, knowledge, and action is not a description of temporal origination but rather a description of His being free from defects and transcendent above imperfections from eternity. He refutes the Mu'tazilite notion that God becomes described by an attribute only when He creates that attribute in something else. For example, they might say God is 'merciful' only because He creates mercy in others. Al-Maturidi argues that if that were the case, one could say 'O He who is not merciful' as blame, which would be blasphemous. Instead, God is merciful in His essence. He further argues that if God were named by what occurs in other beings, He would have to be named by every accidental property, which is absurd. Therefore, God's attributes are essential and not derived from His actions. The passage concludes by affirming that God is transcendent and that success comes from Him alone.

Attributes: Neither Essence Nor Another
Al-Maturidi clarifies that divine attributes are neither essence nor something else.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Maturidi's nuanced position that divine attributes are real descriptions of God's essence, neither identical to it nor fully separate.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real descriptions of His essence, neither identical nor separate.

Al-Maturidi addresses a subtle theological dilemma: if attributes are not the essence, are they then something entirely other? He rejects both extremes, affirming that attributes are 'sifat al-dhat' (attributes of the essence) – real and distinct in meaning yet not independently existing. This passage clarifies his stance against those who might misunderstand his earlier statement that attributes do not establish another entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the repeated phrases 'no other is established' and 'attributes of the essence'.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms 'sifat' (attributes), 'dhat' (essence), 'ghayr' (other), and 'aghyar' (others). Understand how Al-Maturidi uses them.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Grasp the refutation
Notice how Al-Maturidi critiques the statement that attributes are neither God nor other, calling it inconsistent. Then see his conclusion: attributes are descriptions of the essence and are 'aghyar' in a qualified sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : يريد بصفات أن لا يثبت ثمة غير، ولم يرد أنها ذاته، بل كل صفة لقديم أو حديث فهي غيره، وهى قول أو كتاب، وصفات الله الذى نصفه، أو قوله وكتابه، وهما محدثان. مي مسألته. قال أبو منصور رضى الله عنه : ذكرت جملة قوله الذي [به] ختم لتعلموا مبلغ علمه بالله ثم بالصفات : مرة قال : لا يثبت ثمة غير، ولا يريد أنها هو، فإذا لم يرد بالصفات هو ولا غيره، أما يعلم أنه قول أهل الإثبات ثم قال هو قولنا، فقولنا هي ليست غيره حتى نقول ليس ثمة غير، ثم ذكر أن صفات الله ما ذكر، وقال : هى صفات الذات، فإذا ما ذكر صفات الذات، وهو لم يزل بها موصوفا، وهى أغيار له، جل ربنا عما يصفه المبطلون.
Then he said: He intends by attributes that no other is established there, and he did not intend that they are His essence. Rather, every attribute of an eternal or temporal being is other than it, and they are either speech or writing. And the attributes of God which we describe, or His speech and His book, and they are created. His question? Abu Mansur (may Allah be pleased with him) said: I mentioned the totality of his statement with which he concluded, that you may know the extent of his knowledge of Allah and then of the attributes. At one time he said: No other is established there, and he does not intend that they are He. Since he does not intend by attributes He nor other, does he not know that this is the statement of the people of affirmation? Then he said: It is our statement. So our statement is that they are not other than Him so that we could say there is no other. Then he mentioned that the attributes of God are what he mentioned, and he said: They are attributes of the essence. So when he mentioned attributes of the essence, and He has not ceased to be described by them, and they are others to Him, exalted is our Lord above what the falsifiers describe.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of a theological nuance regarding God's attributes. Al-Maturidi is explaining that when earlier he said 'attributes do not establish another entity', he did not mean that the attributes are identical to God's essence. Rather, every attribute (whether of an eternal or temporal being) is 'other than' (ghayr) the being. However, for God, the attributes are not separate entities existing alongside Him; they are real but not independent. He then discusses a statement from someone (possibly an opponent) who said 'no other is established there' but also did not say the attributes are identical to God. Al-Maturidi accuses this person of inconsistency: if attributes are neither God nor other, then what are they? He asserts that the correct position of the Ahl al-Ithbat (people of affirmation) is that attributes are not the essence nor something completely other. Rather, they are descriptions of the essence (sifat al-dhat) and are 'aghyar' (others) to Him in a qualified sense. Finally, he exalts God above the descriptions of the falsifiers.

Key Terms
صفات (sifat)
ذات (dhat)
غير (ghayr)
أغيار (aghyar)
أهل الإثبات (ahl al-ithbat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if attributes are not the essence, they must be fully separate entities existing alongside God, compromising tawhid.
Correction: Al-Maturidi affirms that attributes are 'sifat al-dhat' – real descriptions that are neither identical to the essence nor independently existing. They are distinct in meaning but not separate in being.

Checkpoint
What does al-Maturidi say about the relationship between God's attributes and His essence?
A) The attributes are exactly the same as the essence
B) The attributes are completely other than God
C) The attributes are real descriptions of the essence but not identical to it nor separate
D) The attributes are merely names with no corresponding reality

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are real and eternal descriptions of His essence, neither identical to it nor independently existing entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like His Power or His Mercy, do you find yourself picturing them as separate 'things' added to His being? How might that subtle mental picture compromise the oneness of God in your heart? Commit to correcting this each time you remember.
?
This lesson says that God's attributes are 'neither He nor other'. How will you change the way you speak about God's attributes in your prayers and conversations to reflect that truth? Write one specific change you will make today.
?
Reflect on a moment when you felt God's attribute of Mercy was something He 'sent down' to you, rather than a description of His eternal being. How would your relationship with that mercy change if you truly believed it is part of His essence, not a separate creation? Ask Allah to help you feel the difference.

Divine Attributes
